The Voice and the Woman.
The Woman thought it was she who was writing
the letter. She sat at a table, bent over a sheet of
paper. There was a pen in her hand, and it was
her hand writing “My Dearest, are you sur-
prised ...”
The Angel smiled. He Was leaning out from
the gold bar of Heaven. ...
And the Woman went on. . . “that I have
broken the long silence ? 'For weeks I have been
trying not to write. But to-night I give up the
struggle. How gladly ...”
* * » *
The Angel smiled again, and with the same
glance that watched the Woman he surveyed the
Man, hopeless, abandoned by his kind, not; God-
forgotten only because of his love for the Woman.
His face was hidden in his arm. He was so when
the sunset burned away into grey, then black,
ashes. So would he stay through the night—save
when he raised his head to look at the hour. After
to-night the hour and the sunset would be no more
for him unless ...
His thoughts always came to within a heart-beat
of a woman's name, then jangled into silence.
* * . *
“ I know,” went on the Woman, “ that you are
in trouble—many troubles. How do I know ? I
cannot tell. 1 only know that all through the
dumb .vear my heart has known always of your
She paused,, gave a little moan, mo&d irreso-
POSSIBLY “DE SOLDIERS OF DE QUEEN.”
—From a woodcut by Felix Vallotton.
lutely in her chair and murmured, “If I could
only say the right thing. ...”
“The right thing,” said the inward Voice,
“ would be to say nothing. Faith that urges him
to live, Hope that would spur him to rise—from
you it would be only words, words, words. A
man would rather be sent to perdition by the
woman he loves than saved from it by one he
despises.” - *
The Angel was frowning.
The Woman shrank. “No one has ever been
given the right to despise me,” she whispered,
“least of all he.”
“ Well, the woman to whom he is indifferent,
then. But when a woman loves a man who does
not love her the margin between contempt or in-
difference is so thih that he scarcely knows it is
there.”
“Be silent! ” commanded the Woman.
“If I could only make you understand,” the
Woman wrote, “the kind my love has grown to
be, you would come to me and take as tributes all
I could give (and I would give much) then pass
out of my life, for ever if you wished, but hap-
pier.”
“If you could only make him understand,”
echoed the Voice, “that you are offering to pay
his debts, he would endure the threatened sym-
pathy and judicious advice and come. . .
The Woman made a hasty, repelling gesture,
and continued to write.
The Angel was smiling.
“Dearest,” the Woman wenton, “if I were not
quite certain that Jmu remember as I do, love still
as I do, I would feel, in writing this, as one who
goes into a battle unarmed . . . but if I had any
dodbt I should not write at all. Yet how few ap-
parent reasons I have (I can see you smile at
apparent—as if a great, love were a visible
thing). Am 1 not magnificent ? . . .
“ Your vanity is,” murmured the Voice, “ to run
against the cold steel of that man’s nature, to
think that, because you are constant, his memory
is steadfast.”
“Always faithful to me,” murmured the
Woman.
“ Yet he turned from you . . . How often . . .
and to what husks he made his vows . . . Ah,
your cheeks burn ...”
“ With pride at the memory of his kisses.”
“ So he kissed the husks ...”
“No, no ! ”
The Woman dropped the pen; her head fell
forward on her arm. “ I know whose Voice you
are,” she moaned; “but ah, God ! let me be deaf
for a little while.”
“ These poor mortals,” the Angel mused; out
of vanity and convention they conjure a whine
and call it the Voice of God.”
The Woman’s pen was racihg over the paper
again. Her left hand screened what the other
wrote.
“ You are ashamed of it,” the Voice persisted.
She only smiled and wrote faster.
“ Rake, spendthrift, idler ! ” spoke the Voice.
“ Gay, kind and fearless ! ” retorted the Woman.
“ Unfaithful husband, faithless lover.”
“ Never faithless to me. . . I am the one he
will love to death, my soul his soul will seek after
death.” . „ „
“You remember that autumn two years ago.
She was silent, writing, and the Voice went on,
“You recollect the girl— ‘ empty shell was your
Ph-afreconect. The ‘ shell ’ was prettily
marked. . .”
“Your rival. . .” ,
“No rivalry possible between me and such as
she. If the woman had been another Me. . .
The Angel was siftiling.
“If you and he were free, urged^the Voice,
“do you think he would turn to you?”
“ As gladly as dawn turns to sunrise.
“ His long silences ...”
“ I am glad he did not weaken them by letters
. . . did I not*' know . . . does God write
letters, or need them . . . God is Love. . .’
“ Can I urge nothing that ...”
“ Nothing. Even the greatest crime would only
. . . did I not say I was magnificent ? . .”
She was still writing, unmindful of the chill
night. Her lips and cheeks were red.
The Voice was silent a space, then murmured:
“In love such as yours—and his—every trifle
“In love like ours there are no trifles. Scanty
incidents become events. Every meeting begins
and closes an epoch.” . ,
“ Good. And in such love there is no forgetiul-
“ None. Apparently cruel, seemingly faithless,
it often is—never forgetful.”
“ If you thought that he remembered you only
when he saw or heard from you would you send
that letter?”
“ No. It would be no more to him than waste
paper. It will save him because he loves me.”
“ When you came back from Europe . . . you
had told him the date of your return . . . and
met by chance weeks later ... do you recall his
look of surprise ? He 'said something about your
‘ unexpected ’ arrival ...”
The Woman stopped her pen in the middle
of a word. Her eyes were full of surprise and
fear. “Once,” the Voice went on, “once only
you asked him to do something for you—a mere
trifle. He forgot . . . you forgave him because
of the smile with which he told you he forgot.”
The Woman was looking blankly before her. _
“ It is but a trifle,” she said, weakly, “ proving
nothing ...” The Voice laughed, and she hastily
finished the broken word and sentence.
■ The Angel looked anxious.
* * * *
The Man was stirring uneasily. Once he moaned
rather than spoke the Woman’s name.
* * * *
“ And the dinner at his chambers . . the
Voice persisted.
“ I sat at his right,” argued the Woman ; “ his
eyes . . . his swift hand-clasp under the cloth
. . .” She was writing swiftly once more.
“ One of the guests found his autograph-book.
Names were signed . . . you wrote yours ...”
The. Woman was staring fixedly at the
unfinished letter ; her hands dropped by her sides.
“. . . and he looked over your shoulder,” the
Voice went on, “ and exclaimed, ‘ Felice ’—is that
one of your names ? What a beautiful one it is ! ’
. . . You had signed it occasionally in your letters
to him ... And in love such as yours there can be
no forgetfulness, no trifles ... all is significant—
and eventful.”
* * * •
The Woman had risen. She was shivering. Her
lips and oheeks were pale, and she was angry be-
cause her watch had run down. On the table were
the fragments of a letter.
No Angel leaned out from the gold bar of
Heaven.
The Man waited on until morning had nearly-
waned to noon, and then —and then he died.
N.S.W.
A. Conor-o’Brien.
“ Ignotus ” ■ comments on a recent vol. of the
Contemporary Science Series —
Starbuck’s “ Psychology of Religion ” is disappointing.
It does not deal with religion generally, but with a
religion ; and chiefly with the one phenomenon known as
“ conversion.” The book is long and diffuse. The author
seems really only to establish one thing ; that “ conver-
sion occurs chiefly at three periods. In males, at 12, 15J,
and 18. In girls, at 13, 16 and 19. Are we to conclude,
then, that it is only a pathological sex-disturbance ? Per-
sonally, all I have gained from the book is a suspicion that
the physical changes which occur at puberty are very
complex; that three great disturbances take place. From
what women have told me I should say there are cer-
tainly three well-marked stages of growth to maturity.
As to boys, you cannot cross-examine the “innocent,”
and the avowedly corrupt are very unreliable, besides
being often apparently abnormal. If doctors could work
the subject up and explain it in books accessible to
parents ; if said parents would read the books; if boys,
from 13 to 19, could have proper explanation and advice,
much might be gained. My own youthful experience was
that certain matters—facts both physical and psychical—
caused me great distress of mind, and though I sought
advice and guidance in absolutely the best quarter,
nothing was told me ; except, perhaps, that I was to fast
and pray. I had the current physiological text-books, but
somehow could not understand them. Had I been plainly
told exactly what it all meant, from what pitfalls should I
have been saved!
In Sydney’s Bohemia—the story of the Poet
and the Painter. They had a famous night,
a night of stars, a night of tears and whiskey, and
the Poet missed the last suburban boat. He was
bartonically “stranded” on the cold streets.
Then the Painter—an artist of the breezy open-air
school, whose pictures appear chiefly on hoardings,
rose to the heights of faithful Abdiel. He took
the homeless Poet home. . Up a flight of stairs,
many flights of stairs, ricketty, mouldy, incon-
ceivably crooked. (Maybe the beautiful night’s
exuberance accounted for some of the crookedness.)
And when they arrived, somewhere near the room
the Painter lit an end of candle and “ Enter ! ”
The neatest little garret you ever saw. A single
bed in one corner ; a huge roll 'of canvas in the
other. The Painter wished his guest to take the
bed. “ Never ! ” 'said the Poet. They argued,
, icy argued ; they were nearly frozen. The Poet
was adamant ;—it was a mere bed, not a drink.
Arid the desperate Painter dumped down the
huge roll of canvas and began to pay it out for a
'shake-down. “ Picture of the Mediterranean Sea,”
he explained. “ I painted it for a religious dior-
ama company that went broke and left it on my
hands. Look at the warmth of colour,' dear
boy! Observe the glowing tints of the sea
near Cyprus ! See how the heat shimmers and
shakes in the atmosphere around Malta!
Makes you feel hot to look at it, eh ? By Jove !
you have the best of the bargain, after all.” And
the guest went to sleep witli his ear on Jerusalem
arid his toes curled up in the Straits of Gibraltar.
When he gathered his shivering bones together in
the chill dawn and went away his ague was more
than balanced by the fever of his imagination.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Brown Old River.
There’s a river to the nor’ard,
♦ And a breeze across a bay,
And the breeze is on my forehead,
Though I’m scores of leagues away.
There is mud there, black and clinging,
When the tide is half or low,
But I hear the river singing
River-songs I used to know;
And ’tis calling me, that river—
I can see the ripples shiver
On its breast, and see the quiver
Of the moon deep down below.
There’s a river, and I hear it
Telling stories to the breeze,
And I’m longing to go near it
O’er the weary, plunging seas.
When you swing around Cape Moreton,
Where the silver sandbanks are,
Where the rollers trip and shorten
Ere they sprawl across the bar ;
Then you’ll see the river streaming
As I see it now, day-dreaming,
And the Pile Light’s lazy gleaming,
Like an earth-attracted star.
There’s a river, and it’s muddy,
But its banks are always green,
And its dark-brown stream is ruddy
In the sunset’s bronzelike sheen.
And ’tis always softly singing
To fond favored ones like me,
As it takes its course a-swinging
To the bay that woos the sea,
With a greeting to the bridges
And the mud-banks’ rosy ridges
Where the rusty, ugly dredges
Clank and clatter noisily.
There’s a river, haunt of dreamers,
Black and silver ’neath the moon,
Where the yellow-lighted steamers
Throb and hum an ocean-tune.
I can hear the rollers sprawling
As they stumble o’er the bar,
And I hear the river calling,
Calling, calling, faint and far.
I can see the moonbeams shiver
On that muddy, brown old river,
And the Pile Light’s sleepy quiver
Like a tired and dozing star.
Vic.
QUILP N.
The Day’s Dream.
Young morning’s breath strays lightly through my
hair ;
The sun is hiding yet behind the trees.
And, drinking in big gulps the limpid air
And list’ning to the chatter of the breeze,
I feast my eyes upon the distance, where
A fleet of amber-tinted argosies
Are sailing through the ether ; and they bear
My thoughts away with them to nameless seas.
What, after all, is woman’s love, to ask
The whole, the undivided heart of man ?
A sickly puppet in a tinsel mask !
How mean she looks as in the dawn I scan
Her faded face. Oh ! for some giant’s task,
A wanderer’s life, a place among the van !
And now, the noonday scintillates on high !
The very breezes even are asleep ;
And one by one my fancies droop and die.
A languorous temptation seems to creep
About my heart and drowsily to sigh
Stay, build a cottage where the waters leap,
Where easily the days are floating by,
Strained through a wilderness of roses deep !
And I succumb. My ardent zeal is spent;
Why should I tramp the weary, endless road,
Encounter death a cripple, worn and bent,
To perish painfully beneath my load ?
No ! Happiness is peace and still content;
This tranquil Eden is her true abode !
But, lo ! the sun has vanished in the west.
Oh, tender sadness of the magic eve;
The misty glamour of enchanted rest
Is veiling nature. Softened colors weave
A fairy mantle for the withered breast
Of Mother Earth ; her pulses throb and heave,
A flush of youth returns and calls her blest;
And she remembers—and forgets to grieve.
Old melodies are crooning from the past;
A faint elixir is transfusing me ;
The snows bleak life around my heart has massed
Are melting into silent reverie.
Ah, for the happiness I found at last,
Sad smiling paradise of Memory !
But in the East a silver-flood has swept
The roses from the sky. Vast depths appear.
lam alone in space. The night has leapt
Upon me. Dread infinity is near.
Out of a cosy shelter I have stepped
Into the presence of the fates that steer
The Universe through Time. A chill has crept
Into my breast, and shakes my soul with fear.
Oh, give me love, a woman’s love, to hold
My lonely heart within her precious care !
What are they all to me, your fame and gold ?
I hunger for a gem beyond compare.
The world is pitiless, the world is cold ;
And only love can make it warm and fair !
And thus the cycle ends where it was born,
While days, and months, and years are gliding
by.
And with the throe of many passions torn,
Dazed by the ravishment of visions high,
I find myself with every flaunting morn,
With every fragrant eve that paints the sky,
Still questioning, still purposeless and lorn,
Still asking for an answer to a why.
And 10, the others enter, each his goal,
Where the awards of proud achievement gleam ;
And they inscribe their names upon the roll
Of living fame, of recompense supreme.
The icy hand of Death is on my soul —
The race is run—and still, I lie adream !
VON KOTZE.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—

you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every

to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
■ publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“Correspondence ’’ column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that ive
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Blade and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
■ — i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of lid. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage — lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
SATURDAY, JULY 14, 1900.
The Case Against Westralian
Federation.
The Wesbralian Federal Referendum takes
place on the last day of this month, and in
view thereof the Parochialists of Groper
Land have girded up their intelligence for
the fray. In the main they are making a
poor show of argument. It would seem
that along with a great want of conscience
there is a still greater lack of coherent in-
vention among the leaders of the Little
Groper party, and that stupendous imagina-
tive capacity which made the Federal cam-
paign such a thrilling episode in the East is
wholly absent. There isn’t the material for
even a good snake story among the Little
Gropers. Their literature is tame ; their
inaccuracy, as compared to the glorious
mendacity of the Eastern Parochialists, is as
a cockroach to an Eiffel tower ; even their
inferences don’t show the amazing irrele-
vance of the inferences drawn by their
Eastern brethren, who, if a house blew down
in Sydney, or a d.t. patient slaughtered his
mother in Melbourne, or a ship sank off the
coast of Queensland, immediately rose and
proved that the near approach of Australian
union was the sole cause, and that houses,
however old, never blew down, and ships,
however old, always remained up in a suf-
ficiently disunited country.
* * * *
As a striking instance of the feebleness of
the Westralian Parochialist case, an official
manifesto of the Disunionist League—it calls
itself “National” by way of irony because
it wants to prevent the making of a nation
and preserve instead a series of spots—is
worth quoting in its beautiful entirety :
Intercolonial Freetrade will deprive the Commonwealth
of £1,000,000, which is at present collected from Customs
duties.
The proposed federating colonies now collect
£6,000,000 on British and foreign imports.
The Federal Government will require £7,170,000 to
enable the departments taken over to be kept going, to
provide for the “ new services,” and to enable the quota
of three-fourths of the net revenue to be returned to the
States. [Ffde Mr. Owen’s report.]
The people must therefore be further taxed to the ex-
tent of £1,170,000.
Besides the losses in respect to the departments taken
over, Federation means an additional Governor-General,
two extra Houses of Parliament with members paid at
£4OO a year each, an extra set of Ministers, a further civil
establishment, more courts of justice, an Inter-State
Commission, etc.
All these luxuries must be paid for, and paid for by the
people additional to their present taxation.
To raise £7,170,000 where £6,000,000 only is now raised
must mean increased duties.
The masses must contribute more than at present.
An increased tariff will afford oreater protection
AND LEAD TO EXTENDED PRODUCTION THROUGHOUT AUSTRALIA.
The masses will pay for Australian-manufactured goods
at prices which are within a fraction of the imported
articles.
_ If, owing to increased protection, Australian produc-
tion is greater the volume of imports will be less.
To enable the Commonwealth Treasurer to meet his
engagements, he must obtain his revenue of £7,170,000
regardless of the local production.
The Commonwealth Treasurer can only obtain his
revenue, with any degree of certainty, by taxing those
articles which cannot be manufactured or produced
within the Commonwealth, and which the people must
have for their daily use.
WHAT ARE THESE ARTICLES ?
(a) Arrowroot, sago, tapioca, cornflour, farinaceous
foods, coffee, cocoa, rice, sugar, tea, oatmeal, molasses,
kerosene, spirits, &c., tobacco.
With the exception of spirits and tobacco, which are
taxed everywhere, all the articles enumerated in the
above list are now free of duty in Western Australia.
It is obvious, therefore, that the cost of living must be
greater under Federation than now, not only to the ex-
tent of the duties, but also by the sellers’ profits on those
duties.
TO THE LADIES.
If you desire to compare your present grocer’s bill with
what it will be under Federation, add to the items the
duties thus Arrowroot, sago, tapioca, cornflour, and
farinaceous foods, Id. per lb. ; coffee, 3d. per lb. ; cocoa,
3d. per lb. ; rice, fd. per lb. ; sugar, Id. per lb. ; tea, 4d.
per lb.; oatmeal, Id. per lb. ; molasses, Jd. per lb. ; kero-
sene, 6d. per gallon.
Remember this —That the Government of Western
Australia has promised that next session the duties will
be taken off meats of all kinds, and a majority of mem-
bers is pledged to remove the duties on butter, eggs,
cheese, hams, bacon, &c.
Weigh these matters by July 31 and vote accordingly.
* » * *
Therefore, the beautifully mixed case of
the Provincialist party reads as follows :
(1) Federation is bad because it will lead to £1,000,000
of (interprovincial) customs taxation being taken off.
(2) In the same clause it is set forth that Federation is
equally bad because it will lead to £1,170,000 of customs
taxation being put on. The amount which the Parochial
party says will be taken off and the amount which it says
will be put on almost exactly balance, so according to the
Parochial party Federation will leave the Customs taxes
just about where they were. But the taking off, and the
putting on, and the leaving of things as they were, are all
quoted as being equally good evidence that Federation is
a curse.
(3) After pointing out that the cost of all the new
Federal services—Parliament, courts of justice, Governor.
General, &c. —are included in the iniquitous £1,170,000
of extra duties that are to be put on after the £1,000,000
has been iniquitously taken off, the manifesto quotes
these things all over again and puts them into the bill a
second time.
(4) Then it says that to raise £7,170,000 where only
£6,000,000 is raised now must mean increased duties and
greater taxation on the masses. Yet by its own showing
it is only a case of raising £7,170,000 where £7,000,000 is
now raised, so its allegations are £1,000,000 out, and on
its own figures there is no extra taxation worth mention-
ing to be provided for. At present, according to the
manifesto, the people pay £6,000,000 duties on British
and foreign imports and £1,000,000 on interprovincial
imports, making £7,000,000 in all. And under the new
system, it alleges, they are to pay £7,170,000 on British
and foreign imports and nothing on interprovincial im-
ports, making £7,170,000 in all.
(5) And the Parochial League of One-eyed Men alleges
as a reason against Federation that the increased protec-
tion it is to afford will lead to extended production
throughout Australia. More things will be made here,
and the people will therefore get more work and more
wages in the process of making them. And the Parochial
League implores them to avoid more work and more
wages.
* « • *
(6) And because there will be more production, and
greater abundance of goods, and more competition among
producers, the Parochial League says that prices will be
higher. The Parochialists’ doctrine is that greater abun-
dance makes prices go up ; therefore they are lower
where there is scarcity, and the place where they are
lowest is naturally the place where the supply is least.
In a besieged city which has got down to its last pound of
meat the price of meat would therefore be almost nil.
(7) And in order to raise revenue, according to the
awful League of Unintelligible Gropers, the Federal Trea-
surer must tax things which can’t be produced in Aus-
tralia, such as sugar, oatmeal, molasses, cornflour, rice,
coffee, arrowroot, spirits and tobacco. The League of
Unintelligible Gropers actually doesn’t know that Aus-
tralia supplies itself with great quantities of sugar,
molasses, oatmeal, cornflour, spirits and tobacco, and
that rice, coffee, and arrowroot are growing industries,
and that this continent is quite capable, not only of fully
supplying itself, but of becoming a large exporter. And
the men whose ignorance is so incredible that they don’t
know a little thng like that claim to instruct the people
about large things.
(8) And having set forth, in paragraph 1, that it is
wrong for the Federal Government to take off duties, it
finishes up by the promise that, if Federation is rejected
in Westralia, the Provincial Government will take off
duties. And having set forth that under Federation there
will be more Protection and increased production, and
therefore more work, it promises that, if the people will
only stand solidly by Provincialism, it will give less Pro-
tection, and therefore diminish production and make less
work. And it calls on the people to vote for Provin-
cialism, and less production, and less work.
* * » *
While the League of Uninformed Paro-
chialists declares, in the above manifesto,
that Federation is bad because, among other
things, it will lead, by means of Protection,
to increased production throughout Australia,
it publishes, on the same page of Perth
Morning Herald, another pathetic appeal
to the people to vote for continued Separa-
tism because Federation won’t lead to
extended production. It says Federation
Will hinder the development of town industries, proba-
bly strangling some now progressing.
It will seriously affect the cultivation of the soil, but
will cause much less injury to the squatter.
Also, the first manifesto offers, as a bribe to
the people to vote against Federation, that,
as sorn as that noxious thing is defeated,
the Provincial Government will reward the
people by taking off the duties which now
hinder the importation of meat and cattle
from the other provinces—it being evidently
implied that the people will gain some bene-
fit by the abolition. And the second mani-
festo urges them to vote against Federation
because, though it will necessarily abolish
the meat duties, the public will gain no
benefit thereby. First manifesto :
Remember this—That the Government of Western Aus-
tralia has promised next session the duties will be taken
off meats of all kinds, and a majority of members is
pledged to remove the duties on butter, eggs, cheese,
hams, bacon, etc. Weigh these matters by July 31 and
vote accordingly.
Second manifesto :
It (Federation and the consequent abolition of inter-
provincial duties) will not reduce the price of meat, be-
cause it is the local supply which must govern the price,
the freight from the Eastern colonies being under the
control of a ring of shipowners, and will be raised or
lowered in accordance with local consumption.
Thus it is claimed that the (certain) abolition
of the meat duties under Federation won’t
reduce the price of meat, and also
that their (only probable) abolition under
continued Provincialism will reduce the
price of meat and do the people a lot of
good. The first assertion, that taking off - a
duty under Federation won’t reduce prices,
doesn’t hang well together with the previous
assurance that putting on duties under
Federation will raise prices. If meat will
be the same price with or without duty,
why won’t a similar rule apply to sugar and
coffee ?
* * * *
Also, the first manifesto says that sugar
will be heavily taxed because it is an article
that can’t be produced in Australia. And the
second one goes on to say that Federation
“ will raise the price of sugar because a heavy
tax will be put on in the interests of that in-
dustry on the eastern coast.” So it is
claimed that the fact that sugar isn’t grown
in Australia and the fact that it is grown
there are equally dead-sure reasons why it
will be taxed—though why the growers of
sugar should discourage themselves by tax-
ing their own product passes understanding.
* * * »
The Perth Morning Herald is the loudest
and most rampant opponent of Federation
and the League’s principal ally. The
League’s first manifesto, as quoted above,
sets forth that it will be necessary under
Federation to increase Australia’s customs
duties from £7,000,000 to £7,170,000,
and that this will be bad because
the extra Protection will cause “ ex-
tended production throughout Australia ”
(“throughout,” be it observed—i.e., in all
parts of Australia) and thus create more
employment, which is evidently a bad thing
and one to be discouraged. In its second
manifesto it also sets forth that Federation
is bad because it will increase taxation :
It will, by impoverishing’ the State, and by increasing
taxation, render the consideration of public works impos-
sible, until the degree of financial losses can lie ascer-
tained and their effect upon the public credit of the State
realised.
And the anti - Federal Perth Morning
Herald publishes both these manifestoes
with a loud whoop. But it says (leading
article of 26th May) that there will be no
loss and no impoverishment to the Wes-
tralian State and thereby flatly contradicts
its party’s main argument :
. . . The Premier . . . stated that there could be “ no
doubt that for some years to come Westralia must be a
loser by Federation,” and that reduced to hard figures
that loss would be £300,000 a-year. No attempt has been
made to challenge the general accuracy of the details. . .
Now we do not pretend that the £300,000 which will be
lost to the State revenue will be lost to the country. Not
at all. It will still be in the pockets of the people.
And again in its leading article of 26th June:
We are on common ground with the thinking portion
of the Federalist party when we say that the loss which
federation will involve the State Treasurer in will amount
to about £300,000. This money will certainly be in the
pockets of the people.
So that one anti-Federal legend is that the
State will be impoverished and taxation
increased. And • the other is that the cus-
toms taxes will take £300,000 less annually
from the people, which is diminishing taxa-
tion—not increasing it; and that there will
be no counterbalancing loss, for the £300,000
will be in the people’s pockets It won’t be
lost, and as the money will still be in the
State the State can’t be impoverished.
* • * *
And while the League of Parochialists
objects to Federation because it will increase
customs taxation, its mouthpiece, the
Morning Herald, is against it quite as
bitterly on the ground that it will diminish
customs taxation—so far, at least, as Wes-
tralia is concerned :
Mr. Matheson’s figures are indeed the figures of a
financial phantasmagoria—a sort of fiscal and statistical
nightmare. They are all based upon assumption—and
that assumption the wrong one. Mr. Matheson assumes
that the Federal Government will collect the same duties
as we in Western Australia do now ; and upon this his
financial house of cards is erected. Obviously, it would
be quite impossible to inflict any such outrageous tariff as
ours throughout the length and breadth of Australia.
The tariffs of the eastern colonies range from £1 10s. per
head per annum to £2 11s. ; while our own gathers in the
shekels to the tune of £6 6s. per head. None of the
eastern provinces will ever consent to increasing its tariff
burdens to this enormous extent, so, the foundations
being destroyed,' Mr. Matheson’s lengthy tangle of figures
falls of its own weight.
Thus the Perth Morning Herald damns
Australian union which, it says, will reduce
Westralia’s “outrageous tariff” .and is in
favour of the separate system which created
and maintained, and still maintains, that
“ outrageous tariff.” But a few days later,
in its leading article of 26th June, the same
Morning Herald rose to state that, being
already against Federation because it would
make the customs tariff lighter, it was also
opposed to it because it would make the cus-
toms tariff heavier :
It is admitted that, whereas £6,000,000 is now collected
by the colonies, £8,000,000 must be collected under Feder-
ation.
(The Parochial party’s official manifesto,
appearing in the same issue, says that
£7,000,000 is now collected, and it must be
increased to £7,170,000 under Federation,
but that is a mere detail.)
Therefore the people must pay £2,000,000 more on
something, and the probabilities are that foodstuffs will
be the articles subjected to the additional taxation, and
for this reason :—Heavy duties on luxuries and articles
which are not absolutely necessary to the existence of
the people will not avail the Treasurer, because the people,
if they cannot afford to purchase, will be able to do with-
out them. Again, it will not avail the Treasurer to tax
articles which are produced within the Commonwealth,
for there will be no imports of them, and consequently no
revenue.
(This gives the lie straight to the statement
already quoted that sugar is going to be
taxed.)
Therefore, it follows that, in order to enable the
Treasurer to obtain the amount he requires, he must
frarhe a tariff which will tax those articles which the people
must have, and which cannot be produced within the Com-
monwealth. It is very easy, then, to discover what articles
must be taxed ; they are the articles which are necessary
to the existence of the people, and which are in everyday
use, i.e., foodstuffs, &c. As £8,000,000 must be collected
as against £6,000,000 on these particular articles, it seems
tolerably clear that living- must be dearer.
* » * *
This article stops here, not for want of
material, which is endless, but for want of
space. The case for Federation has been
set forth so often in The Bulletin that it
hardly requires repetition, so for a change
this paper sets forth the case, or part
of the case, against federation, as
it concerns Westralia, and as the
case is drawn up by the Westralian
anti-Federalists themselves. And the moral
of it is this : If Westralia votes against
Federation on 31st J uly it votes for leaving
its Government, its future, its hopes and
prospects in the hands of the crowd whose
silliness, incapacity, ignorance and want of
every intellectual quality which goes to make
even a decent alderman or an intelligent
dog-catcher glare forth in the extracts
above quoted. And if Westralia is capable
of forming an intelligent judgment it w'ill
decide that men like these are not fitted to
run a sawmill, let alone a State.
A Decayed Democrat.
The sensation of the day in Q. polities is the
definite defection of Thos. Glassey. A surprise
it certainly was not, for everyone expected it—but
its occurrence at this particular juncture and in this
particular fashion was hardly anticipated by the
Q. public. It is well-known that Gf asset’s Demo-
cratic convictions sustained a fatal shock when
that pompous politician was deposed from his
position as Leader of the Labor Party. His sub-
sequent adoption of a rampant Jingo attitude
alienated him still further from the genuine Labor-
men of Bananaland, and his present action in
resigning his seat with a view to contesting it as an
Independent candidate is merely the logical out-
come of a lengthy career of wobbling.
* * * *
The latest convert to the Philpocrats is a man
of very slender abilities, of considerable energy
and of enormous vanity. No man in Queensland
was ever more kindly treated by Fortune, and no
man ever proved so mournfully unequal to the
opportunities thrown in his way. Good men
stood aside to give him a chance ; a patient and
long-suffering Parliamentary Party endured his
ludicrous gyrations until Labour-in-Parliament
was become a laughing-stock and a by-word ; the
country tolerated him on account of a creditable
patch in his early public career.
* * * *
The last elections saw a determined effort on
the part of Glassey to form an independent party,
free from the accursed trammels of a signed
platform. The public showed its appreciation of
the effort by casting all the Glasseyite candidates
into the outer darkness, and Thomas himself only
saved his political bacon by meekly subscribing
to the Labor pledge he so bitterly anathematised.
The Jingo fever, however, gave him an opportun-
ity he was not slow to utilise. Noting that the
majority of his Labor colleagues were against the
war, and consequently reaping a temporary
political odium thereby, Glassey hoisted the flag
of Ultra-Jingoism, advocated the levying of a
subscription-tax on the Civil Servants, and in
every way proved himself a worthy comrade of
the Fat Jingoes he formerly abominated.
Unfortunately (for him) the general elections
were not to come off for a couple of
years or so, and, with the abating of Jingo fever,
the crafty politician saw that his prospect of reap-
ing a harvest in the shape of re-election was a
very small one. Inspired by a brilliant idea, he
accordingly resigned his seat suddenly, arranged
that the election should come off in less than a
fortnight, and posed as the statesman who would
be pinned to no platform. His speech to the elec-
tors contained scarcely an allusion to local politics
but bristled with the most ferocious Jingoism.
* * * *
Queensland has seen the old Independent mem-
bers one by one nobbled and noosed. Queensland
doesn’t believe much in the present Government,
and it may not be in accord with the Labor-party,
but it has certainly done for ever with the Poli-
tical Pendulum. This attempt to resuscitate a
mischievous medley of Government discontents
and insincere Oppositionist opportunists should
not be tolerated for a moment. Let the disaffected
Laborite come out as a straight Philpite (if straight
Philpism be a possibility); let him drop his Jingo
jabber; let him candidly avow his affection for
black labor, Seaforth Estate and Barron Falls
fakes, and then the Bananalanders will know
exactly where they stand and where he stands.
* * * *
Naturally the Govt, organs are filled with
admiration for Glassey’s heroism. Years ago that
decayed democrat used to say that their cheery
diatribes against himself were Glassey’s finest
testimonial. If this be so the converse holds good
and their present praise is his most bitter damna-
tion. Naturally Philp and party are filled with
admiration also and don’t intend to put forward a
Govt, candidate to oppose a pervert who comes
along so sweetly and unctuously. And people are
asking with a knowing grin, “ Well, what port-
folio is waiting for this one ? ”
* * * *
After all, it is just as well for the Q. party of
progress that the trouble has come to pass.
Hitherto the poison was working in the blood and
no one knew when and where the pustules would
appear. . . Glassey has taken his plunge, and
even a temporary triumph at the hustings won’t
save him very long from sinking to “ where he
belongs.’
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Fallen Flat.
The cooling down of the military fury of the
Australasian press is a curious spectacle. Six
or eight months ago the rage to send con-
tingents to aid Britain in her alleged “hour of
need ” was deafening; now Britain has a worse
hour of need on hand and things are very,
very cold. Premier Seddon, of Maoriland,
suggested moving, and the local press —the Jingo
press —intimated that he was a swash buckler, and
described his offer as “playing to the gallery.”
A moustachioed person—who from a mere un-
trained civilian has risen to be a Colonel with
astounding velocity, and whom no one takes
seriously —offered to go from Sydney, and Premier
Lyne spoke feebly of a contingent. Promptly
the Australian Star said that war-fever is
a disease requiring to be “choked out,” and the
Evening News wet-blanketed the idea, and the
morning papers gave it no encouragement, and
Opposition-leader Reid sneered at it openly and
in a way which, three months ago, would have been
branded as disloyal; and even the maddest Jingo


among the country papers has suspended its
Jingoism for the present. And yet there are
20 times as good reasons for offering a Chinese
contingent as there were for offering the S’African
one. Consider the relative cases :
In the Transvaal the enemy numbered about 150,000 —
men, wmAen and infants in arms ; not enough to make a
third-rate English city.
The Chinese Boxers are reputed to number 11,000,000,
and apparently the Government, the army, and the
300,000,000 population of Chinkiedom are behind them.
In the Transvaal case Britain had no other war on hand.
She could roll the whole of her hundreds of millions down
on that handful of 150,000.
Now she has the Transvaal war and the Ashantee rebel-
lion and the Chinese trouble on hand all at once. If she
wanted aid with only the Boer States to deal with, how
much more does she want it now with them and two
extra contracts on hand !
It didn’t matter a straw, except for the prestige of the
empire, if Ladysmith, Kimberley and Mafeking fell. The
worst consequence would be that the garrisons would be
kindly treated and well fed at Pretoria.
The need for relieving the beseiged Britishers at Pekin
was the need to save men, women and children from an
enemy who practises torture, mutilation, the amputation
of the feet and the scalding of the stumps with boiling tar,
and other horrors unspeakable. And there may soon be
the same need to save other British settlements.
Ladysmith, Mafeking and Kimberley were prepared for
defence and the garrisons were not so very inferior in
point of numbers to the assailants. There was every sign
that they could hold out easily till Britain came to their
assistance. They didn’t need Australian aid.
In China aid from anywhere and everywhere has been
needed from the beginning with a desperate necessity.
There was no possible hurry to down the Boers. Long
before the Australians arrived Britain had enough troops
on the scene' to protect Britain’s territory from further
invasion. No other Power could intervene, for Britain
had control of the sea. She had years before her in which
to eat up her fly-speck enemy at her leisure, without the
least chance of him getting assistance.
In the present case, not only are British lives andproperty
being lost, but a British force has been defeated, and was
only rescued from destruction by foreign assistance, and
that, to a great extent, the assistance of uncertain friends
who will probably prove enemies later on. If the Dignity
of the Flag is not involved here, what is the Dignity of
the Flag ? And, even in its retreat, the scanty British
force is not held to be safe. As for adyance, that seems
out of the question.
Australia has interests of her own in China. She has
scattered girl-missionaries over that country, and, if any
of them have been captured, they are probably being
boiled alive by the Chinaman. Yet, somehow,
when the Chinese murder an Australian woman
with horrible tortures it only leads to a meeting of
pietists who say it is the Lord’s will. Yet when Paul
Kruger refused the suffrage to some British subjects this
country went mad.
And when Kruger made war on British subjects accord-
ing to the rules of modern campaigning, and treated his
prisoners kindly, this country went raving mad.
Yet when the Chinese made war on British subjects
and slaughtered prisoners indiscriminately, this country
hardlv turned a hair.
Australia rushed to help Britain when Britain had one
war on hand and its army was more numerous than the
enemy’s whole nation. It said the dear old mother
country needed assistance.
Now Britain has three wars on hand, and it is so
desperately short of men on the spot for the third one
that its commander and his force had to leave the British
subjects at Pekin to their fate. The abandonment of
Mafeking, Ladysmith and Kimberley all put together
would have been nothing to this. And the papers say the
dear old mother country doesn’t need any help.
Australian troops could get to China in three weeks,
in time to be of real assistance in upholding the Flag, and
to perish gloriously, or be fried alive if captured. British
reinforcements can hardly get there under two months.
In the Transvaal war Britain’s 100,000 new troops could
get on the spot quite as fast as Australia’s few hundreds,
so there was no urgent want for the latter to fill. Still,
their anxiety to fill it was tremendous.
Of course the Chinese row isn’t a pleasant thing
to meddle' with. It isn’t, like the Transvaal
affair, a case of going to join the side that has
odds of three to one in its favor already, nor is it
going to fight an enemy who gives you victuals
and tobacco and an overcoat when he takes you
prisoner, and sends you to the infirmary if you are
wounded. It would be a case of going to face
heavy odds, and if the Chinese enemy captures
you he probably saws your feet off, as he did with
a Times correspondent in a previous war ; or gives
you 500 lashes, or breaks your ankles to extract in-
formation. But still when Britain’s army on the
spot has got into such straits that it had to be rescued,
largely by foreigners ; and when British women
had to be left to probable torture or outrage, surely
all the Upholders of the Flag who did that noble
drunk last May should come forward. Has the
press that so lately vaunted itself Imperial become
wholly pro-Chow and taken bribes from Li Hung
Chang ? Has it thrown “ Rule Britannia ”
overboard ? Where is Purves, and where,
also, is Dampier’s Chinese-war drama ? What
has . become of the Bairnsdale paper and the
terrible Jingo of the Newcastle Herald? Why
don’t the Melbourne shareholders procesh, and
isn’t there material left for another Bushmen’s
Fund. And among all the people who went mad
when a white man shot at a male Briton in open
war, isn’t there one who seriously cares if a yellow
man butchers female Britons by treachery in time
of peace ?
Gillies’ Price.
Victoria’s two-headed Ministry retained its grasp
of office last week through the support of the
Tories, and defeated Turner’s want-of-confidence
motion by 11 votes. During the debate Gillies
angrily denied the charge that he had bargained
for three seats in the Ministry in return for his
support, and his denial was probably quite
correct, for it becomes increasingly apparent that
the old Parliamentary schemer looked much
further ahead than the life of the present Cabinet
when he gave his support. Canny old Scawtchman
M‘Lean has already stated that he sees no urgent
necessity for greatly reducing the number of mem-
bers of the State Parliament, and now he proposes,
in mapping out the electorates for the Federal
House of Representatives, to give the country
electorates more than their just share of represent-
ation. Also the Tories of the House of Fat have
of late been very busy and insistent with a simple
little scheme of proportional voting which is “to
secure the representation of minorities.” The
party is a great deal more anxious about
securing the representation of minorities
than . the. representation of majorities. The
new idea is sponsored by such men as Sargood,
Walter Madden, Moles worth Green, Murray
Smith, and the Freetectionist Bevan, which
throws a murky cloud of suspicion on its genesis.
These pseudo-Liberals, with charming inconsis-
tency, are urging their scheme on Premier
McLean for the Federal elections, declaring with
one voice that they want, not only one-man-one-
vote, but one-vote-one-value, while at the same
time they applaud the Premier’s decision not to
lay. out equal electorates. This intended out-
voting of the town elector by his less progressive
brother in the backblocks is evidenced by the fact
that the whole of the McLean Ministry’s sup-
porters are drawn from the country, barring this
handful—Minister Watt, Gillies (of Toorak),
Murray Smith, Hume Cook, and Frank
Madden. ; The Federal. Electorates Bill is
the next item on the Parliamentary programme,
and there are no lack of signs that a very
solid effort is going to be made by someone,
per medium of the proportional vote and other-
wise, to secure the representation of minorities so
successfully that the Tory minority will sometimes
get the seat that should go to the Democratic
majority. But in view of the one-man-one-vote
proviso in the Commonwealth Constitution it is
pretty safe to bet that the scheme will fail miser-
ably. The Gillies party has never run up against
absolute manhood suffrage yet.

The Darling Harbour Resumptions.
Premier Lyne’s Bill for the resumption of some
of the dirtiest parts of Sydney has many good
points and a few weak ones. The best point is
that it now gives no power of re-sale, and makes
the resumed property a Government asset in per-
petuity. And among the bad ones it provides for
raising the necessary funds by selling £4,000,000
worth, or any less amount, of State securities, in
London or elsewhere, at interest not exceeding 4
per cent. The London is mentioned so pointedly,
and the elsewhere is so little alluded to that it
seems highly probable the awful blunder of last
February is to be deliberately repeated. And
yet there was an opportunity which even a blind
man might have recognised. The measure should
have said simply that, when the amount of com-
pensation is finally settled, it should be paid by
handing to each property-owner N.S.W. Govern-
ment securities equal to the amount due to him,
said securities to bear interest at 3jr or per cent. ;
interest payable in Sydney only, and securities to
be interminable except at the option of the
Government. This would prevent any more such
blunders as the recent sale of 4 per cent. Treasury
Bills in London ■ when the event showed that
the money could have been easily obtained at 3J
per cent, in Sydney. It would make sure that the
bulk of the new debt would be held locally, and
that N.S.W. would be some millions the less in
pawn to the absentee. It would make N.S.W.
so much the nearer to being financially self-
dependent. It would save exchange on the remit-
tance of interest, and save exchange on the remit-
tance of the principal when the debt is paid off.
And it surely can’t be said that N.S.W. State
securities aren’t safe enough or good enough value.
There is time yet to repair this grievous omission.
Moreover, there is still time to amend the Bill in
another important direction. All new public
works schemes of any size should have attached to
them a proviso that all profits which they may pro-
duce over ancLabove working expenses and interest
shall not go into the general revenue, but shall be
used to pay off the money borrowed for the
construction of these works. It would be better
still to enact that every new loan must have a
sinking fund attached, but even the other proviso
would be vastly better- than The idea
involves no hardship, no sacrifice, no extra exer-
tion, no violent sitting up of a tired community in
its sleep. It doesn’t take anything out of the
taxpayers’ pockets, or impose any new liability on
the Treasury. It merely amounts to the enact-
ment that if any extra net income should happen
to fall on N.S.W. from this particular investment
without any sacrifice or exertion on its part, it will
use the money first to pay its debts with, and only
start drinking the proceeds after the debts are
paid. And that isn’t a hard thing to do. A new
measure of three or four clauses would suffice to
apply the principle, not only to the harbor-side
resumptions but also to the electric trams and other
present and prospective works; and it could be
passed in an hour or two.

Mt. Deddick Railway Syndicate.
When the Deddick private railway proposal comes
before Vic. Parliament, that body will do well to
look very carefully at the suggested agreement
with the Deddick railway syndicate. Apparently
a Mr. Sheddon, representing sundry benevolent
English investors, wants to be allowed to build
90 miles of railway, at a cost of £360,000, purely
out of love for this long-suffering country. At
least, all he asks in return is the lease of a paltry
1000 acres of alleged mineral country, high up on
some lone, bleak mountains in the wilds of
E. Gippsland, and the gift of the land on which
the line is to be built. The country round is poor
and thinly peopled, so there are few prospects of
agricultural traffic. The region where the wished-
for mineral lease is located has been reported on
by Govt. Geologist Stirling and Govt. Metal-
lurgist Jenkins, and both reports are damnatory.
They almost say it isn’t worth Having at a gift.
On the face of it the scheme looks the wildest of
wild cats, and it appears as if the promoters
merely want a concession—almost any kind of
concession —to float on the London market.
It is always possible to fill up a prospectus by
pointing out the success of the Mt. Lyell mines
and railway; the prospects of Chillagoe ; the
enormous dividends paid by the railway to Broken
Hill mineral field ; and then to state that the co.
has secured a concession to build a line to the new
mineral field of Deddick in Gippsland (staggering
statistics of the immense mineral wealth of Gipps-
land could be ; filled in here), and gets the land
gratis, &c., &c. The next probability would be
that the debenture-holders would camp on the
steps of Victoria’s Treasury, crying for relief, as
the Midland debenture-holders are in Maoriland.
There seem to be just these possibilities about
Deddick : If the Federal capital happens to be
fixed on the N.S.W. side close up to the grue-
some hill, then Victoria will have to buy the
Deddick line (if there is one, or the Deddick
concession) in order to make it part of its
railway to the Federal City, and probably it will
have to buy it at more than the price at which the
State could build a new and better line —either
that or build its own line which the Deddick
railway’s competition will make unprofitable. Or
if the Federal City doesn’t come in that direction
(which is more than probable) the Deddick line
will most likely never be built; or else it will be
built and then abandoned from the want of traffic.
In the former case nothing is gained. In the
latter case Victoria will be besieged on one side by
the few local inhabitants who have become used
to the convenience of a railway and want the State
to take oyer the line and work it at a great loss ;
and it will be bombarded on the other side by
English debenture-holders who will say: “You
gave this concession knowing that it must ruin who-
ever put his money in it. You deliberately and
cold-bloodedly supplied the means for our ruin ;
and you gave no warning when you saw us
falling in. Now, what are you going to do for
us ? ” Even the Gippsland Railway Extension
League appears hostile, and things are bad when
any district organisation protests against the offer
of a railway. Agent Sheddon is understood to
say that there is no intention at all of selling the
concession, and that his co. is already in existence
and ready to start work ; but, on the other hand,
it is also reported that several Deddick mining
co.’s have been invited by Sheddon to amalgam-
ate, with a view of making a big float of all the
properties on the English market. In case of a
flotation on the English market the chances are
that Victoria would get all the trouble and
no railway, or else all the trouble and a railway
that wdl promptly close for want of traffic, with
the cry of the ruined debenture-holder thrown in.
The liyne Government Takes the Bull
by the Horns.
After many years of waiting, N.S. Wales appears
to be actually in sight of a reasonably workable
measure providing for compulsory arbitration in
labor disputes. Once before an attempt was made
in the same direction, but it was never intended
seriously. The authors handled the Unclean
Thing (as they regarded it) so carefully that they
did nothing. In the first place, they could not
bring themselves to definitely encourage trades-
unionism and put both feet down on non-unionism,
and when the Court gives a decision against the
workers it is no good imposing a penalty for non-
observance of the decision unless there is some
fund which can be made responsible for the pay-
ment thereof. Secondly, they couldn’t bring
themselves to impose a legal minimum wage.
Probably 80 per cent, of trade disputes arise
because the masters want to cut the minimum
wage down or the men want to put it up, so the
best part of an Arbitration Court’s work
would consist in fixing a minimum wage. Then
they couldn’t bring themselves to interfere with
“ freedom of contract,” and the “law of demand
and supply ” and the “ liberty of the subject ” and
the right of every man to do as he pleased with
his own property. So in the end they fixed things
that when both parties were agreeable the court
might arbitrate, and the parties might accept its
decision if they felt so inclined. The old N.S.W.
arbitration law was a mockery and a joke and a
stuffed image from the beginning. This time the
Lyne Government is taking the question seriously.
It’s Bill encourages trades-unionism in the most
liberal manner, so that when a labor dispute is
brought before the Court there may be a palpable
plaintiff and defendant in every instance —some
one against whom a penalty can be awarded with
chance of collecting the money. The Bill is wide
enough to take in even the wretched Sydney ’bus-
driver, and give him a chance to live under civil-
ised conditions. It is almost, though not quite,
wide enough to take in the ’bus horse. It provides
that one party to a dispute can invoke the inter-
ference of the Court whether the other one is
willing or not —a most essential proviso, for the
other party never is willing. And it gives
extensive powers to fix a minimum wage in
any trade—another most essential matter,
seeing that that is what most industrial disputes
are about. Also it gives the Court authority to
enforce its awards, and to prevent either party
evading them. If the employer tries to avoid
compliance by a lock-out or the unions by a strike
the Court has power to fall on them heavily. Any
employer discharging men who have gained an
award against him is bound to satisfy the Court
that he did not discharge them as a punishment
for appealing to the Court against him. If either
party tries to coerce the other by a strike or a
lock-out while the case is before the Court, the
Act provides for a bulky penalty. These are the
only conditions upon which a workable Arbitra-
tion and Conciliation law can be framed, and the
Lyne Government has faced the problem in the
only business-like way, and seized the bull
of difficulty by both horns, so to speak. It
deserves the support of every democrat in
N.S.W. for its courage. If its scheme becomes
law no doubt holes will be found in it, as they
are in every new attempt at law-making, and
it will require amendment later on, and it will be
jeered at for its incompleteness by the people
who claim that no Legislature should make
any law unless it can make it perfect at the first
attempt, and nobody should be allowed to invent
anything unless the invention is flawless and in-
capable of improvement, and no one should learn
to read unless he can read words like “ inter-
nationalisation ” immediately. But things like
that are unavoidable, and people like that are
everywhere, and the electric tram doesn’t run over
them nearly so. often as it should do.
Minister Perry Washes His Hands.
N.S.W. Education Minister Perry rises, months
and months too late, to say this :
A statement had been made in a section of the press to
the effect that in connection with the recent patriotic
demonstrations pressure had been brought to bear on the
schoolteachers. He wished to give this a public denial.
Some teachers in the country had written to the depart-
ment, asking that subscriptions to the Patriotic Fund
should be kept out of their cheques. This, however, had
not been done, the teachers being left to act in precisely
the same way as the general public. He knew that some
of them ooal 1 hardly afford donations.
And The Bulletin replies flatly that most in-
decent pressure was brought to bear, and teachers
were not left to act in the same way as the general
public, and that Minister Perry’s late and lone
denial is mere waste of good oxygen. The teachers
were circularised by some official of Mr. Perry’s
department, on official notepaper, asking them
what they would give to the Boer Suppression
Funds, and suggesting a minimum of two guineas
(the general public wasn’t stuck-up by its boss or
deputy-boss with the question, “Shall I deduct
£1 or £2 from your wages for this movement ?”)
They took this to mean that the Department
wanted, and expected, them to subscribe, and
a great many of them subscribed because they
thought it was wise, in view of future pro-
motion, &c., to please the Department. Minister
Perry knew, as everybody else knew, that
somebody was using the departmental influence
and the (well or ill grounded) fear of possible de-
partmental displeasure to drag subscriptions from
an ill-paid class of men. He didn’t stop the use
of the departmental influence. He didn’t tell the
teachers plainly and publicly that subscribing or
not subscribing would make no difference in their
future prospects. He simply allowed his under-
lings to use the departmental influence for the
purpose of exacting large sums of money, and
after it was exacted with his full knowledge, and
with the full knowledge of everybody else, he has
the ghastly assurance to deny the whole business.
The Bulletin has reserved many letters —at least
40—from teachers complaining of this blackmail.
Unfortunately they can’t be produced, for every
individual writer requested that his name should
be kept secret lest the knowledge that he objected
to the more-or-less-compulsory levy on his scanty
earnings should damage his future prospects. The
Public Instruction Department subscribed about
one-twelfth of the entire N.S.W. Patriotic Fund.
It subscribed, in proportion to its numbers, about
20 or more times as Ynuch as the general public. It
subscribed enormously more, per head, than the
rest of the service. And the visible reason was
that large sums of money were piratically de-
manded off it by an officer of Mr. Perry’s depart-
ment whether it could afford them or not, that it
paid up because it was led to believe from the
nature of the demand that it would damage its
prospects in Mr. Perry’s department if it didn’t
pay, and that Mr. Perry (unless he was blind and
unable to read and buried in thought) knew then
and knows now that these things were being done.
South Australia has as yet no factory laws worth
the name, for when Kingston tried to introduce
legislation on Victorian lines, the measure was
wrecked through Tory obstruction. Labor-leaders
who mingle with the workers have long complained
that, as a result of legislative neglect, Adelaide is
the worst hotbed of sweating m Australia; in
fact, work is done in the holy city for Melbourne
and Sydney merchants. “Quite recently,” says
an official report, “a line of cheap material was
received in Adelaide from a Sydney firm to be
made up into garments and returned.” The Ade-
laide serf works so cheaply that, even where rail-
carriage has to be paid, she can undersell her
Sydney sister! The Adelaide worker makes
up tweed trousers at from fid. to Is. ; shirts,
lfd. to 3|d. ; nightdresses, 4d. ; men’s coats,
Is. 9d. each, &c., &c. In the case of putting
up goods for Assyrian and Chinese hawkers the
work is done more cheaply still! Premier Holder
last week introduced a comprehensive Bill on
Maoriland lines providing for regulation of prices,
registration of building's and provision of a mini-
mum wage. A majority of the large employers de-
clare that they are in favor of any legislation which
will improve matters but their representatives in
the Tory Leg. Council maintain a different attitude
and the chances of amending legislation being
obtained are far from favorable.

Brisbane Telegraph on the alleged slump of
Jingoism up North :
The treatment extended to Queensland volunteers, who
have just been invalided home, is a melancholy commen-
tary upon the fickleness of public favor. ... We sent
them away with mingled tears and pride ; we bade them
show the world the mettle of their pasture, and assured
them of the glory that awaited their return. They have
returned, only to find that, in very truth, the paths of
glory lead but to the grave—especially the grave of cold
neglect and national ingratitude. That each one comes
not back covered with dazzling distinctions is not his own
fault. . . . Had they done doughty deeds of which the
world rang, there is little question that their return
would have been the signal for a demonstra-
tion. But because they are simple, plain, gallant
soldiers, who have been so unfortunate as to have
been hidden amid the battle’s cloud, and to have
come out unfit for further service, they are
treated with the scant courtesy of mendicancy. . . .
How plaintively, in moments of national stress, is the
patriotism of our people appealed to, and how bitterly we
complain if the appeal be in vain. Is there any wonder
that men think twice before answering the call, with such
instances as this to remind them of the possible reward ?
There are hundreds, nay, thousands, of men whose high
intelligence, if it could be actively enlisted in the service
of the State, would be of inestimable service. But they
shrink from the work, knowing full well that ingratitude
is at the base of all popular frenzy. And if this same
feeling had restrained those who now come back to us
maimed and weary, they would have been spared a bitter
awakening.

In other words, Parkes and Dibbs misspent 18

about 38 per cent. The Bulletin has been
calling attention to this for four years. The
Telegraph kept a dead silence while all this poli-

when it is too late. And the fact, now revealed,
that it is quite capable of understanding how in-
famously the public funds were being misapplied
makes its previous silence look the result, not of
ignorance, but rather of a deliberate intention to
keep in office, at the country’s loss, the most
ruinous and disastrous financier it ever had, so long
as he served the Calico Jimmy interest.
A number of newspaper proprietors and pro-
prietors’ representatives—with here and there a
journalist—met in Sydney the other day to advo-
cate reform of N.S.W. libel law. The most prac-
tical speech was made by John Norton, who
urged that a newspaper commentator was entitled
to just as much privilege as a man in the street,
and no more; and said, in effect, that what was
wanted was to clear away the tangle of legal rules
and pleadings which prevent a libel case from
getting to a jury on a fair issue. Other speakers
wanted a Judge-in-Chambers to certify that a
libel-plaintiff has ground for action before he is
permitted to sue. The Bulletin fails to see the
utility of this. If a plaintiff has the merest shred
of a case, the Judge will be bound to give him
access to justice; and, permission once given,
defendant will come before the jury with the
Judge’s imprimatur against him, as if he had been
formally committed for trial. Besides, a little
man who thinks he has been libelled by a big
paper ought not to be estopped by any
Judge from testing his grievances before a
jury. What is most wanted is the absolute
protection of honest reports of public meetings,
and of honest comments on public men and public
affairs. In other cases, honest comment should
be protected from vindictive damages; but a
newspaper which injures a man’s character, even
inadvertently, is as much bound to pay some
damages as if its proprietor had inadvertently
knocked a man’s eye out. A defendant should be
allowed to put his case to the jury in his own way,
instead of being embarrassed by triangular
pleadings. And the Queensland Defamation Act,
drafted by S. W. Griffith, is on all these points a
model for imitation.

N.S.W. ex-Premier Reid (in the Catholic Press
of 7th July) :
I am not at all attracted by. the Premier’s last proposal
to send volunteers to China. I don’t want to see colonial
volunteers figuring in all the imperial wars. ... I have
no desire to see a military spirit in Australia except for
purposes of self-defence. . . . The difference between
sending troops to share the South African campaign and
despatching troops to China is, to me, simply the differ-
ence between the son coming to the assistance of his
parent in a time of difficulty, and a desire on the son’s
part to meddle in the old man’s affairs as a regular thing.
The latter excites nothing of the sort of chivalry that the
former does.
Curious how that pitiably thin excuse keep crop-
ping up. When Britain had only one war on
hand, with a nation whose whole population is
one-third that of Sydney, and her troops on the
spot already outnumbered the enemy two to one,
and there were enough on the way to outnumber
him six to one, and temporary defeat only meant
the capture of a few soldiers who received kind
treatment from a decent white enemy—then it was
a ease of helping the aged parent in a time of
difficulty. And when the ancient one has
three wars on hand at once, and the odds
at the seat of war are stupendously against
her, and it is a case of saving British
subjects from falling into the hands of an
enemy who butchers his prisoners in all manner of
horrible ways, and when most of the other Powers
who are bearing a hand in the war are more than
strongly suspected of an intention of taking ad-
vantage of Britain, Australia, shouldn’t make
itself ridiculous by meddling in the old ’un’s affairs.
The contrast is so sharp that outside people will
begin to say that Australian politicians and Aus-
tralia’s Jingo papers have much horse-sense;

Donald Macdonald —Victorian Tour.
Sydney Lyceum, which has been shut up
a good deal of late, is suffering a temporary open-
ing under J. C. Williamson’s management. The
show is described as “ The Anglo-American Bio-
Tableau : with Roberts to Pretoria.” It consists
of the same kinematographe views which have
already been seen by instalments at Her Majesty’s,
and some new ones. It never gets to Pretoria —
in fact, a large percentage never gets anywhere
near there. A good part of the time and space is
occupied by the same old visit of the Queen
to Dublin; also the same much-respected
lady inspecting troops; also her stout and less-
respected son inspecting more troops; also more
troops marching down the street; also photo-
graphs of three cheers given by the troops when
they were inspected. The alleged pictures of
real war are excellent, but they don’t inspire
respect. The kinematographe of a body of
absent-minded British troops, making their final
stand against a horde of savages and being
killed to the last beggar, is too wild a joke.
The picture makes it evident that the expedition
carried its own photographer with it —evidently
knowing it was going to be wiped out ; and he
stood just where he would be shot at by both
parties, taking photographs like a Briton ; and
the British, though there was cover at hand,
fought it out in the open so as to make a good
group. The savages evidently rushed in swarms
right over the photographer when they closed
with their failing enemies, and then walked over
him as they returned from the scene of gore, unless
he fled in a cab in the interval. Apparently
the S’African trouble is the first war in which
4000 photographers accompanied the army,
at a cost of nearly £2,000,000 in wages and
£2,000,000 more in apparatus, so that one would
be always there when anything happened.
The view of General French’s cavalry skirmish
has only one drawback—there are no Boers in
sight to skirmish with, so it is probably a friendly
skurry at an autumn manoeuvre ; and though the
firing of the disappearing gun is real enough it is
fired at no visible Boer—probably it is. a dis-
appearing gun in England. Many of the pictures
are very beautiful and well worth seeing, but apart
from sundry views of troops marching through
Capetown (though it may just as easily be Bir-
mingham), there is nothing to connect them with
the war. The Bulletin, for one, refuses to accept
the theory that the photographer was spared when
all the other Britons were wiped out, especially
as the victorious savages come and prance round
the dead, and a man who was standing less than
a dozen yards off with a camera could hardly have
escaped observation. Even though he hung a
white flag above the negative it couldn’t save him
if he was there —the brutal savage would have
disembowelled his camera on the spot, and put
two large black feet through his film, and thrown
his dry plate over the Drakensberg, or else over
the public-house on to the Thames Embankment.
* ♦ * •
Even the departure of Colonel Plumer’s
irregulars (mostly on foot) to the relief of Mafe-
king, though a perfectly peaceful picture, would
be better if supported by affidavits. The idea that
a man with the necessary apparatus took the
trouble to dodge the lions in the far African
interior, and to defend himself from the sandfly
and the hippopotamus with his tripod, when he
could photo, the alleged departure quite as easily
with some supers on a country road anywhere,
and run no risk of crocodiles as he fled back to
civilisation with his life in one hand and his
undeveloped film in the other, looks just thin
enough to arouse suspicion. A certificate from
Plunder to the effect that he saw the instrument
on the roadside as he went by is much wanted.
The best part of the show is where it ceases to seek
its photo at the cannon’s month, and to kodak the
infuriated savage while he rushes at the defence-
less operator, and goes in for fun pure and simple.
In between times, also, Albert YVhelan, the
cleverest mimic in Australia, 'gives a pleasant
entertainment of his own, and W. B. Beattie lifts
up his voice in some more or less weather-beaten
patriotic melodies. The photo of Baden-Powell,
too, appears to be above suspicion. The well-
known and much-slept-in hat (evidently the hero’s
only hat) is there as a squashed and silent evidence
of good faith.
** * *
The “Rose of Persia” is definitely an-
nounced for the 21st at Her Majesty’s (Sydney),
so presumably Lauri feels himself equal to making
“Old Guard ” a live opera till then. Miss Win-
ston-Weir has possibly improved a trifle further
since the. last time this paper did its little moan
over her efforts, but she hasn’t improved suffi-
ciently to lift any of the heat and burden of the
opera off Lauri’s shoulders. Lauri, by the way, is
something more than a mere operatic character ;
he is also a large moral lesson on the decay of
municipal institutions. Mayors were respected
once. Now the stage mayor
is always a clown and a joke,
and is either thin and ignor-
ant or fat and foolish. No
play-writer even professes to
take a mayor in a serious
spirit. And until the stage
becomes more respectful to
mayors, that class of men
will still call vainly on Al-
derman Norton to keep order
and not call Alderman Gra-
ham bad names and Billings-
gate.
