No change is mentioned
in the Criterion bills, so it
seems probable that Dam-
pier finds “His Natural
Life ” profitable enough to
last another week, anyhow.
Also, “Merchant of Venice"
is to be repeated on Friday.
Shylock has already twice
sharpened his knife on his
boot in the damp, old Italian
city, and the Sydpey publio
evidently desires to see him
do it again.
* * *
“The Absent-Minded
Beggar,” still another Trans-
vaal war drama, will unfold
its vasty length at Sydney
Royal on Saturday. If Kru-
ger is in it The Bulletin’s
hired grumbler begs to ex-
press a hope that he will be
a larger Kruger than Ned
Holloway, who recently
labored in the massive coat
and big inky hat of the voor-
trekker at the Criterion.
Holloway was certainly a
good Kruger in all save his
inches, but a bigger op-
pressor would have advan-
tages. Whether Bland Holt
is doing well in putting on
this drama at a time when the
Beggar has become unsale-
able at the music-shops, is a matter to be settled
between him and the takings. There are strange
signs of forgetfulness in the air just now. On
Monday night, at the fateful hour of 8.40 p.m.,
the Lyceum orchestra played a bit of the National
Anthem, and not a soul stood up. They had for-
gotten the Anthem.
** * *
To-night (Thursday) the Lestonian Club
gives a performance of “Our Regiment”, at
Sydney Palace, in aid of the Indian
Famine Fund. As some 50,000,000 of our brown
fellow-creatures are now counting their ribs in a
far, dry land, it is to be feared that the
Palace, which is only about big enough to make
a convenient hat-box for Kruger’s big tile, will not
hold enough money to make much difference in
the mass. But it can at least hold enough to
make the ribs of 100 brown fellow-subjects less
apparent, so the public is urged to roll up. Even
to obscure the bones of one fellow-man with a thin
disguise of meat is a noble act which may not be
forgotten at the sounding of the last trump.
** * *
The new features of the House of the
Nasty Curtain, otherwise Sydney Tivoli, are
another infant performer named Baby Capel, and
three ’Murkan performers billed as Emmonds,
Emmerson and Emmonds. Which is which this
writer does not know, but there are two gentlemen
and one lady. The new baby is a flaxen-haired
little girl, who sings and dances in a blue
plush knicker suit, with a mixture of the en-
thusiasm of the child and the stage-ease of
the ancient of first nights. Emmonds, Em-
merson and Emmonds are, in some respects,
about the funniest trio that Rickards has put on.
A certain complicated dog joke which greatly
pleases the audience is not new, but the breezy
way the trinity work it off makes it as good as if
it was. For the rest. Jack Williams lifts up a
pleasant, black, split-turnip sort of face, and
Almond and Seel and.lrene Franklin and the long
M‘Clain make up a stronger company than usual.
** * *
On another page will be found a portrait
of Miss Kate Rooney, the N.S.W. Dubbo-born
contralto who is about to depart for Paris and
other places, where fame and great men’s praise
mean showers of gold. As Miss Rooney has in
the past given her melody freely at other people’s
benefits it is only right that the big Centennial
Hall should be full to the doors on the night of
Wednesday, July 18th., when the tuneful song-
stress’s own benefit comes off.
** * *
“ Camille,” the veteran Parisian love tale
—consumptive on one side, hysterical on the other,
and honestly improper all along the line—broke
out at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) on Saturday, and
will remain there for another week. In the long
rdle of Camille, the tired courtezan, Miss O’Neil
is much less pronouncedly Nance than in other
characters. Some points of her performance have
a fine surface, and none of it sounds loud. Pre-
sent writer would say that the chief merit of the
O’Neil Camille is in the large lady’s well-sustained
air of submission and ill-health. She is Magda
with a bruised spirit and a gentle cold in the
head which settles on the lungs and proves fatal.
The hardness and loudness of Magda caused some
irritation to people who couldn’t get persuaded
that Nance’s heart was beating within the charac-
ter. Now, the meekness and the sympathetic
snuffle of Camille will be less irritating, yet more
wearisome, to the same people. Dumas’ play was
not highly entertaining when Sarah Bernhardt
realised the name-part, or when Mrs. Potter de-
corated its sick sentimentality with pretty, fantas-
THE JANDASCHEWSKYS, Russian Pantomimists and
Dancers, in Fitzgerald Bros.' New European Company.
tic furbelows of art. The present production is
rather tiresome, outside of the gaudy salon
scenes, and the dullness—let there be no
mincing of the truth—is due to the stage
management. Somehow or other Miss Nance’s
face is kept much out of evidence. Here, as in
“Magda,” the broad back of the central figure is
turned towards the audience at times when her
countenance ought to be boldly mirroring her
emotions. Also her hand shades her eyes with a
distracting glitter of diamond rings. This is
called original genius by some of the newspapers.
* * * *
The second half of the third act was made
tedious by the dim lighting and the persistent con-
cealing of Nance’s face from view. Her death-
scene, supposed to happen on a bright morning, is
also rather unduly dim, but the device in this
instance is pardonable. All things considered,
the death of massive Miss Nance is an artistic
success. She attempts nothing that she can’t ac-
complish, and she contrives to look fully 4st.
thinner than the lady who had formed a black-
velvet heap on the floor of the previous act.
* * * *
Kingston’s Armand is a spirited creature
in the scene where he pelts Camille with money.
But every Armand is spirited at this stage of his
hysteria. The most spirited edition of him that
the present writer can remember was Bill Hollo-
way. To see William throw the tin sequins and
bogus bank-notes about was a Treat. Old Daniel
is too stagey and stilted, and some of the other
parts are poorly played, but the show as a whole
is improved by dressing the characters in old-time
finery. Sarah Bernhardt treated Camille as a
modern sinner, and her lover had to appear as an
ordinary, up-to-date young man, which was all
right for Sarah, but very rough on the young
man.

She was said to come from Paris, but the advt.
was probably a wicked libel on that gay city. At
the Town Hall the silvery voice of Charles Clark
spake good-bye to Melbourne for the positively last
time on Saturday night.
*# * *
Apologists for Nance O’Neil’s death scene
in “Elizabeth” will not be able to persuade old
stagers that the darkness which almost hides the
actress from view is an “artistic effect.” The
suggestion that the dramatist intended the heroine
to be a heap of mystery at the finish looks none
too reasonable. It is hard to believe that any
author would sacrifice the opportunity to give the
leading lady a great acting chance. Anyhow, the
author of “ Elizabeth ” was not such a fool. This
is a somewhat varied version of the Italian play
staged by the Majeronis years ago, and no Bulletin
reader who remembers Signora Majeroni’s picture of
the wrecky old Queen will need to be told that
she died with a strong light on her face.
Present writer never saw Ristori (for whom the
play is said to have been written), but will bet
that she didn’t die in darkness. Another apolo-
gist for Nance says “she can’t die, she is too
virile.” Truly an inane excuse to offer to a world
where Sarah Bernhardt has been the champion
death-scene actress for the last 30 years, or more.
Bernhardt’s virility islthe marvel of the dramatic
age. She takes food enough for five women, and
is ever on the go every day of her life, and at night
she gives up the ghost with awful realism. Nance
O’Neil too virile to die ! What, in the name of
Sarah, do the massive lady’s puffsters take us for?
** * *
The first week’s business at Rickards’ new
Tivoli (nee Bijou) Theatre, Adelaide, was very
promising. The City of Churches is a small place
to support an all-the-year-round variety show,
but probably supply will tend to increase de-
mand, and if the house does little more than pay
expenses it will serve Rickards’ purpose in Sydney
and Melbourne by enabling him to keep a larger
force of artists on hand. By the way, The Bul-
letin is sorry to hear that the energetic Harry is
at present very ill.
* # * SK-
“ Manoeuvres) of Jane,” at Melbourne
Princess’s, is the show that made a very successful
Sydney appearance during Plague-time, and as
there is no Plague to contend with in this case,
the new comedy will probably boom. It is a
comedy of things that might happen under the
circumstances. Author H. A. Jones seems to
have planted an interesting assortment of charac-
ters in good, light soil, where they took root
naturally and brought forth a crop of jokes. Ex-
cellent jokes some of them. The fiendish Pamela,
aged 15 or thereabouts, with her neck turned
awry by chronic listening at the keyhole, is an
appallingly comic truth. Brough’s Lord Bap-
child, a flabby lambkin of ancient lineage, en-
feebled by the moral training of an anxious
mother at a time when he ought to have been
plunged into sin and wickedness for his health’s
sake, is a nobleman who fills a long-felt want.
Ever since Lord Tween ways (of “ The Amazons”)
took a noble family-pride in his physical ailments
we have waited for Bapchild.
* * • *
Bapchild is such a spineless landowner
and pig-fancier that his friends decide to marry
him to Jane Nangle, the turbulent daughter of a
baldheaded capitalist. She seems a suitable tonic
for the Bapchild pedigree. So they bring the
fiery, unwilling Jane along to the manor-house,
and there she discovers her own best boy in Bap-
child’s new steward. And when Pamela, by the
way, discovers the happy pair locked in a long
embrace, Jane pounces on the horrid interloper
and shakes her till the audience roars aloud.
Subsequently Jane makes a mis-elopement with
the steward whilst her friend Constantia is
luring Bapchild to his doom in a rowing-boat.
Constantia contrives to throw her maiden Reputa-
tion on the mercy of that harmless creature. She
lets down her back hair in the back parlor where
they have come in out of the wet, and she hangs
around her victim’s neck so pointedly that he
talks of marriage and is immediately lost. The
relatives who had prescribed Jane for the Bap-
child complaint come to the conclusion that Con-
stantia will do nearly as well, after which the
comedy comes to a conclusion. It stops hurriedly.
• * * *
The company effects a considerable shine
all round in this cheerful four-act narrative.
Brough has done nothing better than Bapchild.
Now and then he may exaggerate the humor of the
sketch in the shading, but the outline is all right.
The creepy young Pamela that Miss Evelyn Mar-
theze has created is another excellent bit of
character work, likewise the little picture of an
elderly spinster by Miss Gillies Brown. Miss
Noble, as Jane, might have been specially imported
for the part, if she hadn’t been here already.
Miss Bessie Thompson is a convincing exponent
of the cold, steely, grey-eyed type of lady, and
Mrs. Brough is, as a matter of course, a satisfac-
tory Constantia. But she is not more than satis-
factory. W. T. Lovell, as a colorless lover, is
rather less than more, whilst Reginald Dartrey,
Mario Majeroni and other people are considerably
more than less. And the comedy is staged in the
best Broughian manner.

All the mainland provinces reached the end of
their financial year on 30th June, while Tasmania is half-
way through. The revenues for the year are known with
reasonable certainty, though a few adjustments have yet
to be made, which will alter the totals in some small
degree. The expenditure won’t be definitely and Certainly
known for some weeks yet, but each province has issued
a more-or-less reliable guess at the result, and things are
somewhat as follows :

The New South Wales deficit is on the actual revenue and
expenditure of the year, plagues and contingents thrown
in. The accounts would have balanced but for the con-
tingents. But the Finance Committee, now sitting, has
been instructed to decide how the much faked and
garbled public ledger should be kept to put it on an
honest basis, and with a view to doing this it will pro-
bably be found necessary to charge the year just closed
with so much of the expenditure which George Reid
should have provided for, but didn’t, as. to raise the
shortage to something near a million. That extra short-
age, of course, is a thing for which Lyne isn’t responsible.
It is Reid’s responsibility and the country’s.

The carelessness of certain steam-users in not
looking after their boilers and the results of neglecting to
clean them regularly when incrustation takes place has
often been pointed out in The Bulletin. A disencrustat-
ing boiler compound known as “ Litofuge,” with a world-
wide reputation, is being placed upon the market by W.
and J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins-st., Melbourne, who
upon application will be pleased to forward catalogues to
those interested. “Litofuge” compounds are powders
without soda, fatty matter or injurious chemicals, which
injure rather than improve boilers. Its action is mechani-
cal and its introduction into a boiler causes no foaming.*

In N.S.W. and in most of the other Australias
there is no law whatever to ensure the solvency of life-
assurance offices. Any concern from, or alleged to be
from, any place may hang out its shingle in these parts,
and promise anything, and scoop in premiums on the
strength of these promises. It isn’t required to show
that it is solvent before being allowed to start business, or
to give any kind of security whatever. Apropos of
which, tHh Independent Order of Foresters, of Toronto,
Canada, has commenced life business in Sydney. The
promises of this institution are pretty large. A man
aged 30 can get from the A.M.P. Society a policy for
£2OO payable at death, with profits, for a quarterly
premium of £1 4s. Bd. The 1.0. F. issues the printed
statement that it will insure a man, aged 30, for £2OO
payable at death, with profits, for a quarterly premium of
18s. and certain “ court-dues,” which make the total
about £1 Is. 9d. And in addition to this remarkable cheap-
ness, it promises to give, without extra charge, these
extra benefits: (1) No premiums payable after age 70;
(2) If the assured becomes totally and permanently dis-
abled at any age, his premiums cease, while the policy
remains in full force ; (3) The assured can draw one-half
the amount of the policy at any age should he become
totally and permanently disabled ; (4) He can draw the
whole amount of his policy by instalments when disabled
by old age.
And The Bulletin has a suspicion that this
wonderfully and gorgeously promising concern is in a
rocky state through promising too much. It is
difficult to say at this distance, but there are things
about it that look very rocky. It claims to have been
working in a large way of life assurance for 10 years, and
in a smaller way for years before that. Yet, in
1898, while the premiums received in its life department
were £357,088, the accumulated fund was only
£617,161, or much less than two years’ premiums.
A very young Australian office, the Oity Mutual,
has about five years’ premiums in hand, yet it
has far fewer liabilities to meet in proportion to the
amount it receives in premiums. It is about the same
age as the Independent Order of Foresters’ concern, but
there is a great discrepancy in their resources. A little
earlier the 1.0. F. had (according to the Insurance
Blue Book) insurances in force for over £32,000,000,
and only £518,724 assurance fund on hand, or
one-sixty-third. The Oity Mutual had £1,068,000 of
assurances in force and £165,000 assurance fund, or one-
seventh. The 1.0. F. claims to have had, at the end of ’9B, a
surplus of £654,734, but its accounts show no such sur-
plus. It certainly seems to have had total funds of about
that amount, but total funds are not surplus.
The 1.0. F. publishes some curious tables. It
estimates that its accumulated funds will yield 4 per
cent, interest, and it publishes a table showing that its
premiums (after deducting 5 per cent, for expenses), if
accumulated at 4 per cent, compound interest till death
or till age 70, will amount to enough to pay its policies,
with so small a margin that bonuses are almost impos-
sible. But it doesn’t average 4 per cent, interest; it earned
that amount in 1898 with a very small margin, but
over a term of years it only averaged £3 2s. 7d. This is
calculated to knock its solvency endways. All decent
offices estimate their interest earnings a good way below
the rate actually received—not above it or just at it. Its
solvency also depends on its expenses being only 5 per
cent, on premiums. In 1894 they were about 9 per cent.
In 1898 (the writer has only these two balance-sheets by
him) “ expenses ” were certainly only a shade over 5 per
cent., but “ contingencies ” were about 5J per cent., mak-
ing 10J per.cent, in all. This also knocks endways a
calculation whose solvency depends on the real expenses
being kept down to 5 per cent. In fact, the real expenses
maybe 20 or 30 or 40 per cent., but the balance comes
out of that levy on the members called “court dues.”
However, the present point is that the 1.0. F. shows that its
solvency depends on it averaging 4 per cent, interest
on its funds and spending only 5 per cent, of its
premiums in expenses—and it spends more than
double that in expenses and contingencies, and doesn't
earn 4 per cent, on an average. Then its tables
make, apparently, no allowance for the extra benefits it
promises ; they seem to merely show (or fail to show) that
it is solvent as an ordinary life office, the old-age pensions,
&c., not being mentioned. And finally it publishes a
table showing, on the evidence of eminent actuaries, the
lowest premium at which an assurance of £2OO can be
safely granted at various ages. The 1.0.F.’s rates are
lower than these alleged lowest safe rates.
There is no evidence in any publication that The
Bulletin has in hand that the 1.0. F. has ever paid any
bonuses. The Insurance Blue Book for 1900 doesn’t
credit it with any surplus—doesn’t even say that it has
enough assets to cover the present value of its liabilities.
Its policies are “ with profits,” but nothing can be dis-
covered about there ever having been any profits. It re-
serves the right to increase the premiums , if need be, so
the rates it mentions may grow quite suddenly and
alarmingly. The levy called “court dues” makes the
premiums really heavier than they look. If the 1.0. F.
increases the premiums too much and you drop out, you
get no surrender value whatever. Altogether The Bulletin
reckons that, allowing for that extra tax called “ court
dues” which are not mentioned in the premiums, and
considering the absence of surrender values, the apparent
absence of bonuses, the risk of having the premiums in-
creased indefinitely, the 5 per cent, expenses which seem
to be reallylOJ not reckoning the “dues” which make them
much more, the 4 per cent, assumed interest rate which
is so often not realised, and the joyful position of an
office with more than half as much life assurance in force
as the A.M.P. and one-twentieth the cash to pay
it with when the members die—considering these things,
it isn’t going to insure its life in the 1.0. F. But it is
going to barrack more loudly than ever for a rigid life
assurance law, State audit, and valuation of securities,
and State standard of solvency.
The 1.0. F. of Canada, by-the-way, issues com-
parisons between itself and the A.M.P. The Bulletin
has also been making comparisons. Allowing for “ con-
tingencies” and “court-dues” its expenses appear to be
vastly higher than the A.M.P.’s, which means less profit
for policy-holders. It earns a much smaller rate of
interest on its invested funds, which also means less
profit for policy-holders. It works mostly in countries
that are less healthy than Australasia. Therefore it is
certain that it can’t give as good value for every £ paid
to it as the A.M.P. does. If it doesn’t economise by
working in healthier countries (and there are none), or by
paying away less in expenses, or earning more in interest,
it can only cut its rates lower by giving less bonuses and
supplying less security.
Geelong (Vio.) follows the example of other
places by starting a Tontine Insurance scheme—the “ Gee-
long and Western District 00-operative Life Assurance
Trust.” The advt. doesn’t state the size of the Tontine,
but otherwise it is built on much the usual plan. Each
member is to insure himself (or, if he prefers it, somebody
else) on a 20-year limited payment plan with the National
Mutual office. Policies are to be made over to trustees.
If a member, or a member’s nominee, dies during the 20
years, the National Mutual pays the trustees of the Ton-
tine the face-value of his policy, and returns to the repre-
sentatives of the deceased all his premiums, with 3 per
cent, compound interest. Therefore, the deceased’s re-
presentatives lose nothing, while the Tontine Fund gains
considerably. At the end of 20 years each surviving
member receives back his own policy, then fully paid
up; also, his share in the Fund accumulated through
the deaths of his lamented fellow - members, and if
the latter are only reasonably diseased the survivors may
have a great time. In such a case there are only two
essentials ; the society which issues the policies should be
above suspicion as to its solvency and capacity for endur-
ance, and the trustees who hold the policies and the cash
should be ditto. In this case there is no objection to
make as regards the society, but the proposal that two
local citizens should act as trustees is most objectionable.
A good trustee co., with a huge capital as a guarantee
against loss, is the only fitting institution to hold the
cash when a transaction is to last 20 years. Personal
trustees often die, or make bad investments and can’t
make the loss good, or have a trusted clerk who absconds
with the money. If anyone would make out a list of
50 unimpeachably solvent and reliable nten in Sydney
or Melbourne 20 years ago, and then consider the awful
things that have happened to them since, he would be
simply astounded.

Haymarket Permanent Land, Building, and
Investment 00. (Sydney), which is now fast recovering
from a severe illness, shows recent results as follows:
Profits. Dividend.

November, 1897 .. 160 Nil.
May, 1898 .. ..223 Nil.
November, 1898 .. 254 Nil.
May, 1899 .. ... 496 Nil.
November, 1899 .. 905 3 per cent, per annum, £lO9O
May, 1900 .. .. 1116 3 per cent, per annum, 1111
During the past six months deposits have been reduced
by £lB9O (from £54,827 to £52,937) ; “ other liabilities ”
have been reduced by £603 (from £41,553 to £40,950) ;
and the co.’s bank account has improved by £4333, the
old overdraft being completely wiped off. The co.
has £74,077 paid-up capital and £40,927 reserves (re-
serve fund £33,148; contingent fund, £1500; profit
balance, £6279), and if its assets are worth their full
paper value its position is very strong. As the gross
income, including commission, fines, and sundries, is only
£3 14s. Bd. per cent, per annum on the investments, it is
doubtful if they are worth their full paper value just at
present, and some doubt is cast on the beautiful
reserve ; still, the previous half-year only showed a
return of £3 4s. lid. per cent., so things are distinctly
improving. At the same time, the policy of paying away
all the profits except about £5 in dividends looks decidedly
premature.

The New York and Brooklyn Tobacco Co.
(Sydney) publishes a rather good-looking interim report
for the half-year to 30th June, 1900. The balance-sheets
of this concern aren’t so luminous as they might be. For
instance, the 1898 balance-sheet showed that the profits
were £2603, but next one revealed that this didn’t pro-
vide for income-tax and some other expenses which were
charged in 1899, and this somewhat changed the appear-
ance of things. Still, going by the co.’s own method,
results are as follows
Profit. Dividend.
1898 (13 months) .. £2,603 10 per cent.—£l,62s
1899 (12 months) .. 1,401 10 per cent.— 1,500
1900 (6 months) .. 906 10 per cent. — 750
There is a balance of £1035 after paying the dividend.
The co. hopes at the end of the year to have £l2OO left
after paying dividend, and speaks of it as available
‘ either to write down certain assets or for reserve.” One
of the assets is “ Brands and goodwill, £2000,” and that
should be wholly written off before any attempt is made
at a reserve. A reserve which is only represented by
paper assets is not worth having, and “goodwill” is
always more or less papery-

The total reserves of the Australian Mortgage, Land and
Finance Co. have thus decreased in nine years by £228,586
(£275,000 written off, less £46,414 surplus of profits above
dividends), and now stand at £333,884. Despite this
decline the co. has got through some very bad times
with better success than most of the large grass-and-
sheep institutions. But £20,000 of the 1899 earnings and
£15,000 of the 1898 earnings were profit on the sale of
property—the sort of windfall which only comes very
occasionally ; and the earnings of the ordinary business'
were only £31,648 in 1898 and £41,586 in 1899. This
doesn’t look as if the glorious 20 per cent, dividend days
were coming back just yet. And the co. is loaded with
£2,000,000 owing on debentures which it should have
paid off out of profits in the good days, but didn’t.

The Maoriland dredging-boom still rages; the>
latest sensation being a one-week’s return for one dredge
of over 12300 z. But N.S.W. dredging is not making the
headway the public have been led to believe. More
sellers than buyers, and, in the majority of cases, no
buyers at any price. In several instances, forfeited shares,
are advertised—in one, even at the third call. The Fede-
ral Co. (Braidwood district) is still making calls, although,
the dredge has been running for months. It is a poor'
look-out if calls do not cease when the dredge is going.
The last return from the Turon was only 250 z.,.
and that of the previous week but an oz.
or two more-or-less. Shares 305., probably an-
other joke. The public are assured that, notwithstanding-
the severity of the floods, little or no harm has been done-
to the dredges. Araluen Proprietary announces a yield
of 540 z. for two weeks’ run. The fact remains that the-
successful N.S.W. dredges will be a very small number
compared to those at work. Some will fail from want of’
gold in the wash, more will fail from inability to get it om
account of bad bottoms and other disabilities. N.S.W.
will, for many obvious reasons, never have a dredging-
boom such as that in Maoriland.

Cobar Gold-mines : N.S.W. shareholders have-
received news at last—a copy of an article appearing in
S.M. Herald last April giving a description of the mine
and plant in the colorless way so characteristic of that-
senile paper. This has gone to London, and havingbeenre-
Erinted, is now sent back to the shareholders, who have
eard nothing officially of the returns from the mine this
year, although the 100-head mill and cyanide plant have
been running continuously, crushing tens of thousands of
tons I Local shareholders have long grown tired of this
sort of business, and unless they receive the returns from
this mine at the same time as they are known in London,.
steps will probably be taken to enforce publicity. It
would be a very simple matter for the Govt, to make a
regulation insisting on the publication of returns, as is
done in Queensland, Victoria, and other places where
mining is conducted with some little regard to decency.

tons of rich ore to crush, at standstill for want of water.
Good prospecting and development work being done
on Gate and Banner Tine, but field badly needs rain.

Ivanhoe.—June production, 9i790z. Hitherto
part of the Ivanhoe’s gold production has come from
slimes and tailings which accumulated before it had a
cyaniding and filter-pressing plant. These are now almost
used up, and then the mine will have to depend on the
ore raising during the month. As this return is fully
IOOOoz. higher than foreshadowed by ex-manager Hewit-
son, no doubt it is considered “ very satisfactory,” and
that his sudden dismissal was “ quite warranted under
the circumstances.” What is likely to happen before the
end of the year—the certain result of this mine-gutting—
was sufficiently indicated in last week’s issue. So far
the cables (published) which brought about the manager’s,
dismissal only go to prove that he was discharged for
quite another reason. Remarkable, too, that Mackinnon,
who took over the mine from Hewitson, only remained a
month, leaving on the ground that he couldn’t
possibly manage it and Lake View as well. Writer
believes it was offered to another man on the
field who refused it because of the insult to Hewit-
son. It was then offered to Skews, formerly of Boulder
Main Reefs ; negotiations have fallen through, and the
manager from the Paddington Consols has gone there as a
stop-gap. ’Twould serve the London directors right if
they couldn’t get a man in Australia to manage the Ivan-
hoe"—which billet means being made a plaything with a
big salary.
• • * •
South Kalgurli, 1550 tons, for 15590 z. How
many people in Australia and elsewhere who bought
these shares at from £6 to over £7 each did so under the
impression that this mine would only average loz. to the
ton ? Probably not one. If newspaper pars, about it
were true, it should have gone at least 3oz. And there is
another phase. All the mines within the marvellous Mile
started with much higher average ore than that they are
now working. The Boulder, when it had a mill
of the capacity of the South Kalgurli’s, kept up
more than double its present average; so, too,
the Horseshoe, which started with 50z., and so also
the Perseverance, Lake View, Associated, Ivanhoe. Now
if the same thing happens to this mine—Lord help it!
Fortunately the sulphide zone, so far as proved, is
of much better grade than the oxidised ; the latter ores
are only worth from 10 to 12dwts. The sulphides will
average considerably over an oz., and it is because of the
large quantity of oxidised ore used in the mill that we get
this return. The mine has not enough roasting plant to
keep the mill running on the sulphide zone. The chief
ore bodies come in from the Perseverance and have not
been proved of any great length, nor are they as large as
in the other mine. At £5 55., the present price, the shares
are too high.
* * • •
Boulder Main Reef.—ll 77 tons for 1850ozs. All
sulphide ore. It gets at one end of the golden belt
what the South Kalgurli gets at the other— i.e., the tail
end of the lodes from the Great Boulder like the S.K. from
the Perseverance. It has 300 to 400 ft. of fairly good ground
—certainly not enough to warrant present price of shares,
345. It is running entirely on sulphides, and uses the Mt.
Morgan roasting furnace.

Oroya.—At last the three Huntingdon mills,
■which have been running for two years, have closed down.
The average has been falling ever since the start, but with
■each return it was given out that the average was more
than payahle, until the impression reigned locally that
the lower the grade the greater the pride of the manager.
But when it got down to 3dwt. the mills had to shut up.
Attention is to be confined to “ the opening up of the
■sulphide zones on the lease.” The mine has one little
■streak of payable sulphide from which it has taken many
months to get less than 500 tons of ore worth about
2oz. to the ton, the value of which will be ab-
sorbed in mining, shipping and treatment charges.
■Of course—“ a new sulphide plant is to go up immediate-
ly ” —one would think that it could be erected in a day
Instead of nearly a year. Re the present sulphide ship-
ment Sydney D.T. says it will “probably be the last
parcel of ore that will ever leave the mine.” Which may be v
true in a double sense. Shares have slumped to 325.,
they were once over £4 and were never worth half present
price.
* « « *
Great Boulder: Last year’s report shows a net
revenue of £213,436 —4 divs. of 6d. each, represent 100 per
■cent, on the par value of the shares, leaving a balance of
£86,000. The manager succeeded in maintaining the
regularity of the output though the mine is passing
through a transition stage pending the installation
■of the sulphide plant. The oxidised ores are
almost depleted. A few thousand tons is neither
here nor there with such a mine as the G.B. Sulphide
reserves blocked out ready for stoping have been in-
creased by 77,348 tons, and there are already over 20,000
tons on surface awaiting the completion of the plant.
This plant should materially increase the gold produc-
tion ; it will need to, for four 6d.-divs. a year is not
■enough interest to get on a 335. share (present price).
But there is a much better outlook. Writer anticipates the
■success of the sulphide plant; if so, it should put through
■6OOO tons a month for a return of, say, 9000 oz. There are
20,000 odd tons to start on, and the mine seems ready to
maintain a 6000 tons monthly-production for years to
■come. There are upwards of 100,000 tons of tailings left-
worth, at any rate, half as many ozs. The Boulder may
be regarded as a safe 10 per cent, investment, and there
is always a chance of its being doubled by the duplication
of plant. Already the mine is not far off warranting it.
The anomaly of G.B. shares remaining in the region of
30s. when others like the Lake View and Ivanhoe went up
£3 each demonstrates that there is something wrong
somewhere.
Associated Gold Mines: .No returns yet an-
nounced for June. They ar» variously foreshadowed
between 3000 and 5000 oz. Chances are they will be under
3000. “ The shipping- of sulphide is to be resumed,” pro-
bably another lie. The mill is still running. The longer
it runs the longer it will be shut down ; it will shut itself
down when the ore is gone. This time last week the mine
had a market value of £2,000,000 ; to-day it is nearly
£400,000 less, and no newspaper seems to take any notice
of the difference. The only gleams of hope in the reports
of the three experts who examined the property have been
seized upon in London and Adelaide as showing that the
mine is not really so bad as was painted.
Victoria has just got a new goldfield. A big *
rush is now on to Mt. William, at the foot of the Gram-
pians, near Ararat; 1500 men are already on the field.
Sinking 2to 4ft. in very wet ground. Prospects good so
far, running from Ito 3dwt. to the dish. Provisions
already scarce, and every milk-dish has been bought in
Ararat stores. Trouble already arising with the local
landowners, some demanding as much as £lO a claim.
Ballarat and Melbourne speculators hastening thither.
Warden to be sent up this week.

Broken Hill Prop, having come to terms with
the Water Co., and having therefore no use for the pro-
posed Umberumberka Creek scheme, Govt, has sent up
officers to report on the suitableness of the site for the
purposes of a supply for town and mines. And
though the big B.H. Prop, is for the present satisfied,
some of the other strong co.’s, notably the Sulphide Cor-
poration, are not. So between the two the Water Co. is
not out of all danger.

Editor Bulletin.—l call attention to the con-
duct of the Woodlark G.M. Co. Some time ago they sent
one of their directors, who has had some experience, on a
visit to the mine, located on distant Woodlark Island,
Papua. Said director received a good fee and his expenses
for his report, which was sent in about a month ago. Up
to now this report, which is said to be favorable, has not
been published. I would like to know what the board
mean by this, considering that the report was paid for by
the shareholders ? This is not the way to encourage con-
fidence in the mine —or the board. Is the Woodlark
going to be added to the list—already too long—of pro-
mising mines sent to perdition by N.S.W. dunderheaded
“wire-pulling” management? The board’s present
conduct is in nice contrast to the authorities’ wavs when
the show was floated. Then they interviewed all and
sundry who could speak a word in favor of it, and pub-
lished the “ reports,” “ yarns,” &c., in double-quick time.
—Disgusted.

Premier Lyne’s proposal of a “ uniform
Australian medal ” for all Australians who have
been _ convicted of firing a gun with in-
tent in South Africa is just a petty provincial
proposal. A South African medal for “Our Boys ”
will come in an ordinary way from the Imperial
Govt., who provided the war for them. If Aus-
tralia particularly wishes to pay for a medal instead
of leaving the Imperial Govt, to supply the sou-
venir, all well and good. The cost would not be
alarming. But when it comes to a question of
two medals for the same campaign—why, the Eng-
lish, Scotch, and Irish regiments might as well
ask for a distinctive provincial bauble to hang on
their coats beside the one that is common to all.

will have a bad time, as the Chinese torture their
captives. When Sir Harry Parkes, Capt. (after-

* • « *
Apropos the spasmodic appearances of the
Plague, it might be mentioned that, in Bombay,
the disease seems to have utterly beaten the
doctors. In the epidemic of ’96 there were 28,737
deaths. Then it seemed to die out, with only
cases now and again. In ’97 it broke forth once
more, and before the end of the year had killed
another 61,220 persons. The epidemic of ’9B-’99
was the most deadly of all; 115,084 people are
known to have perished, while hundreds, if not
thousands, must have escaped observation owing
to the disorganisation resulting from the panic.
Men fell dead in the streets, were found dead in
by-ways, in public gardens—everywhere where a
man might go were to be found plague-corpses.
The medical staff reckoned that 70 per cent, of
the seizures were fatal, and has reported that, once
a district or quarter has become infected, the only
remedy is to utterly abandon it for some better
locality, and inoculate all healthy survivors with
the prophylactic.
• * • *
Up to 27th June there had been 54 cases
of plague in Queensland: —Rockhampton, 28;
Brisbane, 19; Townsville, 4 ; Cairns, 2 ; Ipswich,
1. Deaths at that date were 26 —Rockhampton,
16; Brisbane, 7; Townsville, 2; Cairns, 1.
The Plague demon-
strates some more the
strange fact that, not-
withstanding the mar-
vellous stamina and
powers of type-reproduc-
tion of the Mongol, hsi
individual vitality is low
and timid. Perhaps gene-
rations of fatalism, aided
by the sordid surround-
ings of a life that was not
worth living, that offered
scarcely a single attrac-
tion from birth to bier,
are responsible for this
want of resistance to
disease. The Chinaman,
especially of the lower
class, “gives in” on the
slightest provocation. He
will hang himself to
escape a flogging; he
will expire to escape the
struggle with an attack
of sickness. And yet, or
perhaps, therefore, he
flourishes —as a race.
Life, luck and woman
seem alike in that they
become the more fasti-
diously unmanageable
the more considerately
you treat them.

in a large vague way that
the overcrowding of the
Sydney electric trams
arises through Parlia-

the principles of modern
management. It doesn’t
say what these principles
of management are, pro-

know, but the expressson
is a large flapping gene-
rality that is comforting
to the soul. Yet, what
is really wanted is some
new streets. When Sus-

York, Pitt and Castle-
reagh streets all crook
their way into George-
street and leave it
practically the only
thoroughfare that tra-
verses the city, nothing
can prevent congestion. All the ancient and
modern management on earth can’t get two quarts
of people comfortably into a one-pint street.
* * * *
A small, unpromising outbreak of social-
gimletism has lately happened in Melb. under the
style of the Christian Citizens’ League. The
C.C.L. proposes to hand politics to pure and holy
men—probably the sort who clear out with the
choir-girl and the Sunday School funds—and to
look closely into the morals of every legislator
who doesn’t attend church regularly. At their
inaugural meeting these gruesome reformers de-
cided upon a course of spying on the police to see
they carried out the laws relating to liquor, the
expulsion of golden-haired women from every-
where, and the abolition of gambling in every
form. One hoary-headed person was loudly
cheered for the suggestion that betting on race-
courses should be a criminal offence for layer and
backer alike.

Dear B ulletin .
The moist adroit sweating in N.S.
Wales Civil Service is that prac-
tised upon unclassified and 3rd-
class teachers. Probationers (at
£72 p.a.) are always appointed to
provisional schools averaging over
16 in attendance— i.e., worth £BB
a-year ; whereas many teachers of
some years’ service struggle along
on the ,£72 owing to inability
(rarely their fault) to maintain
said average. Again, a classified
teacher in a school above his
classification is paid “ according
to classification ”; in a school
below same, according to “class
of school.” By this despicable
dodge are injured—A, who in a
school above his class is doing
work for which he isn’t paid, and
B; entitled to A’s school, who is
drumming away in a school below
his class, and is paid “according.”
Also, influence is still a power to con-
jure with, despite the loud headquarters’
protestations to the contrary.— Ped.
• » * *
In a mining-accident case recently tried
before new Tas. West Coast Stipendiary Crosby
Gilmore, the mine-manager, to lus utter astonish-
ment, was fined heavily for negligence, said negli-
gence being the sole cause of the disaster. The
Commissioner’s ruling was so strictly and obvi-
ously legal that there is nothing to be said on
that ground. But it is so long since the law was
enforced, and since an employer in Tasmania has
been held legally accountable for a maimed or
murdered workman, that Gilmore is now being
held up to public execration as a monster of
iniquity bent upon “driving capital out of the
country.” How many crimes and illegalities are
winked at in Australia for fear of (to speak Hiber-
nianly) driving the oppressive absentee out of the
country !

M. Wiegand, the departing Sydney City
organist, -takes with him for distribution in
Europe some hundreds of copies of Syd-
ney D.T., containing a verbatim report of
the extraordinary procedings of the Crty
Council meeting at which his application
for leave of absence was debated. Said
that the report is to be translated into
several European languages; and the
translations, together with copies of the
Wiegand’s diploma, testimonials, &c.,
alongside, will be printed in large type,
suitably framed, and exhibited in the
Belgian Court at Paris Exhibition, headed
by a card bearing, in flaming-red letters,
suitable remarks about the class of men
who may happen to an artist in Australia.

How the rat-hordes of Sydney must
grin at the hysterical shrieks of the home-
made plague-expert re the “good work
being accomplished in the way of exter-
mination.” A few weeks ago in one of
the most popular Sydney resorts not far
from the G.P.O. the proprietor exhibited
to some clients of his, in two large wire
cages (known as basket traps), 43 rats he
had caught in one night. The traps were
set in a very small area, into which the
rodents were enticed by kitchen offal
temporarily stored in boxes. What a
harvest that place should yield to a “ pro-
fessional” catcher !

the relatives well knowing that a summons will
issue and a fine follow. Now—who gets that fine ?

THE BREAD OF CHARITY.
SHE (to amorously-inclined
" johnny”): “My poor
man, you must he very
hungry, so I've bought you
a ban. But please don't
follow me about any more."
The fatuous indifference of the general
Eublic to the horrible diet-risks it habitually runs
as hitherto been reflected in the half-hearted
punishment meted out to vendors of diseased
meat, and the Maitland (N.S.W.) police-magis-
trate, therefore, has set a much-needed example
by inflicting upon the vendor of a diseased cow the
maximum fine of £2O. The law’s penalty, how-
ever, is after all an absurdly mild one. If the
vendor in question had killed a man, either wil-
fully or carelessly, while in the illegal pursuit of
gain, he could be cold-bloodedly hanged ; whereas,
as the law stands, he is able to sow the seeds of
loathsome and hopeless disease among 50 families
for a total expense of £3O or so. The man who
gives you a fatal dose of arsenic is a gentlemanly
benefactor compared with the villain who doses
you and your family with tuberculosis.
* * * *
Chief Justice Stout, of ■ Maoriland, has
ruled that a man is not drunk so long as he is
sober enough to ask for more drink; so that as
long as he can ask for more drink he should get it.
Plainly the teetotal Stout should be assisted by a
hard-drinking legal brother in dealing with liquor
cases. He means well, but he doesn’t know.
Among other things he forgot to define what is
asking for a drink. If a man stands straight up
at the bar and says, “Mine’s whisky* please,”
that is certainly asking. But if a man is lying on
the floor, and can’t get up, and moans with his
head under a table, “Gimme—hie—rumsh”—is
that a legal demand for refreshment ? Stout
doesn’t say. He is so strictly temperate himself
that he is probably quite unaware that plenty of
men ask for drink on the floor. There are even
cases when a man goes down with his half-emptied
pewter in his hand, and has still sense enough,
though speechless, to hold up the vessel at the end
of a loosely waving arm in a mute appeal to have
it refilled. He is too far gone to inquire for beer
coherently in speech or writing, but still he is
plainly inquiring for it. Does Chief Justice
Stout hold that the feeble uplifting of a pewter
by a horizontal customer is a legal and sufficient
demand for a further supply of alcohol? If it
isn’t, where is the line to be drawn ? If it is, does
he rule that the liquor should be supplied, and
poured over the customer if he is unable to find
his mouth ? Or what does he rule ?
* * • *
Amongst the Melb. banks exists an in-
exorable law that no officer shall marry unless his
yearly income amounts to a certain fixed sum.
Recently a youth, more daring than his fellows,
defied said law and espoused a charming young
music-teacher, although his salary fell about £2O
short of the allotted standard. At last the cat
got out of the bag, and the poor benedict was
carpeted by the directors, who at once dismissed
him with but a month’s salary as a solatium.

The clerical inquisitors, sitting as a Bible-
in-State-Scbools’ Commission in Melb., gave them-
selves away neatly the other day. A witness told
them that, from inquiries, he had discovered that
93 per cent, of State scholars in his district at-
tended Sunday-schools. Whereupon the Com-
missioners, unanimously but most unwarily, re-
torted that they knew of whole districts without
any Sunday-schools. So these rev. gentlemen,
whose business it is to provide Sunday-schools,
admit that they neglect the work at their own
doors while they send their girl missionaries to
batter at the gates of Joss in far-off China.

As an instance of how little we are really
removed from the dark ages, there is in Sydney a
prosperous palmist, who calls herself something
like —“Shusta Mollusc Gaska, the Egyptian
World Reader, Futurist, and Geologist .” She
evidently thinks geology lias something to do with
the atomic theory of syllogisms, and her clients
don’t know any better.
* * * *
“ Veteran ” to The Bulletin :
“A Volunteer” (B. 30/6/’00) misses the point about
those Lancer commissions. The public, who support the
whole concern, are more to be considered than the
Lancers themselves. The Lancers are armed, clothed,
and paid, as a possible fighting force for the defence of
Australia, and not as a pedestal on which a few amateur
officers may sport their figures, and the public is entitled
to its money’s-worth in the shape of an efficient cavalry
corps. According to the latest authority on the subject,
“the necessary aptitude for a cavalry-ieader is seldom
found in man, and is always capable of improvement by
study.” But it cannot be known whether or not a man
possesses the “ necessary aptitude ” till he has been tried;
and it is utter folly, therefore, to give commissions to
boys who may be found totally unfit for even common
routine duties. 001. Burns, in promising that his pro-
should pass “ a stiff examination,” shows he doesn’t
know the A B C of his amateur business. If he wants
officers, why doesn’t he examine his sergeants, who
already know something of the officers’ duty ? And, as
Col. Burns has been gazetted examiner, it will be well
that the public should know something of his qualifica-
tions for the post. Fact is, the public never take these
military squabbles seriously, and don’t believe in the
competence of any military potentate of them all. It
regards the whole thing as a mere excuse for wearing fine
clothes and marching behind brass bands.

Remarkable passage from the pen of
Major Spencer Browne, perhaps the best known
Brisbane man at present in S’Africa (Brisbane
Courier, June 29):
I am sorry to say that our numbers in the Queensland
contingents continue at only SO out of UW men ! Some
are in hospital, twenty have joined the police force, but
where the bulk of the men really are no one seems to
know. This condition of things is by no means approved
of, and it is doing our reputation no good. Eighty men
in the field out of, say, 400 is rather too extreme a falling
off to be allowed to go without strong comment.
Four months ago had anyone ventured to predict
such a state of things as Major Browne sketches
he would have been almost stoned in the streets.
And this, too, in the face of fulsome cablegrams
containing glowing reports of Australian heroism,
daily narrations of wondrous feats of arms, staring
headlines in the newspapers concerning Australian
prowess, and general jam ad lib.

Adelaide Corporation stands aloof from
other local bodies in the agitation for a Tramway
Trust and adheres to its agreement with one
Bingham to sell all municipal rights for 30 years
to a private co. Meanwhile the remaining
Councils are urging the Holder Govt, to legislate
for either municipal or State purchase of lines.
There will probably be trouble when the rival
schemes come before Parliament. Govt, control
of tramways was part of the Kingston policy, in
’9O, but it was forgotten in the agitation for
Council and other reforms.
* * * *
Holman’s notion to have prisoners tried in
open court for breaches of prison discipline in
N.S.W. gaols is unlikely to succeed. Prisoners,
it is contended, would commit slight or other
breaches for the sake of the opportunities of passing
backwards and forwards. Their ability to keep
touch with “pals” in the outside world has
already reached a fine degree of perfection.
Another way to gain the end Holman has in view
would be to make all serious breaches of discipline
—such breaches as are visited with flogging—
offences to be tried by a Judge with a jury com-
posed of freemen, the Judge and jury to go to the
gaol for the purpose.

N.S.W. Board of Health is threatened
with an awkward demand for compensation. A
North Shore case of illness was reported sus-
picious and an official doctor sent over. He diag-
nosed it as true Plague, and consequently the
sufferer and his family were removed to North
Head. Further examination proved that the
patient was not suffering from plague. The
quarantined man was in business on his own
account, and has lost his whole connection through
the mistake.

Editor Bulletin.— Re “judicial dignity”
(B. 29/6/’00). I was present at the trial to which
you refer. Defendant, in cross-examination, was
asked whether he still thought plaintiff had taken
the money, and he said “yes.” “What!” said
his Honor, turning round sharply to witness, “in
the face of this evidence do you still say that you
believe plaintiff took one penny of the money ? ”
“Yes, your Honor,” replied defendant, “although
I have been warned by my counsel against saying
so, and know the consequences, I still believe he
has taken it.” “That will do,” said the Judge,
And I saw by the look on his face that defendant
to catch it. And catch it he did.

Up to last advices, 12,000 British troops
had been invalided home from South Africa—
while the deaths from disease had been within a
couple of dozen of 4000 ! With this during the
healthiest portion of the S’African year, what kind
of holocaust will the sickly season bring ? The
Australian dailies are dropping their “scare-type”
headings to the Boer War news and even turning
out of the middle of the paper the cables relating
thereto—the slayings having become so compara-
tively insignificant—but it is to be feared the big
letters will yet be wanted to record the dreadful
doings of fever and dysentery.

* * * *
“ D.” tells of a hard way to get a living :
Do you know the most malodorous business to which a
workman is asked to put his hand ? Tanning, wool-
scouring, soap-boiling, candle-making, and the chemical
industries that smell like the reek of Gehenna—these are
comparatively pleasant to the senses beside the unholy
and inhuman odors raised by the work of unpacking pre-
served eggs. Wholesale confectioners and others who use
many eggs lay down hundreds of thousands of hen-fruit
when it is cheap and abundant. The eggs are stored in
lime in 400-gal. iron tanks—a layer of eggs, a layer of
lime, another of eggs, another of lime ; ditto-repeato till
you have filled the receptacle. Then water is poured in,
and the whole is left to generate a poignant effluvium till
the off-season, when the hens are on holiday and eggs are
away up. Now begins the work of digging for those
eggs. Men get 10s. a tank for the work, and it has to be
done with the bare hands, as the egg is very sensitive to
pressure at this stage. The wet lime eats the flesh from
the finger-tips and burns the arms red-raw, but the
greatest horror is the explosive egg—and about every
third specimen when washed free of lime shatters itself
with a wild cry, and fills the land with unutterable dread-
fulness. The ordinary decayed egg is an evil thing, but
the limed egg that may have been repressing its energies
for many months, hovrever small, holds smell enough to
desolate ten acres and taint the very heavens. When a
few hundreds of these erupted eggs are met and
assembled together—phew ! the very intellect totters,
and walls fall, and men turn grey and wither in the blast.
A hard-working and hardened sinner can earn about 255.
a week at this game.
* * * •
In Tarabo (W.Q.) the Divisional Board
bought the Govt, bore for the purpose of laying
the water on to the “ town.” A number of rate-
payers, however, objected, and formed a Progress
Association with the main object of protesting
against the water being laid on until things
improved. And this in a “ village of the plains,”
which for six months of the year is a little Hades
for heat and thirst. “Progress Association” is
good !

During the performance of a
wedding in a small Melbourne sub
urban church recently the spectators
witnessed a rather unusual display of
emotion on the part of the bride’s
papa. When the parson asked the
groom, a shrinking young man whose
expression implied that the whole
thing was a terrible mistake, if he
would take that woman to be his
wedded wife, there was a pause, and
an ugly old man with a forbidding
eye and a hard chin jumped up, and
ejaculated : “By , he will, an’
well he knows it! ” The astonished
parson spread an admonitory hand
in the atmosphere, and requested
his dear brother to refrain, but this
only irritated the bad old man,
and he snorted yet more fiercely :
“ Ask him again—ask him again !
an’ if he don’t answer this time, by
—- you can just leave me to deal
with him ! ” But the young man
hastily admitted his willingness to
take that woman, and the holy
ceremony hurried to its conclusion.
.« ,« *
One foi;in of the tangled ques-
tion of domicile in suits of divorce
was rationally settled by Justice
Owen in Sydney .recently. The
.respondent lived in Queensland and
.N.S.W. alternately, and possessed
a duly-married wife in each place.
He was tried for bigamy in Bris-
bane, was convicted, and sentenced
to imprisonment in St. Helena. On
his IN'. S. W. wife suing for divorce,
respondent set up the plea of domi-
cile, arguing that legally he was
outside N.S.W. jurisdiction, inas-
much as he was a prisoner in St.
Helena, Q. Justice Owen ruled that, as he was
not'voluntarily in St. Helena, he was still, for the
purposes of the suit, a N.S.W. resident.

The British House of Commons has ap-
pointed a committee to enquire into the army con-
tract scandals, and the committee has dug out
some interesting facts. What the farmer who
grew the hay got never transpired, but the first
man who got it from the farmer sold it for 575. 6d.
per ton to Brown ; Brown sold it to Underwood
and Co. at 755. per ton ; while Underwood finally
sold it to the War Office, for use in S’Africa, at
85s. per-ton. Had the War Office bought direct
from even the first shark, it would have saved 275.
fid. per ton.

With representative feminine loveliness from all
parts of the world, the Paris Exposition is a carnival
of beauty and costumery. Not the least notable fea-
ture of the show is the wonderful improvement in
dressing- displayed by the modern woman. All the old
ugly and beauty-distorting dress elements having been
abandoned for artistic attire which displays the contour
of the female form divine ; the P.D. corset, of course,
being the most valuable and cherished factor in produc-
ing the delightful effect. Heaven smiles on the P.D.*
