A WOMAN’S LETTER.
A/T-ir tv t» ir Sydney, July 10, 1900.
My Deak Myee,—-
The presiding goddess of this season is evi-
dently not Terpsichore. So far, dances have been
moat lugubrious; half enough men, and so few
women that the half-filled ballroom wears a con-
tinual air of waiting for the others to take off
their goloshes. A suburban hop—usually a
specially smart affair—eclipsed all recent eccen-
tricities. Two hundred people were expected;
fifty turned up. The handful of men
obligingly allowed their programmes to be filled,
but the odds were too strong against them. By
the middle of the evening, three black coats alone
flew round among the chiffons. The rest of the
men got up to the smoking-room, and stayed
there. And, strangely enough, the women didn’t
mind. They put on their wraps at 11, and mur-
mured meekly among themselves how nice it was
to get home early, and went away. By 11.30 the
lights were out, the door locked, and a ball that
usually kept on gaily till sunrise had fizzled weakly
out half-an-hour before midnight.
“ 1 don’t bother about anything this year,” says
a pretty society girl. “If I have no gloves, Igo
without, and as for getting a new wrap, why, I
would as soon think of going in for a new ball-
dress.”
The rechauffe gown is the correct caper. Fringe
on net will veil its past alluringly ; also chiffon is
used as much as ever this season. The fichu
draped round the corsage, and falling in long ends
to the hem of the skirt, assists in these transforma-
tions. Laoe, too, is swathed round the bodice
from the waist up. If you ask me “ how ?” I can
only say “anyhow.” You can either swathe, or
not swathe. Anyone who hasn’t the knack can’t
learn ; and the woman who has it can be taught
nothing. In flowers, the latest craze is for the
convolvulus, which threatens to overrun London
in palest mauves, blues and pinks, delicately
intermixed. As far as anyone remembers, this is
the first time the convolvulus has had a vogue. It
has generally been regarded as a wearisome little
weed, but Fashion crooked her eye over the back-
fence and transmogrified the humble creeper into
the wreathings for royalties’ best bonnets. And —
joyful news to the loyal woman who has wan-
dered far but ever remained true of heart to her
early affection —brocade is in again ! Brocade
that sticketh closer than a hundred nets, never
wears out, and will turn up again through endless
resurrections in the guise of an underskirt, and
finally a plain, unvarnished, highly-reliable petti-
coat.
Government House continues on its giddy
round of dissipations. The Bush Party came off
last Thursday. There were 200 country people—
delegates and wives—present. Just to show you
how carefully his Ex. thinks out his parties I must
tell you there was no classical music on this par-
ticular evening. No ; a cinematographe was. the
artless form of amusement considered more suit-
able for country cousins.
At Govt. House, on Monday night, there were
fifty, parsons and fifty parsons’ wives. Priests,
rabbis, ven. archdeacons, Wesleyan ministers,
Primitive Methodists, curates, all were there.
A sprinkling of artists, the- authoress of a
clever new novel, and half-a-dozen professors
represented the wicked world. It must have
cost His Ex. a grey hair or so to fix on
the form of entertainment for that party, for what
is sauce for the cloth is just plain stewed-apple to
the artist and the author. Even the harmless
cinematograph has its dangers. The swirl of
aecordeon-pleated draperies under limelight, that
goes so well on the picture-sheet, might have
created such a wild disturbance among ye gentle
shepherds that they might have all flown to the
Tivoli the night after. Who knows ? Finally, a
programme of music was considered—well, safest.
A liC/t.e pianoforte playing, and a little singing,
and all the songs in English save one—“ Car Mio
Ben.” Thus His Ex. landed himself out of a deli-
cate position, in which no Gov. has ever found
himself (or placed himself) before.
The artists are next on the list—artists of all
kinds, musicians, poets, painters. But his Ex.
has made a little slip—the first, perhaps, in art
matters. He has invited a well-known artist to
one party, and his (the artist’s) pretty, clever
young fiancee to another. For my part I don't
see why a governor should be expected to know
all this sort of thing; but what is a private
secretary for ? I can answer the question myself.
Judging by what we see, and what we hear, and
what we imagine, _ the present Draconic Major-
Domo spends all his time in shadowing His Ex.
Society gets a little amusement out of the other-
wise dolorous affairs by watching the anxious,
nurselike manner of the one aide who is always to
be found at His Ex.’s side. If the Gov. drops his
glove the secretary nearly drops too in his eager-
ness to pick it up.
Gov. Beauchamp will probably get a scare when
he reads the recently-published letters of the
Duchess of Devonshire—Gainsborough’s and Rey-
nolds’ beautiful duchess. There is one letter in
which the beauty’s mother, the Countess Spencer,
warns her daughter in regard to associating with
Mrs. Fitz Herbert. Slie says
As yet nobody but Lady Beauchamp and Lady Brough-
ton have been with her, and these cannot be examples to
follow.
The casual reader may be alarmed to read that a
Lady Beauchamp was ever so improper a character
that a respectable young duchess could not follow
her- lead; out, in fact, the present Beauchamp
title was-not then created, and the naughty lady
was an ancestress of our old dancing visitor, the
Gearl of Yarmouth. The Duchess’s reply to her :
mother was curious:—“Mrs. F., an unmarried
woman suffering ye visits of an unmarried man, is
no reason for not being civil to her.”
With your permission, and with humble apolo-
gies and a deep sense of the iniquity of - the pro-
ceeding, I am going to say a few words about the
weather. As a rule we taboo the subject. But
this week you really must allow me a little license.
I went round town on Thursday morning. The
wind blew, the rain pelted, and I wanted a pair
of goloshes. Do you think there was such a thing
to be had ? Shop after shop—first the big ones,
then the little ones—no goloshes anywhere.
Sometimes I was met with the query, “Can you
wear sevens ? ” And when I sorrowfully and
meekly replied that I could swim, or dive, or slip,
or lose myself in sevens, the shopkeepers assumed
the air, “No hope for you —no galoshes for you !”
I am told, by the way, that in the previous spell
of _wet weather one big firm got through over 3000
pairs within a fortnight.
Speaking of _ weather, we are going to have a
great Ski Carnival this month. The date has been
fixed for July 26 and 27. The Sydney Alpine
Club —sounds a little queer, but so it is—is work-
ing up this Carnival with great zeal, and many
townspeople are making arrangements to be
present. Kiandra will be the scene of the
novelty. Snow is falling conveniently hard all
over the ranges, and it looks as if the only addi-
tional incentive towards a great success is the suit
of blanket that every Carnivalite is advised to
take along.
A new activity in Sydney is cats. We now
have our Cat Show regularly. For picturesque-
ness, I give it the palm amongst all other animal-
exhibitions. Also, it is small, quiet, sleek, and
unodoriferous—as compared with dog and fowl-
shows, that is. Unfortunately, this year’s Show
opened on the wettest day of last week. All the
cats went to sleep. Their little brains turned
sluggish in the humid atmosphere, and you looked
in vain for waving tails and beautiful half-puss,
half-panther movements.
Mr. and Mrs. Keep, two ardent and faithful
cat-lovers, are responsible for the new vogue. For
years they have been quietly working up a cat-
tery. They live at Burwood, and devote half
their house and garden to cages full of special
breeds—Persian, Manx, silver tabbies —even the
common (or garden) black baritone —all are made
happy with the Keeps. Out of this cattery has
grown our Cat-show—-quite a National institution
now—opened by the Mayoress, and largely visited
by all classes and all ages.
Apropos, a cat-lover went to see a professional
cat-catcher, lately, in Bananaland. The two
women sat talking Persians in the drawing-room.
Suddenly, a long howl was heard. Up jumped
the hostess. “Excuse me! There’s that old
black cat on the wall again. I’ll hurry out and
catch him.” “But you’ve already got thirty-
three.” “Ah, yes; but Pll sell this one. You
see, people will buy any cat I like to offer ! ” Out
she went, caught the common yowler, labelled
him, caged him, and sold him for goodness knows
how much. It seems an easy way of making a
living—to say nothing of getting rid of the cat.
The Society of Artists is giving its annual ball
in Needs’ rooms on Wednesday, August 25. Mrs.
Paul and _ Mr. Goring-Thomas are secretaries;
Miss De Lissa and Mr. Dobbin, treasurers.
At the Artists’ ball—that is to say, the Art
Society’s ball—last Thursday, I hear that a hurri-
cane waltzer got let loose. A man’s description
of her runs thusly : “ She was a demon at it. We
whizzed round—everything was in a whirl—l
spied two chairs madly circling—l said let’s sit
down —we did. ‘ Excuse me,’ she said, ‘ but we’re
both on the same chair.’”
Miss Kate Rooney’s concert shows every sign
that it is going to be a bumper benefit. The
much-liked contralto is a native of Dubbo, whose
voice has been polished to its present attractive-
ness by Madame Christian at St. Vincent’s Col-
lege. Her big success was made at the Amy
Castles’ concerts, when contralto repeatedly
eclipsed soprano in popularity, and now there is
scarcely a concert in Sydney—particularly a benefit
concert—at which Miss Rooney does not appear.
The date of her concert is Wednesday, July 18,
at the Town Hall. After her benefit the con-
tralto leaves for Paris.
A Sydney girl was debuting lately at a big
concert. She frocked herself smartly in a
lovely new white satin, and stood quaking
in the green-room, looking tall and notice-
ably smart. Suddenly the star warble-i came
in, walked up to the white satin figure,
looked her up and down in dead silence, tossed her
head, and stalked out. Not a word was said, but
the debutante was made to understand so well
what a crime she had committed in out-dressing
the tired-blue-satin star, that her voice quavered
when she sang and her debut fell flatter than the
star’s top notes.
In a modern novel the hero, blase, wealthy, ex-
claims, “ I give to every charity for children, and
to nothing else.” Charity and children—surely
the two should go together, and surely as
long as they do go together, this isn’t such a
bad world after all. Great appeals are being
made for children’s hospitals, neglected a little
during the late pay-pay mania. The first move-
ment in aid of our own hospital—the Glebe —
comes from the ever-ready J. C. Williamson. It
will happen at Her Majesty’s on .Monday,
July 27th. What a matinee it ought to
be ! His Ex,, Lady Mary, and the full
V.R. suite will be there for the first
time since—but memory refuses to go back so far.
Lord Beauchamp at the theatre lias been the
rarest side-show of the year, but the Children’s
Hospital won presence and patronage, flanked
with an instantaneous cheque. For menu, the
whole of .The Rose of Persia,” and society girls
selling programmes. Surely, that must spell suc-
cess-.
The programmes in Her Majesty’s still only go
half-round. The veteran in charge hands tnem
out gingerly, and long ere the curtain goes up he
assumes an injured expression when asked for one
between two ; that is to say, half-a-one. Surely
“ Programmes is out ! ” is a strange cry to hear
in this well-patronised theatre, where big audi-
ences are always to be expected nowanights.
A new engagement that may interest you is that
of pretty Miss Clare Oxenham, daughter of the
well-known Humphrey, and Mr. Jem Toohey, of
Strathfield.
On dit that a presentation to a high dignitary
is not running on greased wheels. Subscriptions
come in slowly. Not half of the necessary amount
has been received up to date. If the full sum
is not subscribed, it looks as if the particularly
high official acting as secretary will have to make
it up himself.
A Woman’s Residential Club is evidently a
thing you run for pleasure—or experience. The
day for making money out of such an enterprise
seems still far distant, the end of all Syduey clubs
being an auction sale and an awful gap between
what the club owed the butcher and what Mrs. de
Jones owed the club. The lady who engineered
the last of these efforts found herself so woefully
out of pocket, health and spirits, that a number of
society women clubbed together last week, raised
£7O, and presented it to her, out of pure gracious-
ness, and not to prompt her to try another experi-
ment like the one that failed in Phillip-street.
The National .Council of Women is undergoing
a little revivifying. The other day, Lady Aber-
deen wrote Out for names of prominent Austra-
lian women who will send information about
woman’s work in their respective provinces—politi-
cal, social, intellectual—to the International
Bureau, whose head-quarters are in London. The
representative names sent her by the N.S,W.
Branch of the National Council were: Mrs.
Julian Ashton for woman writers, Lady Windeyer
for woman’s social work, Miss Rose Scott for
woman’s suffrage, Madame Rougier for the French
colonists, Mrs. Ellis for the Germans, and Mrs.
Armytage for women typewriters and clerks.
Mrs. Edward Harvey, the dashing golden-
haired lady who used to provide Sydney society
with an inexhaustible fund of gossip a few years
ago, figures in an English magazine among a
brilliant medley of duchesses and countesses. Her
photo shows her in a low-necked, square-cut, ball
dress, against a background of sables. With this
she wears a big black hat, heavily plumed, and
turned up at one side'over a lot of choux and
puffed-out coiffure. The photographed high-
borns are all stallholders at the great National
Bazaar, opened in May by the Princess of Wales,
and Mrs. Harvey has teen selling emu-eggs in the
Australian stall, along with Mrs. D’Arcy and
other Anglo-Australians.
Here is a little news from China that you won’t
find in the cables. Two pretty sisters from Sydney
went visiting to China a few months ago. The
sisters—you would know them anywhere by their
beautiful frocks have both found husbands,
gilded Englishmen; they are to be married
shortly, and go to live in London.
Here is a story which is going the rounds. A
well-connected Sydney young lady became sud-
denly ill. Prominent medicos called in, unhesi-
tatingly pronounced the trouble plague. An
official doctor came along in due course and
differed. Meantime the patient got worse and
died suddenly. So that the law might be con-
formed with and the body buried, the first doctor
was asked for a certificate of death and he as-
cribed the happening to plague. The official
doctor objected, insisting that there was no
plague. The first medico stuck to his diagnosis
but eventually gave way to the extent of making
it “ septic poisoning with bubonic developments.”
Subsequently a post-mortem revealed the presence
of plague beyond question. By-and-bye a letter
came from the authorities, requiring the giver
of the certificate to explain. Latter noticed that
the communication didn’t show a registered
number as should have been the case if it were
official—and straightway went down and told the'
-Dept, that if it’d register the paper in the usual
way a full and complete explanation would be
supplied. .The matter stopped there.
( Down at Quarantine Station there is a
1 otts Point ! ” They use the name per-
sistently, despite the horrible slur cast on the
clean and swagger houses of the elite. Did
1 say clean? Well, with reservations. The
maids' rooms in some of the big houses have put
the slums to the blush for malodorousness. But
wliat can you expect, when the grande dame can
complacently inform the inspector, as in one case
I heard of, “Ai have not looked into those rooms
for 14 years ! ” And, by the way, lam told that
the Health authorities are of opinion that Manly
beautiful Manly, is a very, very dirty place
indeed.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ Madame Wiedermann (Mrs. Pinsehof), the
vocal chieftainess of the Conservatorium, did her
best to beg off Marshall-llall from the chastise-
ment of the Righteous. Besides extolling his
genius and enthusiasm, she pointed out, with
tears in her voice, that the Prodigal has been a
penitent since his ‘great trouble’ (‘Hymns
Ancient and Modern ’ ?) 18 months ago. ’
“Madame is a stout, dark, upstanding lady
used to public performances, and her speech was
really effective. Austrian by birth, this all-round
clever woman is a most accomplished cook ; refer-
ence to Madame’s chicken-salad is warranted to
bring tears of joy to the eyes of any of our Ger-
man Naicest. Same heroine’s long-ago wooingby
Consul Pinsehof is a pet chestnut with stout
Deutsclier-men hereabouts. ‘He nefersent one
lofe-letter mit-oudt a cookery-book for keebsalce ’
murmur these sentimentalists emotionally.
Tom Roberts has been showing a portrait
of Madame Wiederman and another of her two
daughters, ‘ Carmen and Elizabeth ’—blonde and
brunette, respectively. These vivacious damsels
are still in the schoolroom with a younger sister
and are only led forth .occasionally to concerts, in
frocks cut muoh lower than those usually worn ’ by
local spring-chickens.
“Talking of singers, and apropos of' Rev.
Charles Clark’s little show, the only fault which
Melbourne people find in Hetty Holroyd is her
aggressive self-assurance on the platform. A want
of tenderness in her fine, clear (and fairly-well
educated) voice would not be very noticeable if
her stylo didn’t suggest that she had oome all the
way from Sydney to show the locals what she can
do. The difference between Miss Holroyd and
Mrs. Howie as bidders for ‘ popular favor ’ is quite
out of accord with the difference in their res-
pective heights and avoirdupois. When the
interesting Te-Rangi-Pai starts to make rich
music she looks pleadingly across the hall as
though half afraid that exception will be taken to
the Maori half of her.
t “ Some people blame Arnold’s version of
Jones ’ because they say his commercial traveller,
disguised as a Dean, is so unclerically fond of
kissing. . But, after all, a parson with a weakness
for promiscuous rosy lips isn’t a desperate novelty.
Scribe was once on her knees at ‘ family prayers ’
conducted by a venerable prophet with snow-
white curls, ascetic lips, and gleaming artificial
teeth, when a shrill outcry was raised by the
serious though comely Scotch cook, also in the
attitude of devotion. A horrid thrill ran through
the assemblage when Janet blubbered out that just
as the ‘auld sinner’ was pleading on behalf of
‘ Thine handmaiden ! ’ he had ‘ creepit his wicked
auld neif round a body’s middle.’ Of course,
accused was believed when he explained that he
had merely extended his arm in the ardour of
petition, and Janet got a little sermon from her
mistress on. the text ‘honi soit,’ &c. When the
rev. pilgrim took the road again, he carried with
him letters of introduction to his late hostess’s
parental home, still cheered by a bevy of lively
daughters, not long imported from school in
Ireland. When their clerical visitor remarked
one day ‘Greet ye one another with a holy kiss,’
and proceeded to practise as he preached, the
careless colleens aforesaid only laughed and
scuffled and wrote to their married sister joking
her on her ‘ Kisserite.’ When she got their letters
she apologised to Janet, who responded dourly ‘Ah
shu’d have clawed the hoary reprobate.’
“ Melbourne has a small girl violinist (pupil of
Miss Summerhayes) who threatens to be a plat-
form prodigy in due course. The infant, hight
Olive Hutchison, is only a 5-year-old, but she
fiddles with skill, and has a remarkable capacity
for keeping in tune.
“Some members of Melb. University Council
are paralysed with what they call the splendid
audacity of Bishop Goe. On the first day the
Marshall-Hall business was mooted, the Bishop
moved an adverse motion, which was lost.
Subsequently the Council went into committee,
the press being excluded, and on hearing that
the Prof, had undertaken to sin no more,
Dr. Goe is said to have told the Coun-
cil that had he known that, he would have taken
no further action in the matter. Since the
Schools agitation, however, the Bishop wrote
to both morning papers emphatically con-
tradicting an ‘ erroneous rumor ’ to the effect that
he had ever said such a thing.
“Madden’s aides wear pale-blue coat-lappels.
Brassey’s wore yellow, Hopetoun’s pink; Beau-
champ’s wear white. There does not seem to be
any rule governing this kind of nonsense. Which
is strange, considering how smothered up with
silly regulations everything concerning guber-
natorial flummery is. Brown would be a most
serviceable colour, as it wouldn’t show the signs of
soup.
“The refined and delicate-looking Prof. Gre-
gory, Melbourne’s new geology man, goes ’Ome in
a few weeks to prepare for the Royal Geographi-
cal Society’s jaunt to the Antarctic. He is to be
the head of the scientific staff, for which his pre-
vious explorations in Spitzbergen and North
America make him especially fit. The Times,
commenting on his selection for the expedition,
says he is ‘ a man of imagination and enthusiasm
and scientific insight, who is sure to make the
most of his exceptional opportunity.’ Which
view his work,, since he has been in Melbourne,
confirms. He is probably the best scientific lec-
turer the ‘ shop ’ has ever had, and finds not only
‘ sermons in stones, ’ but positive romances.
“ Dion Boucicault’s complaint of the Australian
accent is seconded by Rev. Cheok Hong Cheong
the boss Anglican Chow of Melb. Cheok has a
son, who is growing up with such a pronounced
Australian accent that his fond father much fears
it may be permanent, and accordingly he recently
waited on Commissioner of Customs Salmon with
a request for the exemption from poll-tax of a
tutor who should keep undefiled the pure well of
Cheong, junior’s, Cantonese. Even this pathetic
appeal failed to touch the heart of Salmon, and
Rev. Cheong was told he would have to get the
tutor in under the tonnage regulations, or else
have the degenerating accent of his son corrected
m a bonded store.
ours affectionately,

The plague has had its advantages for some
people, though not tor most. The Sydney Sate Deposit,
for instance, has had a great accession of business during
the last few months, consequent on persons having de-
cided to prepare for the worst .by placing their papers,
&c., in safety. The fear is that when a house is quaran-
tined valuables may go astray. It is well to be on the
safe side, and the Safe Deposit affords perfect protection
against either fire or burglars. It is well worth a visit,
anyway. The Deposit is situated in Ash-street, near to
Paling and Go’s.*

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Kruger’s wealth will prove to have been
mostly on paper—newspaper.
A daughter of Edmund Burton, late editor
of Sydney Mail, was, at latest accounts, in the
mission-field in Peking. She is the only N.S.W.
native known to be in that part of China.
The ground on which the Thomas Walker
Convalescent Hospital now stands, a short distance
from Sydney, was stumped and otherwise cleared
by a gang of laborers, of whom South Pole Borch-
grevink was one.
Apropos the Governor-Generalship. The
difference between Carrington and Jersey, said
a N.S. Wales M-P. the other day, was that when
here, a lot of people liked Carrington, but every-
one respected Jersey.
“ The Turner Govt, thought they had got
the ear of the constituencies and could therefore
throw dust in their eyes.”—Small bull-calf from
the speech of Kirton, M.P. for Ballarat, in the
Victorian Parliament.
Mrs. Lawson, mother of the author of
“While the Billy Boils,” the other day obtained
£450 damages from N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioners for injuries received through an electric-
tram starting before she had alighted.

Dr. Leith-Napier, of Adelaide Hospital-
war celebrity, having made a remarkable re-
covery, has resumed work at the Hospital. His
skull was fractured through a fall from his horse,
and his medical attendants, without exception,
pronounced his chance of recovery hopeless.
“But I may operate on some of them yet,” says
Napier!
Captain Tim MTnerney, of Melb., ap-
Eointed Queen’s advocate at Pretoria, is a long-
eaded young man, and hasn’t wasted his time
while a prisoner in the Boers’ birdcage. Big
brother Dr. T. P. months ago sent him the tip to
study Roman-Dutch law, and he has since been
cramming the subject in the intervals of cursing
his luck.
Donald Cameron, after surviving three
fortunes, finally retired from this scene of finance
and infirmity at the Old Colonists’ House, Mel-
bourne, the other day, age 80. His Australian
experiences extended over 59 years, and he was
fond of boasting that he had shaken hands with
every Yic. Governor from Latrobe to his Wax-
excellency.
The gritty and workful Labor-member
Trenwith (Yic.) will be 53 on Monday next (July
16). He was born in Launceston (Tas.), being the
second of three sons of a Cornish bootmaker, and
he began work at the trade when 9 years old. At
21 he left for Melbourne, and in 1879 tried unsuc-
cessfully for Parliament; it was 10 years later
ere he won his seat at faithful Richmond.
On May 27, at Warwick Square, London,
died, aged 93, the widow of Rev. R. T.
Bolton, who came to N.S.W. in 1837, and was for
many years resident clergyman at Hexham,
Hunter River. Mrs. Bolton has six surviving
children, one of whom is the widow of the
late Justice Windeyer. Mr. Bolton returned
to England in 1864, and was appointed vicar of
Padbury, Buckinghamshire.
Maurice O’Rorke, lately re-elected Speaker
of Maoriland House of Representatives, almost
holds the Australasian record. He was Speaker
for 11 years (1879-90), and again for 6 years (1894-
1900), or 17 years in all. George Kingston was
Speaker in S. A. for 18 years, so he goes one better.
Robert Officer, of Tasmania, wore the honored
wig and the long rags of office for 16 years, and
Francis Murphy, of Victoria, was on deck for 14
years.
The recent Parliamentary congratulation
of the Prince of Wales on his fortunate escape
from the bullet of the cracked-brained youth
Sipido recalls the shock that Parkes once received
when the Prince of Wales was the subject of
similar allusions. Parkes sat in the condemned
cell in Darlinghurst Gaol with the doomed
O’Farrell before him and with a certain S.M.
Herald shorthander jotting faithfully behind
the door.
Parkes : I may be obtuse, shortsighted, but it seems to
me very wonderful that if these Feniaus wish to make an
attack on the Royal Family they do not make it on the
Prince of Wales.
O’Farrell : The Fenians are very fond of the Prince of
Wales.
Parkes : Fond of the Prince of Wales ?
O’Farrell :In this sense. They look upon him as
useful to their cause—because he disgraces Royalty.
Which wasn’t what Parkes had come to hear.
The late N.S.W. Chief-Justice Stephen,
is related, presided at a cattle-stealing trial
at Bathurst. Counsel for accused, in ad-
dressing the jury, was eloquently pathetic
over the fact that his client had suffered
the indignity of several trials for alleged
cattle-stealing, and had, of course, been
acquitted. He (counsel) tearfully feared
that if the present trial went against
him his unfortunate client would be
driven from the district. The jury
acquitted, and the Judge, address-
ing the lucky man, said: “Pri-
soner, you are discharged ; but,
before discharging you, permit me
to say, witli reference to the sug-
gestion your counsel has made
that you should leave the district,
that, as the business in which you
are engaged appears to be a hignly
profitable one, and (looking at, the
jury) it also appears to be one you
can safely carry on here, I would
advise you to remain just where
you are.” And remain he did.
Gov. Broome, who flourished in
Westralia 15 years ago, had “slung
ink.” He was for a period a con-
siderable contributor to the literary
columns of London Times. He at-
tended the marriage of the Duke of
Edinburgh to the Czar’s daughter at St.
Petersburg, and the aooount he wrote
in the Tory organ greatly pleased the
English Royal Family. When he married
Lady Barker, authoress and widow of a
general officer, his fortune was made. After
a brief experience of sheep-farming in Maoriland,
he was presented with an Australian Governor-
ship.
D. H. Deniehy’s “ Attorney-General of
New Barataria ” —Lyttleton Holyoake Bayley,
chief law officer of N.S.W. for eight months in
the Cowper Ministry, 1859, now a Judge in the
High Court of Justice, Bombay, India—on May 3,
celebrated the Jubilee of his call to the Bar at
Middle Temple. He tried Westminster, Victoria,
and N.S.W. without success, but ultimately suc-
ceeded in India. While returning from Mudgee
circuit the A.G. was a passenger by the coach
which was stuck up by Day, the bushranger, near
Hartley. At the trial of the ’ranger at Bathurst,
Bayley, as Attorney-General, prosecuted for the
Crown, and also gave evidence. Day was con-
victed, and got seven years.

Prof. Aytoun, the “Scottish Cavaliers” man.
Laurie was years ago editor andproprietor of
Warrnambool Standard. He ought to
know something about logic, seeing he’s a
professor of it himself, married the daughter
of a professor of ditto, and has a son whom
he’s stuffing to the neck with the same
material.

Speaker Mason has this session, for the
first time since his election to the chair of Vic.
Assembly, worn his wig during the debates. He
used only to wear it on State occasions, following
his predecessor, Graham Berry’s, example. Having
a fine head of hair and not wishing to go bald, he
left off the horsehair lumber ordinarily. But
members like to see the Speaker wigged; they
think it looks Dignified. So Mason has succumbed
—and will go bald in due course.
ARGL'S-editor Willoughby prides himself
on combining acumen with urbanity. “No one
can impose upon me; not even anti-Boers,” he
likes to assert. The other day, a colleague gave a
plausible beggar some cash, to get rid of him, and
was well laughed at by the editor afterwards.
“He brought the same tale to this room, but got
nothing out of me ! ” Then he arose, intending to
put on his tail silk hat—and found in its place only
the brimless boxer of the obvious impostor !
Dr. Edward o‘Doherty, who has died,
suddenly in Brisbane, is the last of the sons (four)
of Dr. Kevin Izod o‘Doherty and “Eva” of the
Dublin Nation. Both still live, the Doctor in his
76th year. Over fifty years ago the latter started
a newspaper, the Tribune, in Dublin. The
sheet reached its fifth issue when the Govt, sup-
pressed it. The deceased Dr. Edward married a
daughter of Major-General French, N.S.W.
Commandant. Some time ago there was talk of
an Australian issue of “ Eva’s ” book of patriotic
verse, but so far nothing has been done.
Chief Secretary Peacock was not too
popular in a small comer of his electorate on the
occasion of a certain Vic. general election, and
one night during the campaign he heard a man
calling to another who had a box under his arm,
“What have you there?” “Peacock’s eggs,”
yelled the other. “What rot!” was the reply.
“ All right,” said the man with the box; “just
you turn up at his meeting to-night and see.”
Peacock took an umbrella to that meeting. Which
recalls that Toby Barton keeps for electioneering
purposes an old surtout which he calls his “ ova-
coat.”
Apropos of something or other. It is re-
lated thatupnorth (Q.) about 16 years ago a certain
would-be Leg. Council nominee got an intimation
that so many Protestant aspirants were certainties
that his only hope of inclusion was as one of the
chosen belonging to the “ True Faith.” Then he
remembered that he had been a good R.C. 30 or
40 years before, and, to the amazement of the
Faithful, he began to attend Mass with regularity
and apparent devotion. But, alas, nobody let
the Govt, know, and as there was no room for him
as a Protestant, there was no thought for him as
an R. C. nominee.
Apropos the mutilation of London Times
correspondent Bowlby by the Chinese in the days
of long ago. When the account of his torture and
death reached England the Times published a full
account of it, but ere the paper was issued a
separate edition of about a dozen was" printed and
sent to his mother, who lived a little way out of
London and was such an invalid as to be confined
to the house. That special edition told how
Bowlby was captured after a desperate fight and
killed almost painlessly by a sudden sword-stroke.
They were gentlemen who ran the Times in those
days, at any rate !
When Vic. Chief Justice Madden was afc
the bar he used to have a system of
mnemonics of his own. While
arguing a difficult case before
the Supreme Court he would
work without notes, yet he
never forgot a point. The
interruptions of judges and
objections of other counsel
might take him from his
point, but he would always
get back and finish it be-
fore going on to something
else. He would have a
book —any book—in front
of him, and would turn
down the corners of a few
pages, one corner for every
point he wished to make.
As a point was finished he
would straighten a page
and go on to the next.
He also used to find it
necessary to have some-
thing to play with in his
fingers. At first he used
to tear up paper, and the
ground at his feet would
be half-an-inch thick with
pieces at the end of a day’s
argument. But one day a
court usher discovered that
a piece of tape would do
just as well. Then the
officials used to be careful
to provide Dr. Madden
with a piece of tape when
he was in a case, and the
floor was kept free from
litter.
Latest portraits of
Lord Rosebery show the
amiable widower to be al-
most as young-looking as
when he visited Australia with
his jolly wife, 17 years ago.
Lady Rosebery, by the way, used, when here, to
receive from England daily a cablegram as to the
health of her children, and every day or two
used to send them long messages.
Apropos the statement that Judge liar ley
will resign the N.S.W. Chief Justiceship on
attaining his 70tli year, Sept. 18 : the Chief will
then be 14 months short of the 15 years’ service
necessary to secure his retiring pension—£24so.
As it is pretty certain that his Honor will not long
retain office after completing his term, the ques-
tion arises—who will be his successor ? There is
no puisne Judge in the running ; some are too
aged, and others have not the needed qualifica-
tions. Then, as to the Bar, Salomons is, for
various reasons, an utterly unlikely aspirant;
Barton and O’Connor look for Federal positions;
Pilcher is too rich and too indolent to covet the
post; Want and Reid are quite improbable ; so
that B. R. Wise appears at present the only man
likely to fill the bill.
“ Dick ” Mooney, Customs officer at Went-
worth, who died suddenly a week or two ago, was
for nearly a generation out in the Far West of
N.S.W. Long before Broken Hill was found,
when the chief value of the Barrier Ranges was as
a retreat and hiding-place for horse-thieves and
cattle-duffers from all parts of inland Australia,
Mooney guarded the N.S.W.-S.A. border for re-
venue purposes, putting up mostly near what is
now Silverton. Long association with rough-’uns
had made him just about as little like a typical
civil servant as a city person can imagine, and the
polite visitor to the Custom House (when, by the
bye, polite visitors did come that way) was pretty
sure of a shock. But he was good iron, if weather-
marked a bit; and he did work for the State
which not a man in ten thousand could have done
so well, if at all.
Like a good many other Laborists in the
Parliaments, Cann, the mew Chairman of Com-
mittees in N.S.W. Assembly, is a cold-tea votary
(though not a “ bigoted ” one), and has done some
work as a Methodist “local-preacher.” He was
one of the first batch of Labor M’s.P. elected after
the big strike of ’9O. Of the 30 then returned,
only three are still Labor M’s.P. —Cann, M‘Gowen
and Edden. Cann, a miner, was on night-shift
when the Labor Executive at Broken Hill was
deciding upon its man ; and, with his “ crib ’’-tin
in his hand, was called in on his way home
from work to be practically appointed first M.P.
for the silver-town. Ferguson, who was years
afterwards elected for another constituency, ran
him closest for the nomination then. Until he went
to the House Cann had been a miner continuously
since he was 10 years of age.
Gordon Sprigg, now again Cape Premier,
is the elder brother of Melb. Tramways W. G.
Sprigg, and the second son of their mutual father,
who was a Baptist parson at Ipswich (Eng. ). Ill-
health sent Gordon Sprigg to South Africa. He
started life on the House of Commons reporting
staff (the reporting being at that time done under
Govt, contract by a private firm—the Gurneys),
and the long hours knocked him out. But the
Parliamentary experience that he had gained in
the gallery at St. Stephen’s made his fortune as a
politician in Cape Colony. Gordon Sprigg is a
five-times Premier, as wel 1 as an ex-Minister of
several departments, and th e fact of his leading
the Govt, in these troublous times shows him to
be a reasonable old campaign er, pro-British rather
than violently anti-Boer. Our Mr. Sprigg, the
Melb. Tramways potentate, explains that it was
his brother who offered, on behalf of Cape Colony,
to “ shout ” a warship for the British Govt. This
expensive outbreak of loyalty has been often put
down to Schreiner, who succeeded Sprigg, whereas
it was one of the reasons why the Afrikander
Bond Par ty put Schreiner into power

Aboriginalities.
A man crossing a N.S.W. river, recently,
was drowned in sight of several friends who failed
to render any assistance, though they made such a
noise that it brought the drowning man’s wife
from her camp near by to view the tragedy. She
saw it through to the end and when the last bubble
had floated down stream she turned away and up-
ended the spectators by remarking reflectively,
“ Well, well, my Bill’s drowned ! Anyhow, there
is no good crying over spilt milk ! ” Then she
went resolutely back to her tent and finished the
baking she had in hand. Hearing the news, the
storekeeper’s wife came down and tried to console
the bereaved woman. She was found weeping
softly and attending to the bread at the same time,
but at the first words of condolence she pulled
herself together and said : “Yes, Missus, it was
very sad. And he bought a new pair of boots
from your shop yesterday.” Probably she felt her
grief just as much as her more eloquent sister of
the city, but the wilderness is a silent, casual
place, and its sons and daughters grow silent and
casual also.

Dear Bulletin. — Re breeding of black
sheep, let me tell “Canip” (8., 23/6/’00), on
authority of M‘lvor’s “History of Sheep-farming,”
that as far back as 1850 a Scotch sheep-owner
started breeding from 20 black sheep, and had in
a few years raised a flock of 250 blacks. Also on
Darling Downs (Q.) a squatter named Allen bred
black sheep for 15 years, with only an occasional
light-spotted animal amongst the products; and
Mr. Bailey, of Narandera (N.S.W.), who has a
black merino flock, testifies that they breed true
to color, and are much hardier than white sheep.—
J. A.O.

Fawk: Near Stuart Town (N.S.W.)
’tother day I met a typical fossicker on his way
to a bit of ground tnat he had “always been
going to try.” Went with him. But it was no
good. “Just a colour, the same as you can get
anywhere about here.” Then he talked. Had
been at it since ’53. Made money at Maryboro’
(Vic.), and lost it. Was one of the first at Wya-
long, and just missed one of the best things there.
His mate, on his way to pay the deposit, had made
a wager and lost, and others happened along. “ I
see in the News that they got 620 z. from the last
crushing, and I couldn’t sleep last night for
thinking of it.” It was the old story. His chil-
dren neglected him now that his money was gone,
though one son was doing well in Westralia, and
another in Sydney, where also a daughter kept an
hotel he had “ put her into.” But —of course!—
he knew of a spot. The gold was there, but he
had no money for sinking. He hadn’t averaged
3s. a week the past eight months. He knocked-
off early to go and sharpen his pick, as he was
“ going to look for the reef next day.” Hope
eternal!

“Ben Sun”: Isn’t it time the phrase
“rabbit-pest” got a rest? Before bunny’s day,
when wheat was growing thinly the cocky looked
for sheep to eat it off to “thicken” it, and save
rolling, while his sons sat on the fence and said
“blanky” and wondered where the bailiff was.
Now bunny “thickens” the wheat—and gets
turned into cash while it’s growing; and the bailiff
is no more. Moreover, nomadic trappers buy
cocky’s produce, and leave the rest of tneir earn-
ings at the local pub. and store. And, again, how
many families are there that find a, few traps or a
good “tyke” a lot safer than “shaking” meat
or dodging the butcher ?

“ W.K.A.” : Re rabbit and ’possum skin-
ning (“ Petrus,” B. 2/6/’00). Writer has skinned
thousands of bunnies, and always found that the
quickest and best way is to slit down inside of a
hind leg to the tail; the skin may then be easily
peeled off to the head. Also, you thus don’t lose
the leg-skins; and bulk is everything to a seller.
Some trappers include the tail and head when sell-
ing by weight. In the case of ’possums, to follow
“P.’s” advice would be ruinous, because, by
cutting off legs and tail close to body, much fur-
space would be sacrificed. The tail is now com-
mercially valuable, and is stripped to the point
by careful hunters. Also, as a squarely-pegged
skin commands the highest price, one minus legs
and tail would be condemned by buyers as
“ damaged.” A ’possum should never be skinned
within 12 hours after death, and writer doesn’t
skin till 24 hours after. The man who skins a
’possum under Bmin. is no skinner he’s a
“spoiler.” A ’possum-pelt, properly pegged,
requires 40 nails, and should be stretched length-
ways, as the centre alone is saved for rugs, and,
by striving for breadth, you open the fur. When
exposing for sale, smooth down each skin from
head to tail, and comb out lightly the tangled fur.
In packing, place fur to fur and skin to skin —and
sell by auction.

The “M'Cooey ’ :
Dear Bulletin. —“ Puyyaka” (8., 23/6/’00) ought not to
have brought Balaam’s ass into this snake-controversy.
I would scorn to seek assistance from any of my relatives.
In first confidently stating that the death adder is “ the
only viviparous Australian snake,” and then, when
jammed into a corner, reluctantly admitting that to
“ those who know better” this is not a fact, “ Puyyaka”
cuts a remarkable logical figure. This is the style of
reasoning he employs. lam quite certain that there is
only one vowel in the English alphabet: but “ those who
know better ” affirm that there are several others ! Not-
withstanding his “Notes on the Morphology of certain
Colubrine Snakes of Victoria,” the late Prof. McCoy him-
self acknowledged my discoveries in connection with the
offspring-swallowing habits of a certain venomous Aus-
tralian snake to be “ novel and, in the highest degree,
interesting.” Therefore, “Puyyaka’s” “flat contradic-
tion ” amounts to nothing.
There was a dead-meat dispute going
on before the Conciliation Board in the M.L.
capital. The poetical official ass. dropped
in as a listener, and, inspired by the gori-
ness of his surroundings, dashed off
the following in red ink. He calls
it an “All-Red Poem” :
Deftly he plies his blanky trade ;
His blanky hands disgust his dame.
That such a blanky man be paid
Low wages is a blanky shame.
They had lived long together and were
happy enough, but yielding to social pressure
they got “spliced.” Soon afterwards a neighbor
met him and he looked troubled. “ It’s this way,”
he said, “’s mornin 5 th’ ol’ gal says, ‘A married
woman shouldn’t be rabbit-trappin’, an’ I ain’t
goin’ any more.’ This was airs, seein’ she had
done it so long, so I gets mad, an’ says I, ‘ You’ve
got to, for I can’t keep yer inside.’ ‘Well,’said
she, flarin’ up, ‘I can keep you outside.’ Then
she collars the setter, an’ she’s keepin’ her word,
an’ I can’t set me traps. When yer passin’ yer
might tell her to chuck it out. O ! she won’t make
no mistake, ’cause she’s lookin’ through the crack
an’ ’ll see yer cornin’.”
* * * *
“ Hori ” : During 'Bl drought, writer was
Eoing down right bank of Barwon (N.S.W.), when
e struck a surveyors’ camp. Boss and four men
almost starving ; chainman away with packhorse
looking for tucker. Flour, 2s. 6d. per lb. in Wal-
gett then, and 31b. was the maximum sold to one
person “until the teams arrived.” If you gave
3s. 6d. per lb. you might get more. Was sharing
loaf of bread with them, when teamster came
along with a load for Barton, the Boorooma pub-
lican. Among other goods was a case of dried
apples —the only thing amongst his stock of imme-
diate use to the surveyor. The latter tried to buy
even portion of the apples, but the bull-urger
refused to be tempted with double value, as the
goods were ordered. Even an offer of £5 was
refused, though any man of sense, capable of
reading the expression in a surveyor’s eye, would
have known better. Then the camp (five strong)
commandeered the apples gratis, and the best
satisfaction the ox-propeller could get was an offer
that the five would fight him all at once. He
talked wildly of the police, but there wasn’t a live
bobby within 50 miles. Met the surveyor after-
wards in Dubbo. He told me he and his mates
lived four days on apples till the chainman got
back, bringing 251 b. flour —or rather 231 b. flour
and 21b. defunct insects. They ate apple-chips
for a meal, and then took a drink of mud and
dead frogs to make them swell up.
* ♦ • *
“Cuss”: Dingoes out about Tibooburra
and S.W. Queensland are simply wiping the
drought-spared sheep off the face of the earth. A
short time since the manager of Bransberry (S.W.
Q.) mustered only 1000 head out of 5000 put on
the country but 12 months previously ; 90 per
cent, of the loss being due to dogs. At Yan-
damma, near Tibooburra, the manager says he is
losing at least 450 head a-week from same cause ;
and at Ticalara in 4 months the dingo-loss was
4300 out of 9000. Other stations report equally
severe losses. Hundreds of blocks across the
S.A. line have been thrown up through continued
bad seasons, and, there being few rabbits left, the
dogs are “working in” towards settled country to
avoid starvation.
* * » •
From “ W.S.”—as showing the condition
of the N.W. country of the interior :
A teamster with 20 good horses, making in for Tiboo-
burra a few weeks since, got stuck on a long dry stage,
and every one of his horses died ! Then, after eating the
solitary goat he had remaining, he humped bluey in. At
one station, situated on a good water-hole on the Cooper,
they have to get in wood for household use in a boat, not
having a hoof of stock left on the place. The men on
the Govt, rabbit-proof fence inspection have to do some
awful stages without water, 50 and 60 miles being quite
common. Men going “ out ” for the coming shearing—
though Heaven knows what there will be to shear—are
passing through Tibooburra in shoals; the “ringers”
almost all on bikes, and the “ rousers” padding the hoof,
as usual, coatless and hungry. Never were seen passing
through so many men without even coats to their backs
or blankets to lie on.
* * * *
A N.Q. paper tells how 12,000 Sesbania
sheep “ are about to meet their doom in a paddock
adjoining the Ayrshire Downs boundary.” The
sheep were about 16 miles from the nearest bore
and had subsisted “five weeks without a drink.”
They were consequently too weak to be shifted,
and the journal above-mentioned gravely an-
nounces that “a falling off in numbers has just
set in.”
* * * *
At Back Creek, near Warren, N.S. W , re-
cently, a man—with a pleasing mixture of fat,
arsenic, and strychnine—poisoned, in one day 580
crows, equal to £9 in bonuses. A little gold mine,
if the Stock Board’s funds are strong enough.

CONTRADICTION.
The curlew came and told a tale
Of dreadful woe, and went at dawn ;
The jackass heard his parting wail,
And laughed the pessimist to scorn
And so between them both I find
A law that governs land and sea.
And why my lovely nevermind
Tells lies “ as well as me.”

“ Alia *’ - Re the speed of rabbits. I was
brought up in a M.L. district where rabbits
abounded, and have frequently run them down on
foot. Generally selected a peculiarly marked one
—white, black or yellow—and, having ascertained
his particular burrow by a preliminary run, would
block it up. Bunny always makes straight for his
hole, and, if kept going, on finding it stopped is
easily captured, if another hole is not handy. A
rabbit is too fast for an ordinary dog for about
500 yds., but has no staying power, and a dog
with a good nose will soon tire him out where
cover is not available. It is a curious fact that, if
you locate a rabbit in a tussock or bush, and walk
round and round same, gradually dosing in and
keeping your eye on him, when bunny tries to
break away he will fall oyer dazed for a few
seconds, so that if you are quick you can nab him.

“Jay”: Refish s wallowing fish (8., 9/6/’00).
I have successfully used small Murray cod as live
bait for the big fellows. Apropos : I one. morning
hung a line up to dry on a stick, projecting from
the bank ; ana, going back in the evening to bait
it, found a big cod banging on, with a 31b. one
inside him —hooked by the tail. The wind had
blown the line down, and the little fish, in fooling
around the bare hooks, had got into trouble.

The first newspaper published in Van
Diemen’s Land in 1810 contained this announce-
ment : —“On Monday, 26th ultimo, R. C. Bur-
rows to Elizabeth Tucker, both late of Norfolk
Island. They had cohabited together for 14
years, verifying the old adage —better late than
never.”

“N. de W.” : Canftot understand the
soapy taste of “L.O.’s” boiled kid (B. 16/6/’00),
unless it was boiled in a soapy pot. (The boiler is
use 1 for queer purposes in the backblocks.) Also,
his “ bad quarter-of-an-hour ” must have been due
to his imagination. I infinitely prefer kid, when
in good condition (which it generally is), to poor
lam’). Nine men out of 10 could not tell kid from
lamb, in any case, and, on many . Queensland
selections, the former is eaten habitually as a
matter of course. Also, in many bush pubs, kid
(re-named) is the staple article of diet. There are
worse moves than running a big herd of goats in
the wayback.

No notice will be taken oj communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.

tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-

The H.H. doesn’t seem to have been heard of on Sydney
side, but Sydney has heard of the brave Brunswicker
who boasts of having cut off a dead Dopper’s whiskers
(since put into a frame and proudly exhibited from the
platform at a pleasant little social)... .Old Bus : But an
insurance office’s expense rate has no connection with the
interest earned on its investments. Every office esti-
mates in advance what interest its investments will
yield, and fixes its premiums so that they (or
rather what is expected to be left of them
after paying expenses) with that interest added
will accumulate sufficient to pay the policies. That office
obtained £1 9s. per cent, more interest than it estimated.
But it might do that and yet spend all that £1 9s.
and more in excessive expense and go insolvent
A. Munro: Britain is a limited monarchy, because the
Sovereign can’t make or unmake laws, or impose taxes, or
help herself out of the Treasury. Her chief power lies in
the fact that she can prevent laws being made, that she
can declare war at her own free will, and that she is sup-
posed to have command of the army. But the two last
don’t'amount to much when she hasn’t the money to carry
on the war or keep the army. The British Sovereign has
so little real power that there is hardly any reason to
complain of her ; the trouble is that she is the innocent
cause of sickening and unmanly grovel in others.
Kitchener didn’t shift the Mahdi’s tomb because
the tomb did any harm, but because it was
the shortest way to shift its worshippers. The
people who knock down images go on the same p1an. ....

A.F.M., Kitchenkop, S.B. 2, M.H., Weel. H.C.
Cabled that when the allies captured some
Chinese forts, the Jap. commander, in leading his
men to the assault, was beheaded. The Chows
have many experts in the gentle art of beheading
—there is so much of it done in the Flowery Land.
The executioner has his victims kneeling before
bim in a row, and generally a very long row, and
their arms tied behind them. As each one’s turn
comes, the butcher’s assistant catches hold of the
subject’s pigtail and pulls forward his head,
whereupon the headsman severs-the neck with one
lazy-looking slash. The Chinese executioner
never cuts twice —that would be beneath his
dignity. In the rare cases when through some
accident the neck is not severed, another man
comes along and hacks through the rest of the
neck with a knife.
“ H. ” : The Boxers lead one to study the
Chinese insurrection, which lasted, more or less
violently, from 1850 to 1864, when Gordon put it
down. The Secret Societies gathered at first
round a certain Wung, who borrowed as much
Christianity as suited him, representing himself
to be another Christ. Li Hung Chang was em-
ployed to suppress, but not as general. Ward,
an American, acted in that oapacity. He said,
“I carry the sceptre of China in my trunk.”
This vaulting ambition was cut short by a mortal
wound. Burgevine, another Yankee, succeeded
Ward, and similarly had his head full of schemes
to make himself Emperor. But Li Hung Chang
fathomed Burgevine, and Major Gordon was
substituted. The success of the Tai-pings was
astounding. They collared the whole south-east
of China, and even captured Hankow and Nankin.
Wung found Nankin his Capua. There he
gathered a harem of 30 women, and became a
bloated Vitellius, and was falling to pieces just
like the Mahdi after Khartoum. Otherwise, it is
said, nothing could have stopped kis taking Pekin.
However, his generals made a good fight after he
had become a nullity. Burgevine, after he lost
his billet as government general, offered to lead
the rebels and proposed to Gordon that they
should make common cause, seize Pekin, and
divide the Empire. Chang heard of this, and dis-
trusted foreigners ever after.
Ennef: The “Hindmarsh Hero's Ride” is a
daisy of a poem, so we give one verse on spec :
You have heed too long the scouters,
Who prate that our clime has thinned
The red to white in the Austral heart —
But, those bladders of blighting wind
Are pricked by Truth’s Ithuriel :
So we’re sure that they have lied,
And their mouth’s are struck by the dash and pluck
Of the Hindmarsh Hero’s ride !
A Midnight Bide.
Mr. Alfred Smith, a settler residing at Upper
Taylor’s Arm, Nambucca River (N.S.W.), thus
describes an eventful midnight ride he under-
took some time ago.
“I was working very hard unloading corn and
placing sheep in a waggon,” said he. “ I had 12
miles to ride home, and it was nearly midnight
when I set out. The river air was damp and a
heavy dew was falling. When I arrived home
my clothes were sodden and I contracted a severe
cold, which settled in my system. Nothing I
tried could combat it, and I was attacked by
diarrhoea, fainting fits, a heavy, sleepy feeling,
and severe pains in parts of my body. I lost my
appetite, and after the slightest exertion my heart
palpitated alarmingly, and the short hacking
cough I had seemed like bronchitis. On the advice
of several neighbours I commenced Dr. Williams’
?ink pills. The second box improved me greatly.
began to get strong again and to eat well. I
continued with the pills a little longer, and was
as active and vigorous as ever.”
Neglected coughs and colds bring serious
results, as in the case of Mr. Smith. A sharp,
cutting pain with chest oppression, feverishness,
and short jerky breath, are symptoms of bronchitis,
pneumonia, pleurisy and consumption. Dr.
Williams’ pink pills rightly used have cured
these ailments after all treatment has failed. By
their tonic action on the blood and nerves they
also cure rheumatism, sciatica, lumbago, neuralgia,
St. Vitus’ dance, paralysis, etc. Sold by the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place,
Sydney, two and nine per box, and by chemists
and storekeepers But mind you ask for Dr.
Williams’, the kind used by Mr. Smith.

\ r SUNNY
Windsor, Saturday.—Last night the flood
attained its maximum height, reaching about-
47ft. above summer level. Only twice before
-—in the Years 1864 and I.B67—has the flood
reached such a height.

Humors of the pestilence. An Australian
firm boldly advertises its brand of disinfectants as
conferring absolute immunity from contagion—
and two of its employes immediately take the
plague.
Melbourne Age, with unconscious satire,
notifies that the Working Men’s College has
started a class in wool-sorting specially for squat-
ters’ sons, who pay extra fees for the privilege of
being put through the course more quickly than
ordinary students.

Be a Bulletin par. of May 19th. The
copper figure of Mercury atop of the Age office
was not originally imported for the Commercial
Bank; it was manufactured locally to the order
of David Syme, by W. H. Rocke & Co., of Mel-
bourne, from C. D. Richardson's, design. The
same firm also manufactured an artistic tympanum
for the new Commercial Bank building, which has
not been fixed up, but will presumably get’there
some day.
“V.”: Met a “right-thinking” Chow
t’other day—a man after S.M. Herald’s own
heart —“ stake in the country,” and all that kind
of thing. Questioned on the troubles in Father-
land, he said that the only possible solution was
to marry the Dowager Empress to a progres-
sive Britisher like editor Philp, of the Australian
Chinese Herald. When told that Philp already
had a charming wife, he said they did not mind
such trifles in China, especially in matters of
state.
Yic. Factories-Act Peacock, M.L. A.,
livened-up the recent jubilee celebration of a
Warrnambool church by slashing around at the
clergy’s neglect to take a genuinely-active part in
social improvement. It was blasphemy, he told a
well-dressed crowd, to call themselves Chi-istians
while they continued to ignore the poverty and
wretchedness of the sweated. Then Bishop
Cooper got up, and, with a peculiar glitter in his
eyes, “cordially thanked” Mr. Peacock for his
“ very welcome remarks.”
The appointment of the commission to
report on the Western (N.9.W.) pastoral drought-
problem came 12 months too late for the squatter
and selector; for the season has, on the whole,
opened better than any for eight years. The only
big question now is whether the country can get
back to its old state, and how to help it to do so if it
can. One thing in favor of its recovery is that the
average squatter and selector can’t afford to pay
the ruling very big prices for sheep or cattle, and
so most runs and selections will fortunately be
understocked for some time ; and, provided the
rabbits can be kept off, nothing could be more in
their favor than that.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Vic. Labor members are recommencing to
attend Trades Hall Council meetings. A sure
sign that a general election is near.
James Ashton may succeed G. H. Reid as
Leader of N.S.W. Opposition when the latter
reaches the Federal Parliament. Ashton’s Federal
ambitions may, however, upset anticipations.
Premier Lyne will not introduce a bill to
prevent members of the Federal Parliament from
sitting in N.S.W. Assembly. Several members
of Lyne’s Cabinet want seats in both Parliaments.
The election for Bathurst (N.S.W.)
Federal Division may possibly result in a political
duel between Postmaster-General Crick and his
whilom legal partner, R. D. Meagher. Heaven
forfend !
N.S.W. Attorney-General Wise’s speech
on the introduction of the Arbitration Bill in
N.S.W. Parliament is one of the few speeches
delivered during the last few years which take
rank with the studied efforts of such men as
Dailey, Martin, and Parkes. The matter and
the manner were alike admirable.
Laborite M’sL.A. are going the way to
kill the public goose which made them Parlia-
mentarians. The Glassey s, Joe Cooks, Fegans,
and so on use the goose by protesting Labor-solid-
arity. When they get themselves sufficiently
advertised they kick Labor-solidarity over, stand
as Independents, and hold both hands out for a
billet.
Premier Holder has been much criticised
For agreeing to an Opposition demand to publish
all cables —most of them confidential —between
himself and Kingston re the Commonwealth Bill.
One wire conveyed the warning, “ Beware of
backsliders, Govt, of Queensland ropeable.” This,
sent early in May, was a particularly accurate
forecast on Kingston’s part.
No Vic. Premier has ever been able to
boom a second time. Duncan Gillies has not been
“in it” since his return from England. At the
caucus at which he presided preparatory to the
abortive attack bn the M‘Lean Govt., t’other day,
all that could be done was to pass a resolution to
the effect that Dunkey’s party would not give its
support to office-seekers led by Turner.
McGregor (Labour M.L.C.) told Presi-
dent Baker and his colleagues in the S.A. Upper
House there was a disgraceful conspiracy to wreck
the Commonwealth Bill by making the High
Court of Australia an expensive nonentity. He
expressed the hope that one of the first acts of the
Federal Parliament will be either to make the
tribunal final in all its decisions, or to put an end
to it as a costly sham.
Ex-Chief Secretary O’Loghlin startled
S.A. Leg. Council t’other dav bv quoting Han-
sard to show that in ’97, between the Adelaide
and Sydney Conventions, President Chaffey Baker
pleaded with his fellow members to recommend
that there should be finality of appeal in Aus-
tralia, and a motion by Eb. Ward to suggest to
the next Convention that the Right of Privy
Council appeal should be retained was rejected by
14 votes to 4.
Premier Lyne is as yet undecided whether
to appoint a commission to inquire into the work-
ing of N.S.W. police-force. Such an inquiry,
conducted by (say) Judge Murray, would inevit-
ably do no end of good. All sorts of queer rumors
(some of them, apparently, well-founded) have
long been current as to the private doings of indis-
creet members of the force, and it is so long since
the police have had a good shaking-up, with an
all-round opportunity .for the’ventilation of smoul-
dering grievances, that it is sincerely to be hoped
a commission will eventuate.
In Tasmania, last year, one-sixth of the
whole number of letters and packets posted and
one-sixth of the telegrams went gratis—partly the
result of the system by which the Flyspeck legis-
lator gets a small salary and takes the rest out in
free postage. The consequent loss was £17,000.
I f half of this is set down to the free-postage legis-
lator, the amount would have sufficed to pay 42
members at £2OO a year each. The system by
which a politician is allowed, in lieu of full salary,
to send his washing home free, by post every week
and get his clean shirts posted back to him is a
ridiculously expensive kind of economy.
Joseph Chamberlain’s intimation of a few
weeks ago, that the British Government did not
think it necessary to further consider the W.A.
Goldfields Separation petition because the chief
grievance—the refusal by Bigjohn and Co. to
submit the Federal Bill to the people—was
about to be removed, is very suggestive. It sug-
gests, firstly,, that Bigjohn only bought the con-
ditional refusal of Separation by promising
submit the Federal question to the people. It”
further suggests that if Federation is defeated by
any wiles or dodges, or even by a straight-out
vote, the goldfields demand of “ Separation for
Federation ” will be further considered.
Maoriland Govt, announces “a more
vigorous prosecution of railway construction ” as
part of its policy for the coming session. A fine
chance for the Seddon Ministry to complete the
three great unfinished railways of its province
without any extra borrowing or any extra taxation
here presents itself. If Maoriland will only make
bank-notes a State monopoly, and pass an Act re-
quiring all banks to keep 40 per cent, of their local
cash reserves in State notes, the required funds
will rise up and confront it. This scheme has
already stood the test of many years in Canada,
and there is no reason why it shouldn’t work here
if the local politician could get out of bis borrow-
ing rut.
The new Victorian Government has a great
chance before it. Like Turner it includes an Old
Age Pension scheme in its programme. Paying
old age pensions is easy when the money is there ;
finding the money is the difficulty. Turner’s
scheme was killed by his want of courage in the
matter of finding the money ; his proposed income
tax on the poor was fatal. If the M'Lean-Shiels
Govt, will propose finding at least part of the
funds by a graduated land-tax such as exists in
S.A., and an absentee tax, it will have the
Assembly with it. As for the Council, if it rejects
the measure, and deprives the people of their old
age pensions to save its own money bags, even the
apathetic Victorians will rise against their 48
Rich Landlords.
The position of Vic. Conservative Remnant
—it can hardly be called a party —in Victoria
closely resembles that of a small boy on a barbed-
wire fence with a healthy dog underneath him.
His situation is painful, yet if he gets down it will
become worse. Said party fumes at the appoint-
ment of Peacock as Chairman of the Factories Act
Commission, and at other democratic acts of the
M'Lean Govt., yet it dare not assist in ousting
that Govt, lest it should bring in one even more
progressive. It isn’t strong enough to form a
Govt, itself, and the Federal and State elections,
both under pure manhood suffrage, are rapidly
approaching, with almost a certainty that the
Remnant will then be so much further diminished
that it will degenerate into a mere scrap.
THE STOUCHING OF
JOHN CHINAMAN.
A remedy for the “push” trouble: send them
to China as the Gorblime regiment, instead of
good upstanding recruits such as composed the
S’African contingents.
An easy way to get population is to pav
people to come iuto the country, and meet the
paymen ts out of borrowed money The other day
200 Scandinavians arrived in Queensland, all get-
ting free passages at the expense of the long-
suffering loan-account. N.S W. was spending
money OI? the extreme tail-end of its assisted
immigration system as late as 1896, though Vic-
toria abandoned the practice many years earlier.
Victoria and S A. paid all their immigration ex-
penditure out of revenue. N.S.W. has spent
£194,430 loan money on immigration; Tas-
mania, £235,714 ; Westralia, £23,034; Queensland,
£2,808,8961 Truly, Queensland is an amazing
country.

“ If any man joined these contingents
with the view of bettering his own private circum-
stances by settlement in another country, he was
an impostor.”— Ex-Pkemiek Reid, in N.S.W.
Assembly. And thus Moree correspondent of the.
Jingo Sydney E. News “ Nearly all the local
men who joined the Imperial Bushmen stated
that their intention was to take up land or billets
if offered in South Africa.” Of course it was.
On the Household Suffrage question in
S.A. Leg. Council, Labor member Kirkpatrick
quoted, with great effect, the dictum of Attorney-
G-eneral Rose-Innes (Cape Colony), coincided in
by Sir Alfred Milner, that the best way to pre-
vent the Transvaal from being run by a capitalis-
tic gang is to enfranchise thousands of men who
are not capitalists. “If,” he added, “the Con-
servatives in this Council say that the S’African
war is not being urged on the franchise question
then they must be pro-Boers ! ”
Already that unhappy family, the
Victorian parsons, in confab assembled to
draw up a schedule of Bible lessons for
the regeneration of the
local youth, are at logger-
heads. The R.C. Church
declined to take part.
Then the Jewish Assem-
bly withdrew. Now the
heads of the Australian
and Unitarian Churches
object to an “elaborate
and cumbrous scheme of
lessons on Biblical alleged
history,” and talk of a
minority - report suggest-
ing merely a few etnical
lessons. How sweet and
blessed a thing it is
to dwell together in
unity !
Referendum Day
in W.A. has been fixed
for 31st July. A
mighty howling is go-
ing on amongst the
Gropers of Perth and
B.unbury, and
they have resusci-
tated the so-
called National
League—the pre-
sident of which
is “Charlie” Har-
per, boss apple-
grower and chief
owner of the
Wist Austra-
lian. The Premier
is in a terrible fix,
and so is Hackett,
who edits Harper’s
paper. To start
with, Hackett in-
duced Bigjohn to rat
over Federation, and
now that Forrest has
turned round Hackett is full of anguish. He
cannot go against Forrest without imperilling
the existence of his paper, which is kept alive
by Govt, advts., and on the other hand, his
pariner, who scents the speedy removal of fruit
and meat duties, defhands the Premier’s scalp.
The fairness or otherwise of architect
M ‘Credie’s remuneration! for supervising the cleans-
ing of Sydney plague area doesn’t really depend on
the value of the work, but on the size of the scare.
The Government had to get a competent and
reliable man at a time when Sydney had the
Terror so badly that people were flying to the
mountains, to Paris, to anywhere—when hotels
were almost deserted, and country drapers
advertised “No Sydney goods kept here ” —when
the job, in short, looked like going down with a
shovel into the mouth of Hell. It could get
abundance of unemployed—the kind which had
found N.S. W. so bad a place that they didn’t much
mind where they went next —but the kind of man it
wanted wasn’t likely to be unemployed. Mr.
M ‘Credie named his price for going into the mouth
of Hell —as was then supposed—and many of the
people who complain now wouldn’t have taken
the contract then for twice as much.
Parker, the anti-Federal delegate whom
Bigjohn Forrest sent to England, ex-
plained there that the W.A. gold-
fields’ complaint about insufficient Par-
liamentary representation (Kalgoorlie
goldfield, one member to about 5000 elec-
tors ; Ashburton
bullock-raising
district,
one mem-
ber to 42
electors,
about 3 5
of them
absentees)
is quite
absurd.
“We have
had three redistribu-
tions of seats in ten
years,” said Parker ;
“we can’t have a re-
distribution every
year.” Quite true.
But what Parker
carefully suppres-
ses is that Big-
john’s redistribu-
tions leave things
on the Goldfields
not very much better
than they were before. He
doesn’t redistribute the seats
according to population, but partly according to
bullocks, and partly according to the necessity for
keeping a pocket borough for little brother
David.
Glassey, ex-leader of Q. Labor-party,
has resigned his seat, and is contesting it anew as
an “Independent” candidate. He still parades
as a “friend of Labor,” but nobody doubts that
his destiny is Boodle wraith’s maw—if he is not
thrust out of politics, as he deserves to be. Glas-
sey has been a traitorous Democrat for a long time
past (he cast his vote and-influence to bolster up
the Q.N. Bank directorate at the expense of the
country); and since he was deposed from the
party leadership there has been little of Labor
about him but the name. Now he poses as a
British patriot opposed to the Worker on the
war question ; and beats the Jingo drum in the
kanaka-constituency of Bundaberg. All good
Democrats should unite to make an end of him.
N.S.W. ex-Premier Reid proposes avoid-
ing the loss of those Australian contingenters who-
want to settle in S’Africa by the off-hand process
of compelling them to come back. As soldiers
they can be forced to return —if they try to avoid
doing so they can be brought back in iron-; as
deserters —and then when they are disbanded
here a lot of the would-be settlers in Rhodesia
will doubtless stay here for want of the money to
get away. It is a high-handed way out of the
difficulty, still this country is supposed to be
going in for the Tommy Atkins business, and that
is how the T. Atkins business is done. T 1 link of
a British soldier telling his commander that he
meant to stay in S’Africa, and cheerfully handing
his accoutrements and his resignation to
Kitchener!
Johnstone Sheldon, the S’African war
correspondent now in Sydney, says that he was in
the thick of the great exodus of Outlanders from
Johannesburg for four days, and that the Bor"
did not ill-treat the departing refugees.
This (the testimony of an Englishman and
a Jingo on the spot) is rough on the lurid yarns
about the bruising and battering of women, and
other yellful stories of the early war times.
Sheldon also says that Gatacre was not deceived
or betrayed by any Boer spy, or policeman, or
other treacherous guide, at Stormberg, and that
. the guide wasn’t arrested, shot, hanged, boiled,
or otherwise punished. It was all tlie usual news-
paper lie. Gatacre decided where he wanted to
go and his guide took him there, but the
swiftly-moving Boers got there first, and trouble
resulted.
The Darling Harbour (Sydney) land re-
sumption scheme is to include the awful “ Rocks ”
district, which thus has a chance of being cleaned
and purified and reconstructed. It should have
included all Sydney. There is a great opening for a
Government which would compulsorily resume all
Sydney and suburbs, at a fixed percentage above
the municipal valuation ; pay for it in 3| per cent.
State securities, interest payable in Sydney only ;
enact that the soil should henceforth be the in-
alienable property of the State ; and fix the rents
to yield 1 per cent, towards a fund for paying off
the borrowed money ; 1 per cent, towards a fund
for the gradual reconstruction of the misshapen
city ; and enough over to pay interest on borrowed
money, repairs, and working expenses. The
Bulletin appeals to Minister O’Sullivan to deal
wit h this small question.
Westralia doesn’t know whether its pre-
sent Assembly will be dead or not after 17th Aug.
The Assembly used to last four years, but the
latest Constitution Act reduced the term to three
years, without explaining definitely whether the
change applied to this Assembly or only to future
ones. All it said was :
The constitution of the existing Leg. Assembly shall re-
main unaffected by this Act until said Assembly is dis-
solved by effluxion of time or otherwise.
This makes it clear that the new provisions as to
extra goldfields seats, woman suffrage, &c., don’t
apply to this Assembly. These things relate to
the constitution of the Assembly. But it isn’t
stated that the three years-clause (which applies to
the duration of the Assembly, not the constitution)
doesn’t apply to this Assembly. So nobody really
knows for certain whether the present House can
legally sit after 17th Aug. or not. But John For-
rest will probably chance it rather than meet hia
new batch of voters this year.
THE SWORD WE DREW!
(As it should be.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sword we drew in deadly ire,
It only swept in battle dire,
To right the wrongs of lands oppressed ;
With Freedom won it sunk to rest.
It never fell in East or West
In wrongful fight on true man’s breast;
It ne’er was raised for Lust or Hire—
The Sword we drew !
It never flashed in fulgent Are,
To do some purple tyrant’s hest
Upon the noble freeman’s crest;
it ne’er was soiled in Mammon’s mire—
The Sword we drew !
Brisbane.
P. LUFTIG.

The Greatest Blood
Purifier of the Century.
A SUFFERER FROM BOYHOOD CURED BY
VITADATIO.
Launceston, Sept. 24th, 1894.
To the Proprietor of “ Vitadatio.”
Sir, —I have been a great sufferer from boyhood from
Hemorrhoids, and the last four or five years from Liver
Complaint, the latter greatly aggravating the former. I
have tried almost all patent medicines I thought likely to
relieve me, and obtained medical treatment, but the usual
result has only been of a temporary character. Some three
months since I was induced to try your “ Vitadatio,” and
it has acted Mke a charm on me, and the usual pains and;
distressing* symptoms have passed away. I have now been
some time without taking the medicine, and feel like a
new man. I might here say I have taken six bottles in
all. You are at liberty to use this memo, as you think fit
and I only hope it may be the means of inducing others
who are suffering as I did to give the “ Vitadatio ” a fair
trial, and I feel assured that they will find relief in it. I
am, dear sir, yours truly, W. HA WEES.
The Price of the Medicine is
Large Size, 5s 6d; Small Size, 3s 6d.
Indian Oil of Cream, 2s 6d.
