Cornelius O’Brien, first discoverer of gold
at Grenfell (N.S.W.), is applying for the full
reward-money. He originally received £3OO only,
out of which he had to pay agents and others fully
one half. An additional reason why he should be
paid the full reward is that, in order to test the
famous reef on O’Brien’s Hill, he gave away
shares. The possessor of one of these gratuitous
shares died intestate, leaving no kin, and Govt,
auctioned the share for £l5OO. O’Brien, in his old
age, has fallen on evil times, and the balance of
the long-deferred reward would be very welcome
to him. . The other day Premier Lyne received a
deputation of members on the subject and gave a
■vrripathetic reply.
“ A GEORGE-STREET PLAGUE:
“Replying in the Legislative Assembly yester-
day afternoon to Mr. Nobbs, the Premier said
there had been 76 accidents since the introduction
of the Sydney electric trams, and of these 11
proved fatal.”
“ Ballads of Battle,” a red-and-gold-
wrappere.d booklet of verses by “Johnny” Sandes,
taken from the “Oriel” column in Melbourne
Argus, looks very much as though it were printed
for publication (at a shilling or whatever the price
may be) among Donald Macdonald’s lecture audi-
ences. The true significance of the gaudy cover is
suggested in the author’s preface—" I desire at
the-present brilliant moment in Britain’s history
to furnish a small memento of the stirring events
in South Africa, and of the glorious achievements of
those Australian troops who have on many a
battlefield cemented with their blood the unity of
the Empire. ”
Several London illustrated papers had pic-
tures of the surrender of Cronje. But the artists
hadn’t worked.together, and there was charming
variety of facts. For instance, one conscientious
genius put a big patch on the fallen general’s right
knee, while the others represented Cronje wearing
pants sound on both knees.
“A NIGHT CAP”
of' cotton or wool is a relic of the past, but of
“D.C.L.” Scotch (Black Bottle) Whisky is the
up-to-date comforter and is a boon to all who
suffer from sleeplessness.*
F. W. Whysall, the new manager of
Sydney telephones, affords a good illustration of
rare Civil Service luck. In Broken Hill’s early
days the billet of telegraph-master fell vacant
through the officer in charge putting up his sign
as a share-broker, and the seniors who might have
gone to the Hill declined without thanks on
account of typhoid and dust. So Whysall, a com-
parative junior, was sent. Just at that moment
Broken Hill began to boom; the little office grew
into a big one ; and other circs, put the P.O. as
well as the Telegraphs into Whysall’s hands.
And he, getting his chance, did so remarkably
well that lie wasn’t shifted; instead he was put
permanently into the first-class of P. and T.
masters by the Service Board. But when the
boom burst, the unfortunate whom he replaced
was glad to get back again into the service, and
now he is buried in some country office.
The other day a Hobart alderman made a
row at the corporation meeting because the coun-
cil had shifted a cabstand from one plaoe to
another. His objection to the new site was that
there was a graveyard on ope aide and a guano
store on the other.

prints the following advt. :

Veteran Solicitor William Roberts, in-
terred at Waverley, Sydney, the other day at the
age of 80, developed a passion for poli-
tics in middle age. The passion at-
tacked him suddenly, blazed fiercely
for a few years, during which period
he was elected for Goulburn, and then
as suddenly died out. In the Sydney
courts he conducted some of the big-
gest cases, Frank Gardiner’s and Ber-
trand’s among them. With the excep-
tion of Solicitor M'Culloch (admitted
a year earlier) he was the oldest on
N.S.W. roll. The solicitors of that
early time comprised a number of tough
old practitioners—men who live long
and work every day of their lives.
Mr. M'Culloch doesn’t practise, but
Mr. Russell, Mr. Billvard, Mr. Daw-
son, Mr. Norton and others of the band
still wear harness.
A Victorian J.P. lately fined a
constable £5 for using an official
stamped envelope for his private cor-
respondence. It was rather a remark-
them. His letter was written on the
official paper, and one unfortunate cop.
mistook it for an official communica-
tion. So. fie sold what tickets he could,
and officially returned the unsold ones and
the money. Perhaps she ought to be fined,
but his boss, who used the public’s stationery and
the public’s tired constable as a medium for
trading off gambling tickets, seems sufficiently re-
markable to call for some attention also.
Donald Wallace’s estate (Lerderburg), of
734 acres, in Bacchus Marsh district, was auctioned
last week, by order of—alas !—the executor. Jas.
Sproule, of Flinders (whose wife, by the way, has
one of the most interesting autograph-albums in
Victoria), paid =£ls an acre for the property, and
will probably make a handsome profit on the deal.
Lerderburg was about all that was left to poor old
Donald Wallace of late years. His will was
proved at only £14,000 or so.
Bright red the color of the gore
Shed on the velt in England’s name,
Bright yellow is the precious ore

TWO SORTS OF FATIGUE.
To be tired is nothing. The bodily powers are
more or less exhausted for the time being. No
harm is done. The sources of strength are not
impaired. Food and rest will set things to rights.
We shall sleep all the better for having come
home under the soothing influence of fatigue. It
is nature’s narcotic, leaving no headaches or bewil-
dered brains behind it. It is the highest licence
to knock off work; it is an order for to-morrow s
supply of vigour. The man who was never tired
with honest labour has missed one of lifes
luxuries.
But the thing this woman talks of is very
different. Rest does not relieve it; the cheerful-
ness and refreshment of the evening meal cannot
be used as an antidote to it; it is a sort of weak-
ness which neither welcomes the darkness nor has
hope in the dawn.
“ Ever since I was a girl of twelve years of
age,” she says, “ I have been weak and ailing. I
had no strength or energy, and was always low
and languid. I had a poor appetite, and the little
food I took gave me great pain at the chest and
through to my back.
“My skin was yellow, and I had a constant
pain at my right side. From time to time I was
taken with spasms, and for hours was racked with
pain. I lost much sleep, and had often to sit up
in bed. I had a gnawing pain and a sinking in
the stomach which made me feel as if I had no
strength left.
“In this low state I continued for years, being
sometimes better and again worse, but never free
from pain. I got so extremely weak that I often
thought I should never live.
“ In March, 1893, my mother-in-law told me
about Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and how
it had done her good. I got a bottle from Mr. F.
Hudson, chemist, Eccleshall, and when I had
taken it a short time I found great benefit. My
food agreed with me and I felt stronger. I kept
on taking it, and soon was better than I had been
for years.
“ Since then I have kept the medicine in the
house, and whenever I feel anything of my old
complaint it never fails to ease me. lam now in
good health, for which I thank Mother Seigel’s
Syrup. You can make what use you lil e of this
statement. (Signed) Mrs. Esther L. Pa:in, Cotes
Health Bank, Standon, near Crewe Nov. Ist,
1895.”
Mr. Frank T. Hudson, the chemist whom Mrs.
Palin names, informs us that he has known her
for some years, and vouches for the accuracy of
her statement.
In the absence of definite information we can
do no more than speculate as to the original cause
of this lady having become, at so early an age, a
victim of indigestion. The unhappy fact, how-
ever, is, that there are multitudes of children,
usually girls, who suffer in the same way. They
are anaemic, pale, weak, low-spirited, short of
breath, and generally incapable. Every doctor
comes across them in his practice, and plenty of
trouble and worry he has in trying—commonly
with poor success—to cure them. The fundamental
defect with these young people is a congenitally
bad digestion. The stomach is dull, weak, cold,
and torpid ; hence food does not nourish, and all
the symptoms and results of non-nutrition follow,
as described by Mrs. Palin. The patient may die—
helpless to resist —of some acute disease like
pneumonia or quick consumption or linger along
for many .years, as she- did, bearing a load of illness
and pain that is pitiable to see.
In these sad cases Mother Seigel’s Syrup has
made a record of cures, even in advanced life,
which stamp it as a genuine remedy. It goes to
the root of the trouble, the incompetent stomach
and liver, stimulates them to normal action and thus
ensures a radical recovery. Despite their dismal
past many a woman having used the Syrup, says
with Mrs. Palin, “ I am now in good health

The cleansers in a Sydney quarantined area
recently discovered in a dirt-heap what appeared
to be two human bodies wrapped m canvas.
Diabolical murder was uppermost in the minds of
all and a big crowd gathered awaiting the arrival
of the police. The “ bodies ” were handled very
cautiously, disinfectants being profusely used,
but, on the wrappings being removed, it was
found that the contents were two dummies,
apparently of the kind used by a stage-ventrilo-
quist.
The Methodists once asked Paul Kruger
for a site for a chapel, and he gave them
an acre. Later on, the Jews applied for space for
a synagogue, and got half an acre. The rabbi
called on Oom Paul, and complained of the dis-
parity. “It’s quite fair,” said the old President;
“ they believe in the whole Bible, and get a whole
acre ; you believe in half of it, and so you get half
an acre.” Despite this disaster, the Hebrew con-
gregation afterwards asked President K. to preside
at the opening ceremony of their temple, and
the grim old man faced a housefull of the Chosen,
and, with his most bitterly wooden expression,
announced, “I declare this building open in the
name of our Lord and Saviour Jesus Christ! ”
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems. *
Is this a sign of the decay of English
respect for civil liberty ? On April 14 two Cape
Colony Boers named Van der Walt and Broysen
were sentenced by court-martial at Nauwport to
10 and 5 years’ penal servitude respectively as
rebels. They were not ringleaders, but had joined
Boer commandoes. Now, all through the Indian
Mutiny no prisoners but mutinous sepoys or de-
serters were ever tried by court-martial. Others,
even when taken with arms in their hands, were
always handed over to the civil authorities for
trial. What is called “ Martial Law ”is unknown
to English jurisprudence, except as laid down in
the “ Army Discipline Act.” During the Chartist
agitation in England John William Croker once
asked Wellington what he thought of the proposal
to proclaim martial law. Wellington said: “I
don’t know what martial law is unless it is the
will of the Commander-in-Chief.” He added that
he never had, and never would consent to control
the civil population of a country except through
the ordinary civil authorities. He acted up to
that principle both in India and the Peninsula.

There is a nice little scandal awaiting ex-
posure at the hands of next Westralian Parlia-
ment. Some years since a crowd of speculators,
among them men on close terms with the Govt.,
acquired a big slice of wild bush-land a dozen
miles from Perth for a mere song. The Mid-
land Railway Co.—an English concern —junc-
tioned with Govt, railway there, and the boodlers
represented in glowing terms the natural advan-
tagesof the place asasitefortherailway workshops,
at present located at Fremantle. A sale of a couple
of hundred acres to Govt, was effected at a colossal
profit on the price paid for it a few months earlier.
The ground was then cleared and a few shanties
erected, but the land—or rather sand—has almost
reverted to its primeval state. Lately Govt, has got
a move on itself, and it is now announced that
£lO,OOO is to be at once spent on new workshops
and machinery. One reason for removing the
shops from Fremantle is the alleged deterioration
of machinery by sea-air, but as no complaints on
that score have been made before it is rather late
in the day to bring forward such a flimsy excuse.
Fremantle is naturally furious, as it is estimated
that £lOO,OOO per year is spent in wages alone
at the works. But in Westralia everything is pos-
sible.

WHEN any part of the body
is not doing the work that
Nature intended it to do, it
puts the whole system out of
tune —out of harmony. When
all of the parts do not work
well together, none of them
can work just right. Sick-
ness in one part of the body
is likely to run into all parts
of the body. It is not
necessary to be sick all over
the body in order that you
may feel sick all over.
When children stand a row
of bricks on end, they knock
the whole row down by up-
setting one brick. That is
exactly what happens to the
health when the bowels fail
to perform their proper
function. Constipation makes trouble all along
the line—puts the liver out of order, is bad for the
kidneys, bad for the stomach. It holds in the
body poisonous matter, and because it cannot go
any place else, it gets into the blood. The blood
carries it all over the system. That makes
,s’:s'•gishness, lassitude, bad breath, and foul taste
in the mouth, fills the stomach with gas and causes
windy belching, stops digestion in the stomach,
causes sour stomach, heartburn and flatulence. It
makes pimples and blotches and causes sick and
bilious headaches. Nine-tenths of all human
ailments are due to this one seemingly trivial cause.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills cure constipation.
They really cure it. They do not sicken, do not
weaken, do not gripe. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills give you a healthy stomach, clean bowels, a
lively liver ; they put an appetite in your stomach
and give you blood that is pure, rich and red. Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills have stood the test of
time ; they have been weighed in the balance and
not found wanting.
Charles Harper, Westralian politician,
parochialist, newspaper-owner, and apple-grower,
: says that, in sending the Federal Bill to the
people, “ the Premier lies open to the. taunt from
his opponents who are opposed to Federation (and
they are many) that he is throwing his friends to
the wolves, on the ground that, because the howl-
ing of the wolves is loud, therefore they must be
many.” Yet Bigjohn is really only taking a
Federal census to see whether the “friends” or
the “ wolves ” are more numerous—to see if there
is a friend for each wolf, so to speak. The W.A.
parochialist invariably declares that the Feder-
alists are a mere handful, and curses terribly at
the proposal to take a vote and prove it.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The V.R.C. struck a bright, dry day for the
first act of the Grand National meeting—results a big
attendance, a safe course for the jumpers, and the fastest
3 mile hurdle-race on Flemington record. If anything
could bring a blush to Ikey Mo’s cast-iron cheek, the
victory of Aquarius would have wrought that miracle, for
Ikey opened his double book on the hurdle and steeple
with a 100 to 1 maximum, and the price against Aquarius
capturing the first of the two events was 30, 40, and even 50
to 1 on the course. The winner had done nothing to speak
of over the sticks and he didn’t come from a fashionable
stable. But there were people who remembered that
Aquarius—by Niagara from Rosary was a racehorse in
his young days (Redfearn backed him to win the Derby in
1893) and a few of these old admirers took the odds, and
rejoiced when the numbers went up. The remaining 99
per cent of the audience said Dam.
* • * *
Aquarius (9st. up) was among rankest out-
siders—loo to 3 going begging up to fall of flag. His
time (5.52 J) is fastest since ’B7, when distance was altered
from 2m. 6f. 397yds. to 3m. Next best (5.54) was put up
by Redleap in ’B9; then comes Emmalea’s 6.55 J in ’95.
Not in any instance was Aquarius tipped for a place. One
Melb. scribe wrote last week—“ Beau Brummel ‘lost’
Aquarius at Moonee, and Goldfinder ‘ lost ’ the Beau at
Caulfield. Therefore, what chance can Aquarius have ? ”
Goldfinder was third and Beau Brummel fifth. Backing
’em is a great game.
* * * •
Of the seven nags quoted at less than 20 to 1 for
the G.N. Hurdle, only the favorite, Goldfinder, finished
among the first three. Insult, al2to 1 chance, fell at the
last hurdle when he was leading the field, and perhaps the
catastrophe caused trouble to some of Insult’s pursuers.
Bat, even supposing that the favorite was then interfered
with, there was still plenty of time for him to have got
his nose first past the post if he had been quite the
stayer he was said to be. The way in which Goldfinder
was ridden suggested stable doubts as to his being equal
to a 3-mile journey with the light weight of 9st. 81b. on
his back. Anyhow, a couple of even lighter-weighted
ones were too fast for the favorite at the finish. As for
the animals handicapped at more than 10st., the only one
to make a decent show was Borderer.
* * • •
Considering that the first five horses in the
G.N. Hurdle Race carried less than lOst. each the
scratching of top-weight Wait-a : bit was probably a wise
policy. But you never can tell till you try what a high-
class flatracer is capable of doing with a crowd of inferior
gallopers. Wait-a-bit was ordered to win the Maiden
Hurdle Race (instead of being kept for the big event),
and the Ballarat champion won just as he liked! Strange
to say, the people behind Delusion thought they could
beat the horse that nearly downed The Grafter in the
Melb. Oup. Moreover, they backed their Delusion for a
considerable sum.
* • « •
A line or two re G. N. Hurdles. Since insti-
tution of this event 19 years ago, N.S.W. has never scored
once. Pat’s win in ’9B is claimed by some Cornstalk
writers, but he was in Melbourne months before the race
and belonged to the Vic. trainer Brewer. Ballarat turfites
—among whom were M. Loughlin and Tommy Corrigan
(both dead)— left their marks on different occasions
through swopping down with something that had been
fixed up privately. Redleap appropriated the money in
’B9 and again in ’92. Flemington trainer Whitty shows
out best in connection with the race. He prepared Buzzi
(winner in ’96 and ’97) and Valdis who got home last year.
Brewer trained and owned first nag past post in ’95
(Emiualea) and ’9B (Pat). Horses humping extra-heavy
weights have never won. Don Quixote’s 11.13 in ’B4 is
still unequalled. The Yeoman (12.1, second) and Kings-
wood (12.7, third) are the only nags who have got a
“ drum” with over 12st. up.
» • •» *
At time of writing, the big Steeplechase, run
next Saturday, looks a “good thing” for Mysore, Hay-
seed, and Chelsea “ mixed.” The latter is rather young
for the task, but he’s an honest, improving fellow, and all
going well with him up to the day and in the race, he
will make a big bid for the stake. On the other hand,
Mysore is “ bound to win ” in a street-corner way of
speaking, if he doesn’t let his temper get the better of
him. A while ago the black son of Wellington threatened
to become a confirmed rogue, but after a long respite
from jumping-work he came out and took his fences at
Caulfield, the other day, as though he liked ’em. Mysore
is an interesting question. He wou last year’s G.N.
Steeple, and (bar accidents) he can do the trick again this
year, if he will. At a reasonable starting price, say 4or
5 to 1, which is a short enough price for a queer-tempered
beast, Mysore will carry the present writer’s two-and-six-
pence. Mysore at less than 4 to 1 would not be a tempt-
ing gamble.
* * * *
Ikey Mo’s vile language when a favorite romps
home has been so very vile of late that the V.IJ.C. is pre-
paring to disfranchise the next Ike who may happen to
score six successive unprintable words in one blanky
break. What an argument for the tote ! The machine
ejaculates no filth. It utters nothing save an all-round
dividend of 90 per cent., what time the bookmaker is
cursing because he can’t cop the whole of the share capi-
tal every time. Touching the average profits of the son
of Israel, his sacred Majesty, King of the Ring Joseph,
prattles ingenuously to Melb. Herald. Says the royal
Alf. J. in a recent letter from London : “ There never has
been such a succession of first and second favourites
winning, and the ring have been having a very bad time
this last fortnight, but as the betting is of such magnitude,
one good day and they could get even. ” As though the mag-
nitude or smallness of the wagering had any bearing on
the point that one good day recompenses Ikey for 10 or 12
bad ones ! Imagine the magnitude of his profits when he
gets only one bad day to 10 good ’uns.
■* • * . . * .
“H. S. ” : Now Rand wick (Sydney) has (bar
Onkaparinga, S.A.) the best and most attractive steeple-
course in Australia, would it not be wise of the A. J.O. to
institute a Great Eastern Steeplechase, of, say, 500 sovs.
of added money, with sweep of sovs. added; first
horse, 500 sovs.; second, 75 ; third, 50 or 30 ; and fourth,
20. No third money to be paid unless five horses start,
and no fourth unless seven start. This sort of division
should induce owners to start and run it out. The keep-
ing of steeple-horses is an expensive pastime on account
of the ridiculous smallness of the prizes and the long in-
tervals between events. Also, as the risks of accident are
great, riders’ fees are high. Nevertheless, such a race as
that indicated would be a great attraction, and a step
towards making Randwick “ Steeple ” about the leading
cross-country event of Australia. For such a prize the
competitors might be asked to negotiate the new course
over the hill the second time round as well as the first,
and the addition of a silver or even gold cup by some
wealthy lover of sport would be “ a consummation de-
voutly to be wished.”
* » * •
Cabled last week that the first lots offered at
Duke of Westminster’s yearling sales induced sensational
bids. Twelve realised 43,300g5. One-time Australian
cash-fielder R. Sievier (“ Bob Sutton ”) gave 10,000 gs. for
the Persimmon—Ornament filly, and 5600 and 5500g5. for
two others. Since R.S. hitched on to the Marquis of
Aylesbury’s sister he’s been having a good time. It will
be remembered that he scooped , over £20,000 by The
Grafter’s win, and £30,000 on the day. Since then ’Ome
files tell us that he took £12,000 to £BOOO in one hand
ahout Diamond Jubilee for the Derby, besides other big
sums. His 10,000-guinea 2yr.-old Toddington has turned
out as good as anticipated, and altogether Mr. Robert
Sievieris having a wonderful run of luck.
• » * *
Not generally known that all wagers, no matter of
what magnitude, are paid over at Tattersall’s rooms in
cash. Should a bookmaker offer payment by cheque it
would be thought he was sparring for wind to gain time to
raise the amount. On one occasion a prominent Sydney
business man won £3OOO, and when Humphrey Oxenham
handed over the money in big notes on settling day at
Tatt’s Rooms, the business man, not knowing the strict
practice, waxed wroth, and accused “ Oxy ” of paying in
cash to advertise the bet."
• * * *
Biggest stake-winners for past M.L. racing season
are Advance and Seahorse. Former won 11 and latter 6
events. The pair have never met, though both figured
at Hawkes Bay and Canterbury last spring.
* • * *
Maoriland turfmen are moving in the direction
of stricter legislation re jockey-boys. They talk of intro-
ducing a 3-year-apprenticeship rule by which no lad who
hasn’t served his full time with one stable will be licensed
to “ sport silk ”in a race. The need for such a rule is so
obvious that a paragraph in support of it seems puerile.
Australasia has nothing to learn from England, the old
conservative school of horse-racing, except the lesson that
a boy who can stick to a horse’s back is not necessarily
qualified to steer the animal on courses which are, as a
rule, dangerously circular, in races where starters are
many and jostlings not always unintentional.
* * • *
The unpopular equine Leap-frog is shortly to be
offered at auction by Chisholm and Co. This should
provide a fair test of the public’s view of his galloping
abilities.

“ Nulla”: Bulletin par 30/6/’OO anent nag
“ Spare-me-days.” _ Explanation of how he was thus
dubbed is that being 17 hands high his breeder (a short-
legged man) had much trouble mounting him, and almost
invariably tackled the task With the expression “ Spare
me days, how ever am I going to manage it! ” Spare-me-
days is 11 years old and hasn’t yet landed a stake.
* * * *
The death of good-natured Phin Thompson in
Sydney, on Wednesday of last week, surprised sports, as
the old man hadn’t been ill and was to have started a
month’s holiday next day. “Phin” was brother to Joe
Thompson, erstwhile leader of the Australian ring—who
has been laying the odds on a big scale at ’Ome for years.
Barney Thompson, also a bookie “ down under,” is
another brother ; so was handsome, manly Jack Thomp-
son (dead some years) who not only donned the bag but
shone as an athlete—particularly at boxing. Jack beat
Christie in Melbourne and toured Australia giving exhibi-
tions with Larry Foley and J em Mace when England’s last
champion wasout here. All who knew “ old Phin ” liked
him ; but somehow he never had any luck after leaving
that little shop in King-street where he’d lay you the
odds to a fiver and give a fairly good price on the double.
Phin was managing a Sydney tobacco business for several
years past and up to the. time of his sudden exit.
* * • »
First fight in Sydney for heavy-weight bruising
supremacy of the continent since iron man Joe Goddard
quitted these parts is to be decided Monday night. Ebon-
skinned 6ft. Felix and sft. 9in. Bill Doherty are the princi-
pals. Former has a reach like a gorilla while Doherty’s
length of arm is no greater than that ordinarily met with
in men of his inches. Same two fought before in
Kalgoorlie (W.A.) and Felix won. Doherty, however,
claims that a flukey punch, which isn’t likely to recur,
settled the business. If Felix were a second Jackson—
which he thinks he is, but isn’t by long chalks —a fighter
of Doherty’s calibre and physique ought never to get near
him. The lathy-limbed black may win but not if Doherty
can land a punch or two. It’s rough on the country that
produced Larry Foley, Peter Jackson, and Frank Slavin,
to find such mediocre pug. material classed as the finest
available.
• * » *
“ Old-Timer ” : Recent Cribb-Sturgeon fight ex-
cited more widespread interest than anv other for years
previously. Batches of wires came from Q., M.L., and
W.A. seeking information as to result. Cribb has a
damaged thumb—sustained in the second round—which
will go agaiust him always if he’s not careful. Sturgeon
complained of bad hands handicapping his efforts, but
writer doesn’t place much stock in that ; “ Snowy ”
didn’t fight with too much heart at any stage of the
struggle. His backer, Charlie Dunn, said subsequently
that several times he would have taken a “quid” for
what he stood to win. Result goes to endorse what Bul-
letin readers have been told over and over again— viz.,
that fighters as tipsters of fights are the worst in the
world. This time the whole brigade were crazy on Stur-
geon—gave him as an absolute “ moral,” and went about
among “books” and betting people generally, telling
them to have all they could get on—even if they had to
lay twos. Snowy didn’t put up anything like as good a
go as before, and didn’t seem to have the same zest for it
either.
• • * *
Middleweight champion bruiser of N.S.W. —
Mick Dunn —and heavyweight Fred Preston fight in
Melbourne to-night. Latter ought to find it hard to keep
Dunn’s left out of his face,' and' should—bar flukes —be
beaten, if only on points, as his right is, so little good to
him that he might just as well leave it home. - Said that
Dunn, in the event of coming out best, proposes challeng-
ing Cribb. If he does he may run against a snag.
**- • - *
“ Old Timer ” : Went up to see Peter Jackson
at Auburn, where he is putting the finishing touches on
Felix, and was surprised at the marvellous change in his
appearance. He scales as heavily as ever. Sciatica has
almost left him, and his face has complecely lost that
drawn, death-like look which he brought from ’Murka.
Jackson had a quiet smin. go with the other Peter, and
was easily master right through, though the effort taxed
his strength. He talks of opening a big hall in Sydney
on a first-class scale, and taking privately-arranged classes
in hand at their own times.
* * * *
“Jab ”: Wouldn’t mind wagering that if Felix
retains the championship after his fight Monday night
he’ll get out of that promise to meet Mick Dooley by
suggesting a big side-wager, which everybody knows
Dooley can’t find. Felix isn’t gone on putting ’em up
with the 43-year-old Sydney heavyweight, and never did
hanker after the job. A match between Dooley and
Doherty is a “ cert.” if the latter comes out atop. They’ve
met four times, and each has two wins to credit.
* * * *
Said that a certain boxer, who had the luck to
cut even with a better man in a big match not long ago,
and whose “ whack ” of the gate reached within a few
pounds of a hundred, magnanimously handed his trainer
£7 in requital for seven weeks’ close care and attention
and to pay board and. lodging. How different others.
When Peter Jackson beat Tom Lees he gave his trainer
(“ Geordie ” Cowan) half the winner’s share (£300) of the
house. That’s the other extreme,' of course.
» » * *
“ Mark ” : Whilom Melb. “sewerage champion”
Billy Edwards got another knock in New York less than
two months ago. Yankee bruiser, C. Johnson, settled
him in 7 rounds. Newspapers said the Australian quitted
deliberately. Writer won’t believe. that, as few grittier
pugs ever left this section. Billy’s doings in Melb. amply
evidenced the fact. They went crazy on his spunk in
S’Africa, where nothing but the bull-dog that is in him
brought victory his way in that 25-round battle with Mick
Dunn—who meets Preston to-night
* • .. _ * *
. Apropos the world’s champion pug. Jeffries
pbre tacks Rev. before his naine,' and is said to he the
finest, sample of muscular Christianity! extant. Inter-
viewed last month, he adduced Biblical justification for
prize-fighting, and reasoned that all the shining spots in
sacred history are set off by championship contests.
“ God had two sons, and one was against the bther,” said
Parson Jeffries, standing in the centre of a group of
bruisers. “ One was the Son of Light, the Lord of Hosts,
and the other was the Son of Darkness,, the prince of the
bottomless pit. They fought, .and the best son won."
The audience never having heard of that fight before
crowded eagerly around the clergyman. “ Then Adam
had two sons,” continued the shepherd, “ and they
fought; and then there was Abraham—he also had two
sons opposed to each other. Jacob, too, had a couple of
sons who were not in unison ; and,” said the old man
finishing up, “doesn’t the Bible say there shall be cities
against cities, and friends against friends, and nations
against nations.”
An amusing item of pug. news is that Austra-
lian fourth-raters M'Auliffe and Brophy fought for the
top-notch bruising honors of India at Calcutta, May 31.
It was a wild scramble from the jump, and only lasted
ljmin., when Mac. outed his bigger opponent. Brophy
was champion up to this light, through beating a big
fellow in Burmah while he (Brophy) was sickening for an
attack of smallpox that nearly blew his light out. Said
brevity of latest battle gave incipient boom a black eye
that it won’t soon get over. Unsophisticated chutnee
and curry-eaters evidently expected two men from the
same land and far away from home to fight on their
merits. Ridiculous! Why, Melb. pugs, won’t come over
to Sydney and go straight, or vice versa ; you’ve got to
get one from each province before you have ’em right,
and then it’s no “ moral ” that they’ll do the proper thing.
Apropos Australians fighting for the supremacy of other
countries. We had Slavin and Jackson at the same game
in England, and if Bob hadn’t specially barred him Peter
and Fitzsimmons would have shaped up in Yankeedom
for the supremac}' of that country and_ the world. Fitz.
and Oreedon and Fitz. and Jim Hall fought for the mid-
dle-weight laurels of America and the world, and Billy
Murphy and Griffo had a cut for similar distinction in the
feather class.
* * • *
“Bare’Uns”: Bulletin par. (23/6/’00) re Cor-
bett-Choynski fight near Benicia (Cal.) 11 years ago. Said
that Corbett used 2oz. and let Cho.vnski have driving-
gloves. To the uninitiated this would look like magnani-
mity on Corbett’s part, but he had the better of the deal,
as padding was a big protection for his hands and enabled
him to send in the heaviest punch without feeling it on
the knuckles. Choynski had nothing but the bare piece
of kid between his fists and whatever bony parts of
“Pompadour Jim’s” anatomy they made impact with.
Results proved the correctness of this view. Choynski
hits as hard as any man in the game, and Corbett is a
light hitter; former was cut and bruised everywhere
while latter, though bad enough, showed comparatively
few sig*s of stoush. Most people think bare fists cause
greater damage, but that is wrong : it has been shown
over and over again that l£oz. or 2oz. gloves will do in-
finitely more harm.
• • * *
Monday’s cables told that Australian sprint
premier Stan. Rowley (N.S.W.) had competed in the Eng.
100yds. supremacy at annual championship meeting on
Stamford Bridge Grounds (London) previous Saturday,
and, after winning his heat, finished fourth in the final.
Same source conveyed information that Yankee runners
appropriated 8 out of 13 championships contested. S.M.
Herald —up to date, as usual —referred to the meeting as
“ International Championships at Paris,” which under
title of Olympian Games, don’t take place till next and
following Sundays. Last Wednesday Rowley, at Wolver-
hampton, accounted for the then holder (Wadsley) of the
’Ome premiership at 100yds. by 2yds. in 10 2-5, which
made his chance in Saturday’s struggle look very good—
bar the possibility of Americans en route to Paris, up-
setting it. And this would seem to have occurred, as it is
hardly likely that there was a better Britisher than
Wadsley, except, perhaps, the Cambridge flier Hind, who
was credited with 9 4-5. Pritchard, of Calcutta, who
won honors in lOsec. and is said to be capable of 9 4-5,
might have competed, also Americans Barney, Wefers
and Duffy, both of whom have 9 4-5 opposite their names.
Rowley has traversed the 100 in lOsec. dead many a time,
and last Nov., at the Union Championships in Brisbane,
was clocked to do 9 9-10, a yard faster than evens. Soon
after reaching the old country Rowley went in a handi-
cap at Glasgow and was beaten by Jyd. Winner’s time
(probably with a good start) was lOsec. All things con-
sidered, Australia’s chance of carrying off one of the blue
ribbon events at Auteuil (France) doesn’t look too good.
• •< * m
M.L. Amateur Athletic Association members
have been much exercised over the question whether
umbrellas, rugs and sich are legitimate “ trophies,” and,
being unable to settle the matter among themselves, they
appealed to Union officials, who solemnly decided, after
much deliberation, that the articles mentioned were not
trophies “ within the meaning of the Act.” Apropos, a
year or two ago an amateur won a prize (unnamed on pro-
gramme) promised by a Balmain storekeeper, and upon
presenting the order was introduced to a large galvanised-
iron bath and asked whether he would have it sent or
take it with him. Matter came before Council of N.S.
Wales A.A.A. Chairman ruled that a trophy was some-
thing that could be inscribed, and upon its being pointed
out that that was possible with a bath he ruled again that
it was either a pendant or an addition to a sideboard.
• * • *
Two American athletes (Kraenzlein and Prin-
stein) have, at Philadelphia, broken the long-jump record,
with a clear 24ft. 7|in.
“ Nat ” : Was amused at an com-
menting on the “ Trudgeon ” (double-over-arm) stroke in
swimming. He says it is only fit for distances from 30 to
.60yds., or for finishing. Its ablest exponents rarely use
the method in longer races than 100yds. The only man
who depends upon it entirely, he remarks, is Australian
Freddy Lane, who is frequently so exhausted that he has
to be helped out of the water, sometimes bleeding freely
from the nostrils through the strain, and (continues the
’Ome scribe) he has been known to have been quite pro-
strate for hours after a contest. This will all be news to
N.S.W. swimmers—most of whom adopt the “ Trudgeon ”
style over any journey. Even schoolboys use it and
never complain of distressing consequences. Lane bleed-
ing and being prostrate for hours after a race is something
quite fresh; he never suffered that way here, though
many a time at his top speed from end to end of a long
stretch. It’s only a matter of getting used to the double
over-arm stroke.

Joe Darling is about to resume farming, and
may play very little more big cricket. It is stated that
he will settle either in the S.E. of S.A., or somewhere in
N.S.W. Darling gave up farming just prior to his acces-
sion to big cricket in 1893, and if hjs resumption of
country life debars him from active cricket Australia will
suffer a big loss. Few people know, by the way, that
Darling was on the brink of retirement in ’99 before the
last team went to England. He still runs his Adelaide
. cricket business.

Generally understood in Adelaide that S.A.
Cricket Association will not renew Ernest Jones’s retainer
r next Sept. “ Jonah ” gets £2 10s. a week, and confines
himself solely to playing cricket. A precarious living at
the best.

Australia’s erstwhile captain, Billy Murdoch,
came to light again recently with a score of 86 for London
County against Cambridge. Since he left Sussex,
Murdoch has practically retired, and, as he is now 45 and
fleshy, “ W.L.” hasn’t much more cricket in him.

Jessop, the Gloucester county team slogger, has
put up a record by scoring 157 runs in 60 minutes against a
West Indies Eleven. But then, Percy M'Donnell, of yore,
could do that sort of thing with less recklessness than
Jessop shows.

Details of Englishman Hayward’s compilation
of 1000 runs in first-class cricket before the end of May
are to hand. On last day he notched 92, thus exceeding
the necessary four figures by 74. Though this aggregate
is greater than W.G.’s for same period in ’95, it isn’t quite
'so good a record, as Hayward played three more innings.
Grace notched over 200 twice, and on two other occasions
exceeded 100. Hayward didn’t reach 200 for one innings,
but he six times passed 100. During his first seven innings
he never totted up less than 55, and was run out once.
Subsequently only 14 runs were credited to him for three
innings, but in the last week he made amends, and with
.a similar number of opportunities against Sussex and
Gloucestershire hit up 278. His average for 13 innings
(twice not out) was 97'63.
Gov. Beauchamp, while up North in N.S.W.,
the other day, was trotted out to the inevitable football-
match. A committeeman, with a burning desire to make

Elain that “ one team was tryin’ to put that there leather
all between them two sticks!” “Quite so,” said N.S.
Wales’ Pretty Boy, with a twinkle in his eye, “ the game
was played once or twice before I left England.”
* * • *
“Bike”: Apropos 16st. 101 b. Smyth’s record-
breaking feats {vide Bulletin par. 7/7/’00). Though highly
meritorious for a man of his age and bulk, the perform-
ances are nothing to enthuse over—all things weighed.
Figures wiped out were put up years ago, when turf-
tracks only existed, and the banked asphalt article was
hardly dreamt of. Leaving motor-pace out of the ques-
tion altogether, human-pacing, as it is now, never
occurred. Said that Wally Kerr (Sydney), whose “bests”
most of those eclipsed records were, expressed surprise at
their standing till to-day. He made them six years ago, and
believes they’ve often been beaten since—on grass as well
as asphalt.
• • * •
The system of handicapping by the N.S.W.
gun-clubs is one calculated to play right into a schemer’s
hands. Say a man is placed on 24yds. at the beginning of
the season, he stays there right through, unless he wins a
trophy. Consequently, if a man is out for the coin, he
can back his gun and fall out when it comes to a chance
of his winning the trophy—thereby staying on the 24yds.
all through the season, no matter how good his shooting.

At a recent London pigeon-show, five “tipplers”
were listed at £lOOO each ! and two fantails at £7OO each.
Which recalls the case in which a wealthy Sydneyite, a
bit of a fancier, had deputed a P. and O. skipper to pro-
cure for him a few pairs of a very rare and costly variety
of pigeon. When the skipper, in course of time, brought
out the birds from England, he sent his chief-steward off
with them to consignee, who happened to be dressing
when the steward arrived, and, being told by the maid
that “ there was a man waiting with pigeons,” said, “ All
right—take ’em to the cook 1 ” Later on, in town, he met
the skipper, who inquired what he thought of the pigeons.
“ Pigeons ? ” exclaimed the other—•“ good Heavens ! were
those the fancies ? ” And he rushed home, only to find
that the prized importations were already defunct and
under pie-crust. He was a rich man, but a £5O pigeon-
pie was a staggerer.

The Acetylene Miners’ Lamps, introduced by
the Acetylene Gas 00. of Australia, Ltd., have proved a
great success and are now largely used in the mines
throughout the colony. The company has lately been
rushed with orders for lighting up gold-mining dredges
owing to the many advantages offered by the light for
that purpose. It would be well for mine and dredge
owners to ascertain full information as to cost, etc., from
the Head Office of the Company, 36 Pitt-street, Sydney.*
To prevent Bubonic Plague wear Metzler’s
“ Protective Anklets,” a certain protective against
the access of vermin and disease germs from the
ground. They are worn by several members of the
medical profession, including doctors of the staff of
the Board of Health, and are worn also by mem-
bers of the N.S.W. Police Force on duty in the
plague-infected quarters of Sydney, Obtainable
from all first-class chemists or from Head Office
of the Proprietary, 14 Castlereagh-street, Sydney.*
The sudden upheaval of the Chow and
consequent subversion of the Foreign Devil, has
moved Messrs. Sands and McDougall, Ltd., of
Melbourne, to publish an up-to-date map of the
centre of disturbance and the territories adjacent
thereto. Japan, Corea, Manchuria and the
eastern portion of China are shown, with list of
distances, railways made and projected—-chiefly
projected—and other useful knowledge. To any-
one who wishes to follow the course of the trouble
it is a useful work.
When the news of the capture of Major
Bayly was discussed at Sydney Barracks, a very
distinguished officer remarked : “ Well, I think it
was quite time that Major Bayly took the steps
he did, to let the New South Wales authorities
know what had become of him.” By the way,
Bayly put Jawbone Neild under arrest for writing
a letter to the papers; now Bayly has been put
under arrest for robbing a Boer hen-roost.
Apropos, what has become of the report of the
tribunal which so long ago investigated the row
between Jawbone Neild and a brother officer ?
As Nelson Illingworth, the clever Sydney
sculptor, after an extended period of unusually
hard luck, is now suffering from severe illness, a
meeting was held on Monday last at the Hotel
Australia with the view of taking steps to assist a
man who was never behindhand m succoring"
others. It was resolved that a fund for the pur-
pose in question be formed upon lines to be
decided by a committee including ex-Speaker
Abbott, Messrs. B. R. Wise, Bruce Smith, and
numerous other representatives of politics, art,
music and literature.
The Belgian police have amused them-
selves by testing the “murderous” possibilities of
the revolver which young Sipido lately discharged
at the Prince of Wales. The weapon was re-
loaded with cartridges taken from the pocket of
the bloodthirsty young crank, and was then tested
in a series of attempts upon the imaginary life of
a hospital corpse, Half the cartridges missed fire
and the bullets from the others trickled so feebly
from the barrel that they didn’t even penetrate
the corpse’s chest-protector. Their career terrain*
ated between the Waistcoat and the shirt of the
defunct. It is probable, of .course, that Sipido’s
foolishness had a deadly intent; all the same, the
incident reminds one of another young crank who
sent a thrill through the British nation some 30
years ago. The poor fellow rushed up to the
Queen’s carriage with a small brass-bound volume
in one hand and a humorous, broken revolver in
the other, and was consigned to a lunatic asylum
for his trouble. David James and Tom Thorne,
favorite burlesque artists and joint managers of
London Vaudeville in those days, hit off the
terrible event in a topical verse 1
“ You’ve heard about the villain bold, the vilest ever seen,
Who had a mind to ’sassinate our well-belov6d Queen ;
With pocket-book and pistol, too—but he forgot to cock
it, . .
And when they took him up they found the trigger in his
pocket.”
Remarkable how history repeats itself. _ Topical
rhymes re the attack on the Queen’s Heir Appar-
ent will go somewhat like this :
■When Britain talks of Sipido the cheek of Britain pales,
For he’s the wretch who tried to “pot” ’Bertedward,
Prince of Wales ;
And had his bullets hit the mark (the thought inspireth
loathing),
They might have done some damage to his victim’s under-
clothing.
The Building and Engineering Journal
(Melb.) publishes the following letter from Prof.
Waddell, a leading American bridge engineer
Messrs. Monash and Anderson, 49 Elizabeth-street,
Melbourne, Australia.
Gentlemen, —Since dictating a letter to you this morn-
ing I have read over the conditions for the competition on
Sydney (North Shore) Bridge, and have come to the con-
clusion that it is a case of “ heads I win and tails you
lose.” Let us analyse the thing. The city (Govt.) offers
prizes to the amount of about 7500d01. for the two best
plans. Let us assume that there are 20 plans prepared.
Each plan, if properly got up, will .cost in cash, say,
1500d01., making a total of 30,000d01. This is the value
of the work done by the competitors for the city, and
they pay 7600d01. for it. Again, what does each bidder
have to do in addition to the preparation of the plans? He
has to putin a bid on the work, and will be called upon to
back that bid with a 50,000d01. deposit; but if his design
is the most satisfactory, he has no assurance that the
contract will be given to him, because the city buys the
plans outright and can call for bids in competition on
these plans. The city should have given the engineering
with the usual 5 per cent, fee to the successful bidder in
addition to the £lOOO prize. Had such been the case, it
would have been worth while to compete, but as it stands,
it is not. We have decided that under the circumstances
it will not pay us to touch the work. If you think best,
you can send this letter to the Government official in
charge of the project. I feel certain that they will get no
designs from really reliable parties and competent bridge
engineers in the United States,
v Very respectfully yours,
(Signed) J. A. L. WADDELL.
He looks on the competition as a kind of depart-
mental confidence trick, and there is solid ground
for the opinion. Some years ago competitive
designs were invited for the Hawkesbury College ;
after the prize was allotted a change of Ministry
occurred, and, for economy, the erection was
made to plans supplied by N.S.W. Works Depart-
ment. The cost complete was practically the
same for a much inferior structure to that repre-
sented in the winning design.

At Poverty Point.
Marchesi, The Bulletin hears on high authority, is not
too enthusiastic over Amy Castles and her vaunted voice.
Perhaps Madame expected much. But she couldn’t have
expected anything finfer than the Australian puffsters had
promised her.
Lalla Miranda’s recent Italian opera deb (it in London
was, according to Signor Della Robbia (in Sydney-D.T.),
an unqualified success. The part was Gilda, in “ Rigo-
letto,” and the lady, it appears, though on her first entry
“ suggestive of one of Raphael’s angels,”. was rather
coldly received, and showed some consequent nervous-
ness, until her rendering of the beautiful “Caronome”
brought down the house. “It would be absolutely im-
possible,” says the critic mentioned, “to hear it more
tenderly or passionately delivered, or with more consum-
mate vocal and artistic and dramatic expression.” As
regards the histrionic side of the performance, “ all her
actions,” it is stated, “ were spontaneous and subtle, and
one had not to agree that she was doing the right things
with her arms and hands, but that any other actions than
those adopted would be impossible.” Her acting, it is
added, recalled that of Tietjens, and the summing-up is
that the lady is “ an emphatic artistic success.”
It is remarked that Nance O’Neil has not yet made stage
love or allowed it to be made to her, and there are no kisses
in the bill. The Australian public yearns to see Nance
make love to somebody. The Bulletin is ready to buy
the house for first night.
“ Stroller” : The “ School for Scandal” reminded me of
Garrick’s advice to Bannister : “You may fool the town
in tragedy, but beware of comedy.” It was not so much
lightness as variety that was wanting. It was in two or
three keys—already familiar from “ Magda” and “ Eliza-
beth.” Nance’s was the first Lady Teazle I have seen who
has failed to get a round for her line to Joseph to leave
honor out of the question. Because it was no sudden
lapse out of coquetry into seriousness—she had been
serious from the beginning, and, therefore, the audience
were not surprised. Also, the quarrel scenes, by too much
of the same earnestness, were changed into mere man-
and-wife squabbles—which are not comic at all, to any-
one who has been there. The comedy arises from the
feeling that it is a mere contest of wit—with no life or
death on the issue. Married folk will appreciate the dis-
tipction. Realism turns it into drama—at least to the
more sensitive of the audience, probably to the married
portion. This was proved by “ hushes ” interrupting the
laughter at Joseph’s pretended condolences with Sir
Peter. These were from people sorry for Sir Peter.
Realism is as fatal to 18th-century comedy as was Daudet’s
remark that “ he was sorry for the frog ” to the Jumping
Frog story. But no one believes it a real frog, the humor
is the resourcefulness of his owner. In the same way,
make Teazle v. Teazle a real divorce case, and you are too
sorry for either to enjoy it as comedy.
Regina Nagel hopes to make her first appearance in
London next Nov., at a concert where she will get a fair
start as the rival of Clara Butt and Ada Crossley. It
appears that Marchesi’s faith in the one-time timid
warbler of “Ben Bolt” has grown stronger than ever.
After hearing Butt sing in Paris the other week, Marchesi
declared that her pupil could give points to the big
contralto, but Nagel took the flattery with several grains
of salt. “ They talk like that about all of us —before we
come out,” she said.
Tom Fitzgerald sends from London a programme for the
first ten performances of the Covent Garden Opera
season. Melba led off as Mimi in “La Bohfeme”(in Italian)
and was “ great,” says compatriot Tom Fitz. Three days
later Melba sang Marguerite in “ Faust,” and on the
Monday following Mme. Miranda (Mile. Lalla Miranda no
longer) made her debut as Gilda in “ Rigoletto,” and
scored “ a great success,” according to friend Tom’s pro-
gramme notes. The cast for “ Der Ring des Nibelungen ”
series (4 operas) included Frau Schumann-Heink, the
contralto, who was lately concerting in Canada with
Antoinette Trebelli.
Oarados, the London Referee critic, had something
kind and thoughtful to say about Dion Boucicault’s per-
formance in “ His Excellency the Governor,” a play which
didn’t make a big success. “To the first class, which is
so very exclusive, Mr. Dion Boucicault cannot be admitted,
but he is either a first-rate second-rate actor, or a second-
rate first-rate actor, and please understand that I am
placing the rate as high as possible. I have never seen
Mr. Boucicault play any part badly; I have never seen
him play any part other than well, and I have never seen
him play any part in all sorts of parts better than he plays
His Excellency’s private secretary.” Five inches taller,
and with physical strength in proportion, Dion Boucicault
could have been a mighty mummer in comedy or tragedy.
The Bulletin has always worshipped the great little man.
F.: Re your “ Sundry Shows” complaint that Nance’s
declamatory Magda seemed ‘ ‘ out of the picture,” a carping
critic of the criticism told you that the massive lady
would be unnatural if she were not “ out of the picture.”
The picture, said the C. 0., is the parental home from
which Magda has been long exiled, and where, on her
return, she is out of harmony with her humdrum sur-
roundings. Now, I would say that the play is the picture,
and the “ home ” setting happens to be the only scene in
the play. Call the home the picture if you like. Magda
is the central figure. She returns, according to the dra-
matist, in a subdued spirit. The dialogue tells us that
Magda has on some previous nights gazed wistfully at the
windows of her old home until the lights were extin-
guished. At last she is persuaded to come along and be
reconciled to her father. The brilliant prima-donna con-
sents to sleep under her father’s roof. She is inclined to
be a new Magda pro tem , no doubt about that, for when
her father starts to cut up rough on the subject of
her past the dramatist makes her say, “ Now I
am the old Magda again.” Does Miss Nance O’Neil, as
Magda, ever give a convincing performance of the woman
who had kept watch outside the home ere she made up
her mind to re-enter it ? Does she ever suggest a soft
side to her nature ? Is there an air of reality about her
show of submission to her father and the precious pastor ?
If not, she is “ out of the picture ” —right out of it.

“Wat! noe wunne hyah?” Now, Mary’s long-nosed,
wooden-headed son was not the handsome, short-nosed
soldier of Mistah Plimmah, and he did not talk like a
“colonial experiences” From his polygot breeding he
would most likely have said: “Hech.'mon! Personne
pas ici n’est ce pas? Som< piddy vas not hierans, aint it?”
Melb. : Chas. Clark here experiences the fate of many
other worthy artistes. His powers do not decline, but
his audience slides away. The younger generation.
“Viator” : The Belles of New York must always have
one idolised English actor. In my time they were Mon-
tague, Rignold, Bellew.
Holdar M. : I fancy the trained warbler of the Austra-
lian platform doesn’t live too luxuriously these times.
There are hordes of her —and him—in every large capital,
and, albeit their “ regular fee” ranges from three to five
guineas per single concert-engagement, 7s. 6d. to 10s. is
mostly offered—and gladly accepted. The flavor of
ad. alone induces many of ’em to sing for nothing at
benefit entertainments, and the majority despair of ever
raising sufficient “ oof ” to pay their passages to happier
lands. But is there a happier land ?
Sarah Bernhardt, always up-to-date, is introducing
cinematograph effects into a battle-scene in her latest
production, “ L’Aiglon.” Also, a London manager has
tried some animated war-pictures in a topical patriotic
drama.
They have had a Jap dramatic co. performing at the
Bijou Theatre, New York. The little brown artists play
(in their own language of course) a comedy which is
mainly an eastern version of “ Pygmalion and Galatea.”
Jap adaptations of “Hamlet” and “ Merchant of Venice ”
are promised.
According to rumor (says Sydney D.T.), Mme. Melba
returns to her native land with George Musgrove’s
new company. . Her intention is to give two performances
a week “ for the charities,” so as to heap coals of fire on
the heads of talkative countrymen who declared that only
gold galore would bring her home again.
Mrs. Bernard-Beere’s new husband is the son of a Church
of England canon.
Miss Edith Berry, now at Melb. Her Majesty’s, although
a handsome and shapely girl, bears a striking resemblance
to the caricatures of her father, the extinct democratic
volcano.
The customs of the aristocracy, as lately seen in “ East
Lynne ”at a Sydney theatre, are at least original. Lady
Isabel, in a low-down gauze ball-dress aiid diamond neck-
lace, gives the butcher’s orders, that tradesman evidently
standing somewhere outside on the doormat just as the
evening guests are coming in. Again, Sir Francis strug-
gles with his overcoat in Lady Isabel’s presence before
removing his bell-topper, and thus gives the impression
that the girlhood’s home of Lady Isabel does not possess
an entrance hall. This aristocratic conduct so impresses
Dampier’s stout lady that she forthwith takes the fatal
step which leads to her dying; healthily in the last act,
looking as if Sin agreed well with her.
R. 0. Carton’s new comedy, .“ Lady Wentworth’s Ex-
periment,” ought to be an Australian success one of these
days if Brough gets hold of it. The heroine, unjustly
divorced by her “ bad egg” of a husband, takes a situa-
tion as cook, and appears most of the time in natty kit-
chen attire. Old friend Dion Boucicault is said to give a
fine performance of the noble “ bad egg.”
An interesting theatrical divorce-suit—Synge v. Synge
—was decided the other day. Petitioner, a daughter of
General Stuart, sued for divorce from Major Synge on the
ground of adultery and desertion, and when a jury had
certified to the adultery, his Honor sat on his own to hear
the arguments re desertion. Seemed that the Major,
after two or three periods of absence (in India) from his
wife, retired from the army and proposed to settle down
to domestic happiness in London, but the lady refused, as
she had refused four years previously, to live with him on
other than sisterly terms. Also she was determined to
take to the stage. The Major, finding his wife struck on
the stage, and no longer struck on him, refused to live
with her as a mere attendant “ brother.” Hence her plea
of desertion. Judge Jeune, in refusing a decree nisi, put
the law this way : “ The object of married life was ex-
pressed in the marriage service, and was none the less
true because they were the utterances of a more plain-
spoken age than the present; and while human nature
remained what it was he would hold a husband had a
right to decline to submit to a demand by his wife that
he should live with her as husband only in name. The
conclusion he had come to was tha the respondent did
not desert his wife but rather that she deserted him, and
that if he did desert her he did so with reasonable cause.”
As for the adultery it had happened between times of the
wife’s refusal to live up to the marriage service and there-
fore didn’t count. Moreover, the frigid matron had not
known of it when she finally repulsed the gallant Major.
Trebelli’s mother waa a Frenchwoman, her stage-name
oeing partly her family-name—Gilbert —spelt backwards.
Then she married . the Italian violinist, and hyphenated
his name to her assigned stage-name, making it Trebelli-
Bettini. So the daughter of that musical union is pro-
perly Mile Antoinette Bettini.
Some U.S. towns have proscribed the play of “ Sapho”
since Olga Nethersole’s appearance before the Court in
connection therewith, and in several of these Mrs. Lang-
try has been forbidden to present “ The Degenerates.”
The Newark (N. J.) authorities having decided to permit
one presentation of her play, on probation, before them-
selves as censors, she angrily cancelled her engagement
in that place. If there was anything giddy in the piece
the beaks wanted to get the benefit of it. “ Sapho.” (the
book) was interdicted at Melb. Customs when the late J.
Beef Patterson was in office.
The Big Scrub Amateur Opera Co. lately revived
“ Pinafore.” Sir Joseph Porter’s hat was a tea-cosy, and
Captain Corcoran was so awkward with his sword that he
unbuckled it and dropped it over side of ship, and, when
he wanted it later on, he calmly stepped into the ocean and
dredged up his weapon amid much applause. A soprano
behind the scenes used to sing the tenor’s high notes
while he gesticulated appropriately in front.
“La Tosca” has been operaticised by the young
and brilliant Italian composer, Puccini. The five acts of
Sardou’s tragedy have been compressed into three, and
the story is all the stronger for the excision of certain
scenes.
Irving makes one think of Kean when he said, “ They
give me £2OO a week, but I acted better round the barns
for fifteen shillings.”
“Paris”: Du Maurier took the name, “Trilby,” from
the favorite mare of M. de Camors, in Feuillet’s romance
of this title, but Feuillet took it from a tale in some vogue
nearly 100 years ago.
Charles Mathews had a most agreeable way with him.
From his published diary : “ June 1. —Called on Jackson
to pay him the £3O I owe. Ended by borrowing £4O
more.”
Has anybody ever heard of the star actress who didn’t
take to the stage much against the will of her parents ?
Sothern, of “Lord Dundreary” fame, was the champion
jokist of the stage. At New York during one season he
was in a theatre within easy reach of another playhouse
where an intimate friend was “ starring ” in melodrama.
It was Sothern’s habit, between acts at his own house, to
run across to his friend’s theatre, no matter in what
character he was dressed, walk upon the stage at any
period of the play, and, putting his hand on the shoulder
of the hero of the melodrama, say to him with great
earnestness: “You are sure you will marry the girl in
the end?” And then, pretending to have received an
affirmative answer, he would add with the emotion of a
“heavy father” : “ God bless you, my boy ; I always said
you were a good fellow.”
The first stage appearance of Elenezer Ward,
the S.A. politician, was as an amateur at a special benefit
got up at Melb. Royal for G. V. Brooke. The play was
Colman’s “ Poor Gentleman.” In the cast with Ward
were Jas. Smith (of the Argos), Edgar Ray (Punch), John
Edwards (solicitor), H. J Chambers (solicitor), John
Hayward (father of Mrs. R. Stewart, jun., and known on
the stage as Deorwyn), A. C. Fox (Hobson’s Bay Railway
Station Master), and other prominent citizens. A cheque
representing £220 was presented on the stage to Brooke
by Jas. Smith, but it was whispered that it was but so
much waste paper, as the cash proceeds were seized by
creditors.
G. V. Brooke, who was a prodigious favorite in Aus-
tralia, and who also did well in America, failed to establish
himself in London. He made a hit on his first night with
Othello, but he could not maintain the good impression
in subsequent performances, aud finally smashed himself
by quarrelling with Macready, then all-powerful. John
Mori ey wrote of Brooke after his death : “He was a melo-
dramatic actor. He must have been a noble fellow
(referring to the foundering of the s.s. London), but he
could not act Shakespeare.” Laman Blanchard, critic of
London Daily Telegraph, records in his published diary
that Brooke’s Othello was a mistake. Helen Faucit
(Lady Martin), who had acted with the tragedian, states
in her reminiscences that he was incapable of bringing
any brain power to bear on his work. It is possible that
Brooke was a better actor in Australia than anywhere
else. The climate suited his voice, and he took to the
people the moment he landed. But he fell away sadly
during his last two years on the Continent. Brooke
began his stage career as an infant phenomenon—a hand-
some boy with a lovely voice.
Dampier’s popularity in the “ Profesh ” is wonderful.
Given as actors are to the depreciation of each other, not
a malicious word is ever heard of Alfred. Much the same
might be said of George Musgrove, the secret probably
being that both men keep the members of their companies
always in pay, even if the caste allows of little work.
When Henry Irving had reached the top-notch in his
profession, and all Londonfivas flocking to see his Hamlet,
he met Charles Dillon one day, in the Strand, and spoke
to him. Dillon responded, with an air of non-recognition.
“You don’t seem to remember me,” said Irving, “ but I
was a stock-actor at the Edinburgh Royal, when you were
starring there. My name is Irving.” “ Ah, yes,” drawled
Dillon, “ I think I recollect. Doing anything now ? ”
Walter Montgomery' comitted suicide at his hotel in
London. He had invested large savings in Melbourne ;
and he did well in America on his way to England. He
married a handsome woman before leaving the States,
and on reaching London took a theatre to play Shake-
speare .at the fag end of the season. The venture was a
failure. Without closing the house, he fired a pistol into
his mouth one afternoon. Just before the fatal act, he
sang out to his wife, in the next room, “ Good-bye,
Lily ! ” and that, she said, was all she knew about it.
The true cause of Montgomery’s melancholy end will now
never be known. He was a beautiful elocutionist, with a
picturesque stage figure. Many of his Australian ad-
mirers thought that if he had been a level-headed man
(which he was not) he would have got ahead of Henry
Irving, who was never intended by Nature to be an actor,
though he achieved a triumph of industry.
When Henry Irving made his first London appearance
under Bateman at the Lyceum, the wily lessee advised
him to adopt some grotesque peculiarities, on the prin-
ciple that half the critics would condemn and the other
half applaud, so that in the midst of the hullaballoo he
would become a personage. Irving accordingly said
“ grole ” for “ growl,” and “ ole ” for “ owl,” and rapidly
reached the pinnacle of fame.
One of the oldest buildings in Sydney, the Creswick
Club Hotel, Bent-st., is about to be demolished. Long ago
it was the residence of a leading Government official, and
it obtained its present name from the fact that Creswick,
the old-time tragedian, lived there when fulfilling a
Sydney engagement.

WHEN YOU’VE NOBODY TO THANK.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When you’ve nobody to thank for the balance at your
bank,
With a ready-money screw and no paper overdue,
When your neighbors all respect you as they should,
And your darling wife adores you, and neither scolds nor
bores you—
Is there any virtue then in being good !
But when your coat is seedy, and you’re feeling sick and
weedy,
And you’re seeking for a billet,' with a hundred more to
fill it,
Do your neighbors then respect you as they should ?
No, they almost fail to greet you when by some mischance
they meet you —
There’s a lot of virtue then in being good !
You will find your Christian brother shuts one eye and
winks the other,
And his smile is so sardonic, one would think the plague
bubonic
Was upon you by the strangeness of his mood ;
For the Christian up-to-date is on Dives proud to wait,
While Lazarus may whistle for his food.
G.R.L.
“ Ex-R.E.” to The Bulletin
Re the Boer mercenary question (B. 23/6/’00). Every
soldier who serves for pay is a mercenary. It is not only
I who say this. Napier printed it 60 years ago m his
“History of the Peninsular War.” In Wellingtons
Peninsular Army, and also at Waterloo was a German
Legion carefully'kept up to 20,000 strong. There were
also in the aforesaid P.A. 6 brigades of Portuguese. As
far as the statement that foreigners cannot enlist in
British regiments in a greater proportion than 1 to 50
British ; there may be such a rule on paper, but I know
for a fact that 40 years ago quite 30 per cent, of eight
battalions in the British army were Germans. As for the
Indian native army, there are very few natives of British
India among them. The famous Gurkas are foreigners
from the independent State of Nipal, while whole regi-
ments are! made up of Pathans. (Afghans), Persians, and
even Turkmans from the Russian territory beyond the
Oxus.


The beautiful and elaborately-fitted yacht
Noma, which won such reputation for speed in
Cuban waters during the war, and was recently
bought cheap by Gerber, Michaut and Co., of
Sydney, now goes into the pearling trade on behalf
of a Thursday Island purchaser.
Even as the tattoo of the British drum is
to be heard resounding in every quarter of the
globe where law’n’order (and trade) can be intro-
duced, so is David Storer and Son’s Tattoo Oil
finding its way to every part of the world where
civilisation and sheep flourish. And even as the
British claim their empire to be the greatest of
world civilising powers, so do the proprietors of
Tattoo Oil claim it to be the one absolutely safe
wool-marking or sheep-branding oil. *
The National Cash Register Company has
recently bought out the Hamilton Cash Register
Company, taking over the patents, stock-in-trade,
and all business for the sum of 245,000 dollars.
The N.C.R. Co. is now building a very large fac-
tory at Hamilton, Canada, and another in Ger-
many ; and with the grand capital of 5,000,000
dollars at its back to make it feel sound and com-
fortable as money can make it, it keeps going
ahead generally the world over. The co.’s record
month of business was put up last May.*
New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.,
Ltd., reports that the wheat market is remarkably dull.
Buyers resolutely refuse to give more than what they
term “ current rates,” while the sellers purse their lips
and stick out vigorously for the prices of the end of last
month, and as a consequence the sales have been few.
The co. is not particularly fond of posing as a prophet,
but it ventures to opine that owners of prime samples
who refused to be beguiled by the blandishments of the
would-be buyers are not likely to suffer by their firmness,
as it has an offer for a large parcel of wheat at equal to
2s. lOd. per bushel, and is now communicating with
sellers. Quotations may be given as—-prime, 2s. lOd. to
2s. IOJd. ; P.A.Q., 2s. 9d. ; pinched and chickwheat down
to 2s. 3d. per bushel.*
The recurrence of the Hawkesbury
(N.S.W.) floods will no doubt revive the largely
mythical yarns of the Hawkesbury floods of 1806.
Phillip’s narrative of the “evidences, 90ft. above
the bed of the river, of old-time inundations,” will
doubtless also be trotted-out, in defiance of the
fact that the river-banks are only 30 or 40ft. high.
It is, however, true that during the sixth flood, a
6-year-old boy was rescued from a floating house-
top. And, just as hundreds of children claim to
be the first child, still living, born in Victoria, so,
up to within a few years ago, Hawkesbury resi-
dents by the score used to claim to have been the
youngster in question. As a matter of fact, the
salved juvenile grew up to be William Bradley
(one time owner of Wentworth Court, Sydney),
who died under singularly interesting circum-
stances in that city a few years back.
Lord Carrington got a good deal of fun
out of the recent second reading of the Colonial
Marriages (Deceased Wife’s Sister) Bill in the
Lords. “The House,” he said, “ might imagine
the case of a rich Australian squatter who had
married his _ deceased wife’s sister, a man
honoured in his own country, who might even be
made the first Australian peer. (Laughter). He
might buy himself a house in Curzon-street on a
long lease, and, finding it not large enough, might
buy at the back some freehold land, and build a
ballroom. An accident might happen to him. He
might be killed. His lawyers would then tell his
son and heir—“ So long as you are in the leasehold
house in Curzon-street you are legitimate, but if
you go into your ballroom, that does not belong to
you, because you are not legitimate—the property
being freehold. No doubt your father was a peer,
but you cannot inherit a freehold estate, and a
peerage is a freehold estate, and, therefore, you
cannot be a peer.” Carrington’s “borak” pro-
bably contributed a good deal to the subsequent
carrying of the reading.
