The Foreign-Trade Deficit.
The other day, Sydney Telegraph rose with an
immortal truth in its mouth, and pointed out that
N.S.W. had now accumulated a visible deficit of
over £4,300,000, and that, but for the swindlesome
policy of charging ordinary expenditure to a tem-
porary loan-fund (one of the many public frauds of
the awful Reid regime), the visible shortage would
be over £5,000,000. The figures are quite beyond
dispute j in fact, when the (Finance Committee
finishes investigating the horrors of Reid’s finance,
the amount will probably be more than this.
There is “the deficit,” so-called, of over £2,000,000,
of which at least half is the result of G. Reid’s
mental and moral confusion; the shortage on the
Centennial Park suspense account; the ditto on the
Martin-Place suspense account; the payments to
various sinking-funds which RfelD didn’t pay ; the
huge shortage on the Civil Service Superannuation
Fund; and the deficit of this year, arising
through extra contingent and bubonic ex-
penditure. Then, in addition, there is an item
which the Telegraph did not include —a
further deficit of £500,000 or £700,000, which
will probably arise if the Cash Basis fraud
is put right, and the shiftless system of carrying
the unpaid bills forward is abandoned. The
Telegraph tells another great truth when it says
that N.S.W. wants more revenue, not only to pay
off its past deficits, but to keep its accounts sol-
vent in the future. The Bulletin has been say-
ing that for years, and it is a pity the Telegrath
didn’t start saying it at the same time The Bul-
letin started. The great truth it doesn’t tell is
that it and the S.M. Herald and the News, and
their joint and several Foreign Shoddy fetish are
the prime causes of the whole melancholy busi-
ness. These papers demanded a violent form of
Freetrade, which involved the sacrifice of much
customs revenue. They dared not demand a Is. 6d.
in the £ income-tax and a 3d. in the £ land-tax to
make the loss good ; their loudest whoop and the
point on which they claimed most credit was that
their tin god could do with much less revenue
than the Protectionist Dibbs, being sustained by
the inward grace of Calico-Jimmyism; and that he
had “lightened the country’s burdens.” They
dared not propose cutting down Civil Ser-
vice salaries 50 per cent, to make up for
the deficiency in revenue which their system
involved ; their next loudest whoop was that
Freetrade was a Jolly and Fat policy which meant
decent wages for everybody without serious bur-
dens on anybody. They dared not suggest econo-
mising by stopping the charitable vote and throw-
ing the sick and aged out into the street. Their
party had bought office by the reiterated lie that
the old Protectionist Government was wildly ex-
travagant, and that they could give a better
administration of affairs for less money. _ So they
had to work strange dodges to make it appear
that their Foreign-trade policy supplied enough
revenue to pay the country’s way, and to keep up
some fleeting semblance of Jolly-and-Fatism.
They cut down the road-maintenance vote from
£1,100,000 to £550,000 and let the public highways
go to ruin. They charged current expenditure to
loans wholesale. They invented the Cash Basis
fraud and the Temporary Loan delusion. And
by the showing of their own leading supporter
and fugleman, the Telegraph, they left the
accumulated deficit fully £1,000,000 perhaps
£2,000,000 —worse than they found it. The Tele-
graph now warns Lyne seriously that this kind of
thing must not go on—which is a solid fact of long
standing. But it does not say what Lyne is to do.
If the revenue is to meet all legitimate expendi-
ture in future, and leave something over to pay off
old shortages, the land and income taxes must be
made to yield at least double what they do, or
some new sources of revenue good for about half-a-
million a year must be discovered, or N.S.W.
must go bald-headed for Protection. In fact, it
probably needs to do all three things. Its present
troubles arose out of the frantic effort to make it
appear, by faking the public accounts, that the
Telegraph and Herald and News and the
Foreign-trade party in general were not departing
from the truth when they said that N.S.W. could
pay its way on Reid’s Foreign-trade tariff,' and
that they were not the prize Ananias family of
Australia when they said that Foreign-trade
meant solvency and prosperity. Now the Tele-
graph denomstrates by great masses of indubit-
able figures that the Foreign-trade epoch
has brought N.S.W. finances to terrible
disaster. And it demands that N.S.W. shall
fight for the maiiitenance of the Foreign-trade
policy in the Commonwealth. And, being to some
extent the organ of Fat, it doesn’t advocate
imposing graduated land and income taxes on a
big scale, and huge absentee taxes, arid the like, to
fill the gap. It simply says that Lyne ought to
do something. So he ought. If Lyne were an
absolute monarch the obvious thing to begin with
would be to hang or exile the Foreign-trade editors
whose bad advice made the great N.S.W. deficit
inevitable, and who swore for four years that their
dreadful Foreign-trade Government was quite
solvent and thus prevented a remedy being applied
earlier.
; , +
The Australian Star’s excuse for upholding the
Transvaal contingents when the hot fit of blood-
drunkenness was on it, and yet for throwing cold-
water on the proposal to send a contingent to
China now that the cold fit has come, is that the
Chinese trouble “does not in any sense menace
.the foundations of our empire.” Well, does the
Star propose to heave such a blasting insult at the
British empire as to imply that a war with the
150,000 population of the Boer States (about one-
third the population of Sydney), or even an up-
rising of the whole 500,000 people of Dutch descent
in South Africa (about as many as the population of
Melbourne), menaced the foundations of the
British empire ? There are about 40,000,000 white
people in Britain alone. All the unpleasant things
The Bulletin has said about England in the whole
of its career don’t add up to this tremendous insult.
The Bulletin has called the Englishman unjust
and greedy, but it never suggested, as the Star
does by implication, that he was a cur, or that 80
of him were in any danger of not proving a match
for one Boer.
The opinion of Attorney-General Wise that mem-
bers of N.S.W. Leg. Assembly are bound to pay
income-tax on their Parliamentary salaries, less
the statutory exemption, accords with the opinion
of the public, but does not accord with the word-
ing of the Act under which the salaries are paid.
The Act describes the salary as a “ reimbursement
of expenses incurred,” and in other walks of life
such reimbursements are not taxable as income.
Reimbursements for expenses incurred is, as Syd-
ney D.T. remarks, “tne merest pretence,” but
that .is not the question. The money is drawn
under one Act and the other Act excludes money
so drawn from ranking as taxable income. The
other day an ex-solicitor, who is M.L. A., pleading
for re-enrolment before the Full Court, altogether
omitted mention of his Parliamentary salary
when making a full statement of his sources of in-
come, and their Honors, who knew his political
status, suggested no correction. The public all
along called the pay salary or wages, but the Tory
opponents of payment of members, backed up by
a kindred press, coated the objectionable measure
with phrases meant to conceal the fact that they
had to give in to a democratic demand. Now,
when they want to make the paid members dis-
gorge some of the cash, they describe the phrases
as the “merest pretence.”
The Story of Private Ghoul.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[Based on the accounts given by some of the Australian
troops of their own doings, and published with evident
pride in certain Australian Jingo papers.]
The old man’s eyes flashed brightly and his bosom swelled
with pride,
The wrinkled hand no longer dangled listless by his s ide
But grasped my coat-sleeve firmly, whilst I patient heard
once more
How his son, Private Ghoul, became a hero of the war !
’Twas at Shrapnelpompomfontein, ended was the fight at
last,
Great guns had ceased to vomit, droning shells to sear
and blast;
The foe had fled ere nightfall, and our victory was com-
plete—
And Private Ghoul’s battalion rested from the smoke and
heat.
But my son, though he was weary, and with the struggle
worn,
Was anxious that some trophies should his distant home
adorn ;
So he stole forth past the sentries, and across the open
veldt
Went the brave lad armed hut lightly with a clasp-knife
in his belt 1
The light was of the dimmest—little wonder moon were
loth
To pour down her pure splendour upon those red scenes
of wrath.
The tainted wind was moaning in a dismal undertone,
As though the dead men’s ghosts were holding vigil there
alone.
But his stout heart was undaunted, as he picked his care-
ful way
Among the silent friends and foes who all distorted lay.
At length his quest was ended ; where the mountain
downward dipped
With strange disfigured upturn’d face, a bearded Dopper
slept.
Private Ghoul, he paused a moment. P’raps the Dopper
was not dead
(They have such a trick of shamming—Boers are liars bom
and bred!)
But when he stood beside him, scarce his rapture could he
curb—
For the whiskers were luxuriant, whilst the molars were
superb !
He was indeed a gruesome 'sight, all shattered, scarred
and torn ;
A human form of nearly all its human semblance shorn ;
One eye had met some dreadful bolt —the other, open
wide,
Seemed fixed in gaze beseeching upon him who stood
beside.
A foreigner may well have quailed before that frozen
stare,
And slunk away appall’d, the whilst he breathed some
heathen prayer ;
Nor, shudd’ring, dared to mutilate that mutilated head,
But he feared no Dopper living and he feared no Dopper
dead
My son was of the bull-dog breed and worth a score of
such!
His young hand never falter’d when with bold and sweep-
ing clutch
He grasped the beard and shore it from the broken face
beneath :
Then with a handy boulder pounded out two splendid
teeth !
And when the troops were landed, and he marched along
the quay,
The martial port of Private Ghoul was glorious to see.
He was burned quite brown and swarthy— why, his hands
were almost red !
You may see the war-spoils hanging just above his
mother’s bed.
The bid man’s gaze grew wistful as he looked across the
hall,
To where a glass frame shuddered on a text-strewn bed-
room wall;
And he grew a little husky—“ Yes, his mother’s darling
he
Was always, but he might have—brought an ear or so for
me.”
He wrong my hand in siience as we parted at the gate
Which bore the name of “ Ghoul ” upon a staring brassy
plate,
And in my dreams that night I knew the joy the old man
felt—
I dreamed that I was carrying teeth and beards across the
veldt. ■
I drfeamed that I was Ghoul, and round me toothless
Doppers lay,
I had shaved them and I’d shorn them, and I bore their
hair away.
. And sacks of teeth I’d plundered, and was coming back
for more,
And that I, too, was a hero—a hero of the War.
T. BRMM.

A PERFECT CURE
-• ASCRIBED TO AM
•l v
Act of Providence.
The Case of
Mrs; JAS. COPLAND.
{From the
'■ Newtown Chronicle. )
(pHte other day a “ Chronicle ” reporter WAs
tnaking his usual tour, seeking for news of
interest to our readers, when word reached
his ears of a most remarkable case. He
pursued further enquiries in the matter,
and the same day he proceeded to enquire
into the cure of Mrs. Copland, of Prospect-
Btreet* Erskineville, near Sydney ihis
lady received the man with the notebook
with unusual courtesy, asking him to be
seated, and volunteering to afford him al l the
information that he could possibly require.
He first of all said : f
“ I hear you were ill, and are cured.
“Well, rather,” replied Mrs. Copland,
“ I was most emphatically ill —ill for many
long and weary years with a complicated set
of complaints, which entirely defied the
efforts of the doctors, as well as the enor
mous quantities of pills and medicines which
I was induced to take. It is a great wonder
that I survived all this physicking. lam
sure it did me a lot of harm, and prevented
my food from digesting.”
“ Do you mean that you deprecate the
Use of all medicines ?”
“Oh, dear no ; but I mean to say that
you must use the right kind—if you don't
you will only suffer for it, and your illness
will be prolonged. I found the right kind
in the end, and one bottle absolutely cured
me from a most distressing list of maladies.”
“ Kindly relate your experience.
“ I first of all found that my vitality was
gradually waning. I lost my interest in
things. My work wearied me, and I felt
it was too much trouble to even walk up
the street. My usual cheerfulness of spirits
disappeared. I was dull and depressed, and
it often seemed to me that I was too tired
even to think. I took some strong tonics
and pick-me-ups, but they were all failures.
1 continued to get worse. Slowly my
digestive organs lost their power. Bilious
attacks came on at frequent intervals, and I
was seized with fits of sickness. I now never
experienced the sensation of real hunger;
indeed, it was a matter of utter indiffer-
ence to me whether I ate anything or not
As my complaint established itself I experi-
enced severe pain, especially in the breast
The sensation was exactly as though my
chest was full of lumps of wood. Again
pains in the back and sides began to assert
themselves, besides a heavy languorous ache
in the fore portion of my head. Often I had
to seek the quiet of my bedroom oppressed
with a terrible sickness and sick headache.
At these times I couldn’t bear anyone to come
near me, let alone to speak to me. I was
excessively irritable and peevish.”
“No wonder, Mrs. Copland.”
“And I could take nio pleasure in any-
thing. My nights were a torture to me. I
indeed went to bed, but not to sleep. Night
after night I never closed my eyes, and I
got out of bed languid, tired, and fairly
broken down. I lost greatly in flesh. My
eyes got dull looking, and my complexion
was very sallow and unhealthy. On and
off I suffered like this for years—a regular
burden to myself, and a cause of distress to
my friends. Meanwhile, I consulted doctors.
I took bott e after bottle of physic, and
boxes of pills too many to count. All were
ineffective, and I was forced unwillingly to
•the conclusion that I was doomed to be a
martyr to my complaints, and an invalid for
life. This was a very saddening reflection
for me, and I often wondered why all
ills should descend on a poor weak
Woman. My nervous system was not abl*
to stand the strain, and I got utterly broker
down.” .j
How unfortunate you were.
Yes, I was; and I don't know whan
Would have happened to me but for
something that, though it seemed at the
time a pure accident, I shall always look
upon as a veritable act of Providence.. 1
read in a newspaper about an astonishing
cure of a lady from a derangement of the
liver and other internal organs. On reading
further down the column, I found that not
only had she suffered from similar symptoms
to mine, but had been entirely cured by
Clements lYnic after the doctors had given
her up as lost. Of course, under these cir-
cumstances, it would have been sheer mad-
ness on my part not to have given rnyself a
chance, so I sent for a large bottle.”
“ What was the effect of Clements Tonic
in your case ? ”
“ Well, it would have to be felt to be
described. Imagine my astonishment when
1 found that before I had taken half a bottle
a marvellous change for the better had come
over me. The pains in my back, side, and
chest disappeared as though I had been
touched by the magic wand of a good fairy.
My spirits plucked up, and I felt toned and
strung up all over. From that time I began
to enjoy and digest my meals once more, and
to sleep the peaceful, refreshing sleep of a
healthy woman—and, mind you, 1 hadn’t
done this for years. That’s the astonishing
thing about it! None of the other things
I took were of the least use to me, yet here
I had run across a remedy, which in a few
days had put me on the high road to
recovery after years of illness. By the time
I had completed the first large bottle all
disease seemed eradicated. However, I
continued Clements Tonic as a general
restorative, to enable me to pick up my lost
nervous strength and flesh. It did this most
effectively, and made me as strong, and
vigorous, and bright as I could wish.”
“ Is there anything else that you would
like to say about Clements Tonic ? ”
“Yes, there is, and I think it not the
least wonderful portion of all that that
remedy has done for me.”
What is that, Mrs. Copland? ”
“ Why, I’ve been absolutely free from any
sign of my old complaint ever since Clements
Tonic drove it out —or, to use a somewhat
slangy expression,” said Mrs. Copland, with
a smile, “ I was cured at one go.”
“ May we publish this ? ”
“ The sooner the better, as I think such a,
miraculous recovery as mine should be made
public.”
DECLARATION BEFORE A MAGISTRATE.
1, Mrs. Jas. Copland, of Prospect-street,, Erskine
ville, Sydney, in the colony of New South Wales, dt
solemnly and sinceiely declare that I have carefull}
read the annexed document, consisting of five folio?
and consecutively numbered from one to five, and that
it contains and is a true and faithful account of my
illness and cure by Clements Tonic, and also contains
my full permission t 6 publish the same in any way ;
and I make this solemn declaration voluntarily and
without receiving any payment, conscientiously
believing the same to be true, and by \irtue of
the provisions of an Act made and passed in the
ninth year of the reign of her. present Majesty,
intituled “An Act for the more effectual abolition
of Oaths and Affirmations taken and made in the
various Departments of the Government of New
South Wales, and to substitute Declarations in
lieu thereof, and for the suppression of voluntary and
extra judicial Oaths and Affidavits.”
ei^r
Declared at Erskineville this 24th day of November,
1898, before me, ANDREW MURRAY, J.P.

DEPOT:—32 SHOW HILL, LOHOOH, EHS.
Australian Colonies-Of aU leading Wholesale Houses.
The deposit of tailings looks like becoming a
serious trouble at Hannan’s within the next year or two.
Those of the Ivanhoe, Horseshoe, Boulder and Lake View
are already spreading themselves over large areas ot the
co.’s leases, and layer after layer will be added until they
reach an inconvenient height. A large quantity wi go
below to fill in the stopes, but this will represent less than
40 per cent, of the total. A fair proportion of the balance
might be well used in filling up the shafts and workings
of “ cats ” on the camp, and thus wiping these latter out
of existence and into oblivion. The 1 ‘ cats ai eanej e
sore to the place ; many are distinguished by their mas-
sive poppet legs and miniature mullock-heaps^
Westralia has one paying copper - mine, the
Murrin-Murrin, in Mt. Malcolm district. Pegged out as a
gold-mine, the case with everything m the Golden
West.” One Taylor, a Montana man, came along and
thought it was good enough to work for copper , put up
a am til blast furnace, and is smelting with a combination
of charcoal and coke, the latter costing him £lO a ton.
Running through some 15 tons of ore a day and producing
nearly two tons of copper. Has several deposits of rich
oxides, carbonates, and sulphides, and is said to be pro-
ducing and shipping copper for a little more than h alf its
market-value. The worst that can be said of the Muirn -
Murrin is that it seems too rich to last, a remark not para-
doxical when applied to copper deposits. Rich gold
deposits seldom last, copper hardty ever. Quite possible,
however, that the Murrin-Murrin deposits will last long
enough for the owners to make a fortune them.
It isn’t safe to own shares in a mine the output
of which other people can mostly take away in their
pockets. Some of the big Westralian mines find that lots
of telluride is pocketed and carried off ; in fact, small
plants for treating telluride have been discovered in the
camp. So they mean to institute the changing-house
system. , *
White Feather Main Reef : Formally known as
Bissenberger’s, when it was a very poor surface show, has
developed so well with depth that it is now a long way
the best mine of the camp. Has only worked out two
levels; has the 300 intact, is going out from the 400 ;
has several ore bodies, one about 300 ft. long, and is alto-
gether working 700 ft. of ore. Reef averaging from 2ft.
6in.: value, 25dwt. A simple crushing and cyanide pro-
position, making less than 25 per cent, slime. Cyaniding
costs 3s. per ton and leaves only a dwt. in the tailings.
Paid a number of divs. ; more in sight, well-managed
and economically worked. It is a ref resiling thing to find
one mine at Kanowna, which is nearly dead in every
other respect. The thousands of alluvial diggers have
gone, some of those Remaining look as if they cannot get
away; more than half the shops are shut; nearly all the
“cats” are closed down; the pubs, remain open fora
little business, and an air of depression reigns every-
where. One mine can’t keep Kanowna alive.
* * * *
Hainault: —The value and prospects of this
mine during the last three or four years have changed
with kaleidoscopic rapidity. Easy to meet men who will
tell you of the momentary amazing richness of it at one
time or another. How they were induced to buy shares
because of a face that bristled with fre* gold and how the
very next week it had all vanished like a dream. Another
will tell you of an amazing telluride deposit, when a face
glistened as if plastered with silver. This, too, did the
disappearing trick in a night. The shares have been as
high as £7, they are now as low as 255. blot long ago a
crosscut came into a wonderful ore patch. It did not go far
up or down, nor to the right or left. Probably spent to
date £30,000 in finding less than 1000 tons of good ore.
Now following a pipe of rich stuff, but the will-o’-the-wisp
tricks already played give little hope. It is just outside
of that accursed break which is distinctly seen in the
Ivanhoe and although not very clearly defined in the
other mine it is there in some shape or other beyond
doubt. _ _

The move-on of the “rushers” to Cape Nome,
the new Alaskan beach goldfield, has begun ; H. P.
Pearse one of the pioneers of North Coolgardie, and
before’that of all Australia, Maoriland, and Papua, has
sailed that way.
* * * W
Henry Bratnober, the London mining engineer,
well-known in Australia, is looking at mines in Alaska,
which he describes as a great gold-field ; he writes of Cape
Nome that it is very rich and extensive. So far, it is all
alluvial; there is no quartz country that carries gold.
There is any quantity of quartz that is barren, and he
presumes that some day they will find gold in formation
similar to that in Westralia.
• # • *
A pretty little trouble is looming ahead on the
Alaskan coast. The Alaskan beaches in places contain
much gold, and a small army of miners are making a fair
living by treating the sands at low tide. A number of
American capitalists are building large suction dredges
which are to be anchored over the alluvial beaches at
high-tide and put into operation—to the ruin of the
miners. The latter are all armed, and say they will fight,
but the dredges are also to be armour-plated and supplied
with U.S. Govt, is despatching some thousands
of soldiers to the scene.
* • * •
That well-known old miner, Captain William
Dick, has been forced by rheumatic gout to throw up his
Kalgoorlie appointment and take another health-trip.
He will try the hot-air cure in Melbourne, and, failing re-
lief will go on to the M.L. Hot Lakes. He gives anything
but a cheering account of the Kalgoorlie fields, and is of
opinion that the sulphide question is far from settled—
the assertion of the chairman of the Associated notwith-
standing. Over 5000 men are workless at present ; a de-
cidedly blue look-out. # ,
A Bulletin man in Melb. met an old miner from
S’Africa, the other day. He was an Australian who knew
Queensland, N.S.W., Vic., Westralia, Maoriland and
Papuan fields, and who has spent a few years in Rhodesia,
trying it for all it was worth. His straight opinion is that
the country is no good for miners from these parts ; Aus-
iralia offers hundreds of bigger and brighter things for
the speculative digger. The discovered country that a
pair can work is rarely worth the working, and the on y
hope for Rhodesia is possible lodes at a great depth. Big
antique granite mortars, probably used for crushing
quartz in the dim past, are occasionally found a few feet
under the surface, and Rhodes is trying to give his wilder-
ness a golden reputation on the strength of them. Aus-
tralian miners are attracted by Rhodesia where the
Chartered Co. exacts 50 per cent, royalty !), but the ex-
perienced man who has been and seen reckons that 99 out
of every 100 who go will have the sornes very bad directly
after getting there. # » *

although there are lots of white men seeking work. The
co it appears, prefers to send to Victoria for white men
and to give all outside work to Japs.-Locusr, Horn

The Australian (German) Metal Co., running
the magnetic zinc-concentrating works at Broken Hill,
talk
of a complete reorganisation. ,
The ulant which the Australian Metal Co.—the
iu er l word SI or le twor o i r s kS ’the t principle 1 upon which
this and all the six or eight other electro-magnetic
processes are based :-Nearly all substances posses, some
magnetic properties, varying, however, in degree. Ma,ny
substances are of the same or nearly the same specific
gravity; when they are they cannot be separated by
flowing water. Also many substances (ores, for instance,
and irangue or waste) are so intimately associated that, in
order to separate the particles, the whole has to be
crushed so fine that it floats off as slime in water. Where,
however, the particles in either of these two cases possess
different magnetic properties it is possible by usin<* elec-
tro-magnets to make some sort of a separation. Some-
times the magnetic properties, always weak, are too much
alike to allow of effective work ; and when that happens
it is often possible to alter the constitution and character
of one of the elements by roasting or some such process.
So far, however, no preliminary roasting has been found
necessary in connection with Broken Hill zinc “ tailings.

Recently a S.A. Govt, party, headed by the
Minister of Mines, paid a visit of inspection to Tarcoola
goldfields. The journey back and forth occupied alto-
gether 15 days, but of that time only Four Hours were
spent on the mines, the rest being accounted for at
different stations and other stop ling-places en route.
Yet every member of the expedition considers that he
knows all about the prospects of the different claims.
* • • *
Gold-mining in S.A. must be in a pretty bad
way when the Bird-in-Hand, the best-known and hitherto
among the most productive of the dusty province’s shows,
appeals to Govt, for help to keep the thing going. And
this, too, during one of S.A.’s quarterly gold-booms.

of subjects for elegant conversation there are

“A.A.A. ” : An old black and dirty tub
of a schooner was lying at Bullock Island dyke
(Newcastle, N.S.W.), a while back. Went on
board to look around, and the first thing I beheld
on reaching the deck was a big, fat A.B. working
all he knew at a pump. “What 0!” I asked,
“does she leak as bad as that; in still water, too ?”
The seaman cast a careful look around before re-
plying. “Leakl (a couple of vigorous pumps.)
By h —, I’ve pumped Newcastle harbor through
her three times since we got here —that’s eight
days ago. Poes she leak ? ”
George William#, 54 years of age, the
other day committed suicide at Wyong, N.S.W.
by hanging himself from a rafter in an outhouse.
His father and brother suicided in like manner.
T’other day a lad was chopping wood in a
Ballarat backyard when he was flabbergasted by
the timber suddenly disappearing, followed by the
axe, down a hole 200 ft. deep. It is not so long
since the top of an old shaft caved in in a Ballarat
main street. One of these nights a prominent
citizen will disappear hastily, to bump loudly on
the roof of Hades after descending a few hundred
feet. Ballarat ought to be careful where it puts
its statues; the possibility of one of them drop-
ping suddenly into the far interior of this globe is
too awful to contemplate.
The Mockers.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The woman seated in the doorway was a study
in feline immobility; yet her half-closed eyes saw
each living thing that moved in the street. The
urchin toiling in the rain-flushed gutter before her
became an object of ferocious interest. She knew
that the piling of rotting garbage in the floodway
meant the swamping of her house ; and he was
laboring with impish stealth to attain that end.
The remarkable hatred existing between the
boy and the woman was a thing that passed under-
standing in the street. It was known that he
had been the playmate of her dead son ; it was
since the funeral that hostilities had begun.
Suddenly the boy found that his dam was pro-
ducing some effect—the water swirled over the
pavement and touched the woman s feet. She did
not move. Then the boy doubled forward and
laughed—laughed in jerks with his hands grip
ping his knees. .
The slow venom in his voice brought her into a
sitting posture. Her mouth twitched as though
something had cut it. She began to sing, and the
boy ceased capering in the filth. The words o.
her song caricatured his ill-shaped head, hn
squinting eyes, his dubious parentage. She was-
Irish, and had discovered long ago that it wa:
easier to wound in rhyme than prose. Her fee
beat time in the slush. She clasped her hand
fervently and jeered. . , .
The boy stood, white and palpitating in in
childish fury. Then suddenly he moved away,
affecting a grotesque limp that only permitted
him to pass her with painful slowness. She
stopped singing ; something in his pretended limp
struck her into a fit of blind coughing that left
her grovelling in the doorway.
“ Them two are at it again,” said a man oppo-
site. “The kid beats her, though. Y’see, her
Jimmy wot pegged out uster limp like that; an
this kid’s imitatin’ it— her think a bit,
y’see. By cripes, look at her !
The boy went limping up the street.

Household duties should not be a drudgery—
they are not when women are strong and well.
But where backs are aching and heads throbbing
as though they would break, even the joys of life
are turned to drudgery.
Do you know what makes the backaches, and
headaches, and the all-tired-out feeling ?
Sick kidneys. They are the filters of the blood,
and cannot stand all the work put upon them.
Backache is the cry of the kidneys for help—and
it is a warning of more serious trouble to follow.
Unless this backache is taken in time, it will run
into urinary troubles, Diabetes, Bright’s Disease,
and—Death.
Women do not need to suffer—there is a remedy
for backaches and for all other symptoms of kid-
ney disease —Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills.
They should be used to stop the progress of any
and all disorders of the kidneys and urinary
organs, and may be thoroughly depended on.
When your kidneys are not doing their work it
will be manifested by pain or dull ache in the
back, rheumatic pains, sciatica, sediment in the
urine, scanty supply, scalding irritation in passing
it, obliged to go often during the day and to get
up during the night, uric acid, sleeplessness, ner-
vousness, irritability, sallow, unhealthy complexion,
puffy or dark circles under the eyes, loss of energy
and ambition.
If your water, when allowed to remain undis-
turbed for 24 hours, forms a sediment or settling,
or has a cloudy appearance, it is evidence
that your kidneys and bladder need immediate
attention.
If you have any of the above symptoms, Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills will set you right and cure
you. We will gladly send you the names of thou-
sands who have testified to what Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills have done for them.

Bishop.

Rivals.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sir Valour, with his buckskin glove,
His simple forehead smote ;
Quoth he : “To please my Lady Love
I go to lands remote !
“ I go to lands remote, to fight
A race I never knew.
No matter; God defend the Right!
My Lady Fair, Adieu! ”
His Lady Love, in ancient mode,
Gave him a favor white ;
And sighed, as down the street he rode,
“ Adieu, my own true knight! ”
Sir Comfort in his arm-chair sat;
His look was keen and sly ;
He seemed a sleek, old fireside cat,
With half-shut mousing eye.
And, as he gazed into the fire
He chuckled quietly:
“ The only woman I desire—
She makes a mock of me.
“ She says her heart is with her knight,
Her Love, her Hero grand—
A large swashbuckler gone to fight—
And fast—in Boerland.
“ And ever, as the days go by,
She reads the cables grim ;
And, pretty idiot, wonders wb y
They do not mention him !
“ He wrote, she says, to tell her how
He slashed around, and made
A brilliant capture of a cow,
While on a scoutirig-raid.
“ It’s odd, these fellows sent to fight
Should think—they make me sore—
That they must Commentaries write,
Like Caesar, on the war.
“ But deeds of valor on the veldt,
And courage in the kloof,
Though worthy of a champion-belt,
Produce but little oof.
“ Maidens there are who value more
Love, Fame, and manly looks
Than stacks of d ress and gold galore—
They live in story-books.
“And real maidens, too, will dare
For love the coldest storm —
If they have padded gowns to wear,
And furs to keep them warm.
“ And they will faded roses keep,
Wherewith they once were wooed,
And over them will softly weep—
When they are in the mood.
“ But, when they meet with other girls,
To roses they prefer
Some simple rubies, strings of pearls,
And diamonds for their hair.
“ Their dreams, in hours of fond desire,
Across the seas may roam ;
But they are cats that love the fire,
And keep their hearts at Home.
“ Sweet Isabel is like the rest,
As fickle and as vain ;
And young Romance is now a guest
Where Fact and I shall reign.”
O, he was old and worldly-wise,
And in experience deep ;
And, closing then his crafty eyes,
Sir Comfort fell asleep.
Alas, for Love that come 3 to flee,
And Faith that cannot stay !
Alas, for Woman’s constancy,
And Lovers far away !
Sir Comfort married Isabel.
It was a wedding fine ;
The world that day went very well;
The sun did gaily shine.
She was in whitest silk arrayed—
A vision glorified—
And all the papers said she made
“A very charming bride.”
Sir Valour died upon the veldt,
And, so his comrades tell,
They heard, as by his side they knelt,
But one word—“ Isabel! ”
Sir Comfort, rich and old and sly,
Holds vows of woman cheap—
I’d rather like Sir Valour die,
And my illusions keep.
CREEVE ROR.
The Bet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Things were not going well w ith the Rev. Theo-
philus Broadbent. The Rev. Theophilus was a
Nonconformist parson, with a small wooden
church and a smaller, more wooden congregation.
The Nonconformist parson was a tremendous
stickler for conformity: he conformed with innu-
merable laws, commands, customs, and traditions,
little and big. His life was a perpetual round of
conformities, and he expected it to be the same
with his congregation. But his congregation sul-
lenly rebelled and expressed its disapprobation
by icily ignoring the plate. Consequently the
Rev. Theo. Broadbent was a lean, shabby, and
frequently a very hungry parson.
Had parson Broadbent been something more of
a bad man, he might have been a great one ; but,
lacking a certain capacity for evil, he was a failure.
He had a conscientiousness that nobody could live
with. I may be expected to except Mrs. Theo.,
but their marriage was so complete a success that
the pale, small wife had been quite assimilated by
the Rev. Broadbent,*and, to all moral intents and
purposes, had been non-existent for some thirteen
years. She could not be said to have a soul to
call her own, and her intense admiration for her
husband was only an aggravated egotism.
The Rev. Theophilus was strongest, most
vehement and most insistent in his condemnation
of gambling, and this was a theme to which he
almost entirely devoted himself during the run of
the great racing event of the year, and for some
weeks before and after. His congregation
affected all the seasonable sins, and withheld
contributions more determinedly than ever, with
a view to buying tickets in Tatt.’s Sweep. The
Plate, in a word, suffered for the Cup, and an
empty plate is a poor thing to dine from.
The Rev. Broadbent carried his principles into
his home. He was always a preacher ; he could
not pass the mustard without making a sermon
about it. He was one of those men who find ser-
mons in stones and good in everything—terrible
men to live with, for they will insist on imparting
their discoveries. The congregation could escape
from the clergyman’s preachments—the family
could not. Little Paul Broadbent, aged 12, and
little Mary Broadbent, aged 10, and four other
little Broadbents, were brought up in the odour of
sanctity. The little Broadbents had no compre-
hension of a more hideous villainy than gambling.
A playing-card Theophilus called a Free-Ticket
to the Pit; dice were the Corner-stones of Hell.
After the great racing event in 1895 the wooden
congregation in the little wooden church was more
reserved than ever before in the presence of the
plate. It had mostly backed the wrong horse and
was soured as well as impoverished.
One evening Elder Dourer, a truculent but
devoted apostle, in a fit of ungodly wrath, carried
a tiny toy plate, taken from, a child’s tea-set, from
pew to pew, but the wooden congregation was
impervious to his fine irony, and the Rev. Theo-
pliilus admonished him severely and at great
length, after the service, taking his text from
Ezekiel, chap. 3, verse 3.
This was the worst time Parson Broadbent had
experienced since he first took up the interminable
and crushing burden of the Church. He was
behind with his rent. To be shabby always and
hungry now and then were phases of his martyr-
dom, and he had the pride in them a soldier has in
his wounds ; but to owe rent he thought was not
respectable. To crown his troubles, the boy Paul
was apparently developing a serious sickness.
Paul was a tall, strapping lad for his years, a
cadet, and at 12 already imbued with a passion for
militarism.
Paul suddenly lost his exuberant animalism and
his vigorous appetite. He became pale and
heavy-eyed, and moped at home. After the
inefficacy of domestic remedies had.been proved,
a cheap doctor was called in, and his conclusion,
after investigating, was that Paul had something
on his mind.
The Rev. Theophilus Broadbent considered this
at great length, and prepared an improving dis-
course for Paul’s especial benefit, basing his
remarks on Proverbs, chap. 1, sth verse. The boy
collapsed under it, and wept miserably, but all
efforts to induce him to unburden his mind were
vain. Paul neither admitted nor denied, he only
sobbed and moaned desolately, and after that
became worse than ever. Mrs. Theo. was dis-
tracted.
As time went on Paul grew worse. He secreted
himself in out-of-the-way places; he hated to be
under his father’s eye, and ate scarcely anything,
sitting at the scanty meal with the downcast, fur-
tive looks of a convicted criminal. Theophilus
was profoundly disturbed. It occurred to him
that perhaps the boy had discovered the true state
of the family finances and prospects, and was
allowing the dismal truth to prey upon his mind.
In the course of ten days Broadbent’s unhappy
son had worked himself into something like a
fever. The doctor gave medicines, but insisted
upon the accuracy of his first diagnosis— the
patient must make open confession. On the night
of the tenth day, after the rest of the family had
fone to bed. the Rev. Broadbent tried to talk to
‘aul like a loving father. He only succeeded in
making a sermon, and the boy was dumb. Then
Mrs. Theo. broke with rule and precedent and
took Paul into her own hands, and talked to him
like a mother, with profuse tears and little femi-
nine appeals to the protective instinct that is the
pride of the male animal.
The mother’s appeal broke her small son up ;
he became almost hysterical, but presently fled
from the room, unconfessed and unshriven, and
for an hour the father and mother sat silent in an
atmosphere of grief that defied the parson’s obsti-
nate religious optimism. So occupied were they
with their trouble that they did not notice Paul,
who had entered the room again, and stood just
within the door, clad in his white nightgown, and
holding the garment gathered in front as if
carrying something heavy in its folds. A sob from
the lad attracted the parents’ attention, and each
uttered a cry of alarm at the sight of him. He
was ghastly pale and hollow-eyed, and the ex-
pression of his drawn face was the more shocking
for the mystery of it.
The boy stiffened himself and walked across the
room, gazing on his father as the condemned man
at the gallows gazes upon his executioner. He
stopped again and let his nightgown fall, and from
it streamed a shower of sovereigns ; a heap lay at
his feet, others ran hither and thither about the
room, eddying in the corners. Then Paul threw
himself face downwards on the hearthrug. When
he spoke his voice was clear and'high.
“ I won it! ”he said. “ I’m a gambler ! The
officer gave five shillings for the best shot in our
regiment. I was the best shot. Other boys were
betting on the horses; they were always talking
about it, and I put my five shillings on a double,
and I won/”
There was a pause of nearly a minute, and Paul
centinued.
“I wouldn’t have minded if I hadn’t won. I
hid the money away. I was afraid of it. It was
horrible, all that money. I couldn’t bear it !
I—l—l ”
Paul broke down, and lay cowering, waiting the
catastrophe, and the Rev. Theophilus Broadbei.t
gazed upon him, speechless for the first time
in his life. Twice he opened his mouth and said
something in an unnatural voice about the epistle
to the Colossians, first chapter, verse sixteen, and
each time his utterance failed him. His eyes
drifted from his son to the minted gold on the
floor.
And then, with a strange unearthly cry, he seized
a handful of the money, fled out into the dark-
ness and paid the rent; and as he ran he felt that
his cherished principles were dropping off him like
grasshoppers—and that he was Lost !
EDWARD DYSON.
